Divine Decree
beginner
5 min
The Second Position Defined: Distinct Powers for Obedience and Disobedience
Maturidi states the second position and defines its key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second position held by al-Husayn and others that the power for obedience is distinct from the power for disobedience, and how each power is defined as tawfiq and 'isma for obedience, and khidhlan and tark for disobedience.

Core Idea
The power for obedience and disobedience are two distinct divine gifts, not a single human capacity.

Imam al-Maturidi now presents the second of three views on the nature of human power. This view, attributed to al-Husayn and others, posits that the power enabling obedience is ontologically different from the power leading to disobedience. It defines these powers in theological terms: tawfiq and 'isma for obedience, and khidhlan and tark for disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms: قوة الطاعة, قوة المعصية, التوفيق, العصمة, الخذلان, الترك.
Notice the structure: statement then definition.
2
Identify the definitions
Identify what the power for obedience is (tawfiq and 'isma) and what the power for disobedience is (khidhlan and tark). Understand that 'tark' is 'letting be' according to choice.
The phrase 'على ما يختار' indicates human choice plays a role.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself why these terms are used and what it means for power to be distinct. This sets the foundation for the proofs that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال جماعة منهم : قوة الطاعة هي غير قوة المعصية ، منهم الحسين وغيره يذهبون إلى أن قوة الطاعة التوفيق والعصمة ، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يختار ؛ ودليل ذلك وجود سؤال المعونة والعصمة ، على الإحاطة أن ليس معهما زيغ ، والتوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة ، وكذلك القول الظاهر باللهم قونى على طاعتك وأعنى عليها ، وبتعوذ من الخذلان والإزاغة ، ثبت لو كان يكون بكل واحد منها ما يكون بالآخر لم يكن الذى يسأل بالسؤال أحق من الذى يتعوذ منه ، ولو كان يكون بالعصمة زيغ لم يكن يطمئن القلب عند الوجود ، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر ، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد . وأيضا أنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه مرفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر ، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن ، ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان والآخر الخذلان . وأيضا أن القوة إذ هي لا تبقى وقتين ليصلح بها الفعلان ، ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد ، ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما ، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اختيار كالنار في التحريق والثلج في التبريد ، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار ، وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان ، والعدوان مع الكفران ، سببها مختلف على اختلافها ، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And a group of them said: The power for obedience is other than the power for disobedience. Among them is al-Husayn and others who hold that the power for obedience is tawfiq (divine facilitation) and 'isma (infallibility), and the power for disobedience is khidhlan (abandonment) and tark (letting be) according to what one chooses. The proof of that is the existence of asking for assistance and protection, with the encompassing [knowledge] that there is no deviation with them, and tawfiq with the encompassing [knowledge] that with it is correctness, and likewise the apparent saying: 'O Allah, strengthen me for Your obedience and help me therein,' and seeking refuge from khidhlan and misguidance. It is established that if what occurs by each one of them were what occurs by the other, then the one who asks by asking would not be more entitled than the one who seeks refuge from it. And if there were deviation with 'isma, the heart would not be at peace when it [the act] exists. So it is established that the power of each type is other than the power of the other type. And it is established by what is asked of 'isma and tawfiq, just as assistance and strengthening are asked, that they are in reality one. Also, no one says about the disbeliever that he is facilitated to faith and protected from disbelief, nor does anyone prevent that from the believer. So it is established that the meaning of that is assistance to faith and the other is khidhlan. Also, since power does not last for two moments so that both acts could be performed by it, and there is no way to gather the two opposite acts in one moment, it is established that it is a power for one of them, not for both, and that that which is for both would remain for their possibility, and there is no power except by Allah. Also, power cannot exist except with choice, like fire for burning and snow for cooling—it occurs by that which has a nature necessarily. And that is like friendship with faith and enmity with disbelief—their causes differ according to their differences. So the matter of power over the two things is similar, and there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 862

Commentary
This passage presents the second position held by al-Husayn and others: the power for obedience is different from the power for disobedience. They define the power for obedience as tawfiq (divine facilitation) and 'isma (infallibility), and the power for disobedience as khidhlan (divine abandonment) and tark (letting be) according to the individual's choice. The author provides several proofs. First, the very fact that people ask Allah for assistance (mu'awna) and protection ('isma) shows that these are distinct from the opposite; if they were not distinct, asking would be meaningless. The same applies to seeking refuge from khidhlan and misguidance. Second, no one would say about a disbeliever that he is facilitated to faith or protected from disbelief, nor would anyone deny that for a believer. This shows that the meaning of assistance is for faith and the other is khidhlan. Third, power does not persist for two moments to enable two acts, and opposite acts cannot occur simultaneously, so the power must be for one act alone, not both. Fourth, power exists with choice, like natural forces: fire burns and snow cools necessarily. Similarly, faith is associated with friendship (wilaya) and disbelief with enmity ('udwan), each having its own cause. Thus, the power for opposites is not a single power. The passage ends with the refrain 'there is no power except by Allah'.

Key Terms
توفيق
عصمة
خذلان
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this view negates human free will entirely.
Correction: The text explicitly states 'according to what one chooses', showing that human choice is involved; the power is divine facilitation or abandonment based on that choice.

Checkpoint
According to the second position, what is the power for obedience?
A) Free will
B) Tawfiq and 'isma
C) Khidhlan and tark
D) Natural ability

Key Takeaway
The second view defines the power for obedience as tawfiq and 'isma, and the power for disobedience as khidhlan and tark, making them ontologically distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in obeying Allah, do you attribute that to your own strength or to Allah's tawfiq and 'isma? How does this lesson confront your pride in your good deeds?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by saying 'Allah wrote it for me'? This lesson says the power for disobedience (khidhlan and tark) comes only after you choose it. What is one sin you have been attributing to divine decree that you must now own as your own choice?
?
If the power for obedience is a distinct gift from Allah, how should that change your preparation before every act of worship? What specific du'a or mindset will you adopt before your next prayer?

Divine Decree
intermediate
6 min
Proof from Supplication: Distinct Powers
Maturidi provides proof from supplications for assistance and protection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the very act of asking Allah for assistance and protection demonstrates that the powers for obedience and disobedience are distinct.

Core Idea
Our supplications for guidance and refuge prove that the power for each act comes from a different divine source.

Imam al-Maturidi now presents a proof grounded in everyday worship: the fact that believers ask Allah for tawfiq and 'isma while seeking refuge from khidhlan shows that these are different realities. If they were the same, such supplications would be meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'ودليل ذلك وجود سؤال المعونة والعصمة' and continues up to 'فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر'.
Note the key phrases: سؤال المعونة, التعوذ من الخذلان.
2
Unpack the logic
The proof argues that if the same power could produce both obedience and disobedience, then asking for one and seeking refuge from the other would be equivalent. Since they are not, the powers must be distinct.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: لو كان يكون بكل واحد منها ما يكون بالآخر لم يكن الذي يسأل...
3
Reflect on the heart's peace
The proof also notes that if 'isma could involve deviation, the heart would not be at peace. This reinforces that 'isma is a distinct, pure power.
The phrase 'يطمئن القلب عند الوجود' relates to spiritual certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال جماعة منهم : قوة الطاعة هي غير قوة المعصية ، منهم الحسين وغيره يذهبون إلى أن قوة الطاعة التوفيق والعصمة ، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يختار ؛ ودليل ذلك وجود سؤال المعونة والعصمة ، على الإحاطة أن ليس معهما زيغ ، والتوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة ، وكذلك القول الظاهر باللهم قونى على طاعتك وأعنى عليها ، وبتعوذ من الخذلان والإزاغة ، ثبت لو كان يكون بكل واحد منها ما يكون بالآخر لم يكن الذى يسأل بالسؤال أحق من الذى يتعوذ منه ، ولو كان يكون بالعصمة زيغ لم يكن يطمئن القلب عند الوجود ، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر ، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد . وأيضا أنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه مرفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر ، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن ، ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان والآخر الخذلان . وأيضا أن القوة إذ هي لا تبقى وقتين ليصلح بها الفعلان ، ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد ، ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما ، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اختيار كالنار في التحريق والثلج في التبريد ، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار ، وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان ، والعدوان مع الكفران ، سببها مختلف على اختلافها ، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .

Among them is al-Husayn and others who hold that the power for obedience is tawfiq (divine facilitation) and 'isma (infallibility), and the power for disobedience is khidhlan (abandonment) and tark (letting be) according to what one chooses. It is established that if what occurs by each one of them were what occurs by the other, then the one who asks by asking would not be more entitled than the one who seeks refuge from it. And if there were deviation with 'isma, the heart would not be at peace when it [the act] exists. So it is established that the power of each type is other than the power of the other type. And it is established by what is asked of 'isma and tawfiq, just as assistance and strengthening are asked, that they are in reality one. Checkpoint
What does the proof from supplication primarily establish?
A) That Allah answers all prayers
B) That the power for obedience and disobedience are distinct
C) That human free will is absolute
D) That tawfiq and 'isma are the same

Key Takeaway
The act of supplication for tawfiq and 'isma versus seeking refuge from khidhlan demonstrates that the powers behind obedience and disobedience are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for tawfiq and 'isma in your daily life, do you truly believe that the power to obey comes solely from Him, or do you still rely on your own strength? How does this lesson expose hidden self-reliance in your heart?
?
Have you ever felt a deep unease when seeking refuge from khidhlan? What does that unrest reveal about your trust in Allah's distinct power to protect you? Commit today to making such supplications with full confidence that He alone grants purity.
?
If the power for obedience and disobedience were the same in your own actions, your prayers for guidance and seeking refuge from misguidance would be meaningless. Does your actual supplication practice reflect this truth, or have you been treating these prayers as mere rituals? What will you change starting today?

Additional Proofs: Logical and Natural Arguments
Maturidi presents three additional proofs for the distinctness of powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional proofs: from the impossibility of attributing facilitation to a disbeliever, from the temporal limitation of power, and from the analogy of natural forces.

Core Idea
Opposite acts cannot share a single power, just as fire and snow have distinct properties.

Imam al-Maturidi provides three more arguments to reinforce the distinctness of powers. First, no one says a disbeliever is facilitated to faith. Second, a single power cannot enable contradictory acts in the same moment. Third, natural forces and moral states each have their own specific causes. These proofs strengthen the case that the power for obedience is different from that for disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic text from 'وأيضا أنه لا أحد يطلق القول' to 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Identify each proof as separate: (1) about the disbeliever, (2) about power not lasting two moments, (3) about natural forces.
Notice that each proof begins with 'وأيضا'.
2
Analyze the first proof
The first proof: no one says a disbeliever is facilitated to faith or protected from disbelief. This shows that assistance (for faith) and abandonment (for disbelief) are distinct.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof: power does not last for two moments to perform two acts, and opposite acts cannot coexist. Therefore, the power must be for one act only, not both.
This is a logical argument about the nature of power.
4
Analyze the third proof
The third proof: power exists with choice, like natural forces (fire burns, snow cools). Similarly, faith is associated with friendship (wilaya) and disbelief with enmity ('udwan), each with its own cause. Thus, power over opposites must differ.
The analogy strengthens the argument from nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال جماعة منهم : قوة الطاعة هي غير قوة المعصية ، منهم الحسين وغيره يذهبون إلى أن قوة الطاعة التوفيق والعصمة ، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يختار ؛ ودليل ذلك وجود سؤال المعونة والعصمة ، على الإحاطة أن ليس معهما زيغ ، والتوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة ، وكذلك القول الظاهر باللهم قونى على طاعتك وأعنى عليها ، وبتعوذ من الخذلان والإزاغة ، ثبت لو كان يكون بكل واحد منها ما يكون بالآخر لم يكن الذى يسأل بالسؤال أحق من الذى يتعوذ منه ، ولو كان يكون بالعصمة زيغ لم يكن يطمئن القلب عند الوجود ، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر ، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد . وأيضا أنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه مرفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر ، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن ، ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان والآخر الخذلان . وأيضا أن القوة إذ هي لا تبقى وقتين ليصلح بها الفعلان ، ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد ، ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما ، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اختيار كالنار في التحريق والثلج في التبريد ، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار ، وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان ، والعدوان مع الكفران ، سببها مختلف على اختلافها ، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the second proof (about power not lasting two moments)?
A) Power can be used for both obedience and disobedience
B) Power is for one act only, not both
C) Power is eternal and unchanging
D) Power is created by human effort

Key Takeaway
Three additional proofs—from common sense, logical necessity, and natural analogy—solidify that the power for obedience is distinct from the power for disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly feel that without Allah's assistance (tawfiq) you cannot even turn to Him, and without His abandonment (khidhlan) you would not fall into sin? How often do you recite 'there is no power except by Allah' with this awareness in your heart?
?
When you look back at a good deed you performed, do you secretly credit your own strength? Based on this lesson, what would it mean to attribute that act entirely to Allah's grant of a specific power?
?
Imagine the moment you are about to commit a sin: Do you recognize that the power to resist or commit is not your own to direct, but that Allah is the one who bestows or withholds it? How will you change your du'a in that moment?

Divine Decree
intermediate
6 min
Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Power and Compulsion
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila on power and compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's denial of power's persistence leads to compulsion, and how Maturidi refutes them by showing their own logic leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Denying power at the act's time makes the agent compelled, not free.

Maturidi begins by noting that the Mu'tazila's view sounds pleasant but is flawed upon analysis. Their claim that power does not last for two moments and is absent at the time of the act forces the conclusion that acts occur by necessity, not choice. This passage exposes the internal contradiction in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi first acknowledges the surface appeal, then states the Mu'tazila's core claim, then provides evidence, and finally gives an illustrative example.
Pay attention to the terms 'قدرة' (power), 'اضطرار' (necessity), and 'طبع' (nature).
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premises and Maturidi's inference
The Mu'tazila say power does not last for two moments and is absent at the time of the act. Maturidi infers that if so, the act occurs by necessity (ilm al-idhtirar) and nature, not by choice. Then he provides evidence: loss of all causes prevents the act and makes the agent compelled. Loss of power is even more deserving of that description.
Maturidi uses the concept of 'علم الاضطرار' (knowledge of necessity) to link the absence of power to compulsion.
3
Reflect on the consequence: friendship/enmity become irrational
If the agent is compelled, then becoming a friend or enemy of Allah is not based on free choice. Maturidi uses their own example: moving at a second moment occurs inevitably unless prevented externally—a sign of necessity. Yet they still ascribe moral categories like friendship and enmity, which is intellectually monstrous.
This highlights the inconsistency: they deny free power but still maintain moral accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر طرفا مما يبين قبح قول المعتزلة عند التحصيل ، وإن كان قولهم في الإطلاق قولا لذيذا في السمع يشبه أن يكون حقا ، والله الموفق وهو من أن قولهم أن القدرة لا تبقى وقتين ، وأنها ليست وقت الفعل ، فوقع الفعل في الحقيقة ولا قوة له وقت وجوده ، أو ذلك علم الاضطرار ووقوع الفعل بالطبع ؛ ثم الدلالة أن حق مثله الاضطرار أن فقد جميع الأسباب التي بها الفعل لوقته يحيل الفعل ويوصف صاحبه بالاضطرار ، ففوت القدرة التي لها الفعل أحق بذلك ، فصيروه مضطرا إلى ما يصير به وليا لله تعالى ، وعدوا له عند الاختيار . ومما يوضح ذلك أيضا أن من قولهم : إن من أراد التحرك للوقت الثاني منه أنها تقع لا محالة ، ولا يقدر صرفها إلا بمنع من قبل غيره ، وذلك آية الضرورة ، ثم وجبت الولاية والعداوة بمثله ، وذلك وحش في العقل.
Then we mention a part that clarifies the ugliness of the Mu'tazila's statement upon attainment, although their statement in absolute terms is a pleasant saying to the ear that seems to be true, and Allah is the granter of success. And it is that their statement that power does not last for two moments, and that it is not at the time of the act, so the act occurs in reality while there is no power at the time of its existence—or that is knowledge of necessity (ilm al-idhtirar) and the occurrence of the act by nature. Then the evidence is that the like of that is necessity (idhtirar): that the loss of all the causes by which the act occurs at its time prevents the act and its possessor is described as compelled (mudtarr). So the loss of power by which the act occurs is more deserving of that. So they have made him compelled into that by which he becomes a friend of Allah Most High or an enemy of Him at the time of choice. And what clarifies that as well is that from their statements: that whoever intends to move for the second moment from him, it occurs inevitably, and he cannot turn it away except by prevention from another—that is a sign of necessity. Then friendship and enmity become obligatory by like of that, and that is monstrous in the intellect.
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine regarding power (qudra). He acknowledges that their statement initially sounds pleasant and seemingly true, but upon examination it is ugly. The core of their error is their claim that power does not persist for two moments and is not present at the time of the act. If so, then the act occurs when there is no power, which means it occurs by science of necessity (ilm al-idhtirar) and by nature (tab'), not by choice. The evidence is that if all causes for an act at its time are absent, the act is prevented and the agent is considered compelled (mudtarr). Since they deny the presence of power at the moment of the act, they have effectively made the agent compelled into becoming a friend or enemy of Allah, which contradicts the notion of choice. He further clarifies using their own example: they say that if someone intends to move at a second moment, the movement occurs inevitably and can only be prevented by an external obstacle—this is a sign of necessity (idhtirar), not free choice. Yet they still ascribe friendship and enmity based on such acts, which is intellectually repugnant.

اضطرار (necessity)
طبع (nature)
مضطر (compelled)
الولاية (friendship)
العداوة (enmity)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila view that power does not persist is obviously false and has no appeal.
Correction: Maturidi first acknowledges that their statement 'is pleasant to the ear and seems to be true' to show that it is not self-evidently false; the error becomes clear only upon analysis.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows from the Mu'tazila claim that power does not last for two moments and is absent at the time of the act?
A) The act occurs by free will.
B) The act occurs by necessity and nature.
C) Power must be present at the time of the act.
D) The act is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that denying power's persistence logically entails compulsion, which makes moral categories like friendship and enmity meaningless.

If the Mu'tazila deny power at the moment of the act, they make you like a machine – but you know your choices feel real. Does this passage challenge you to own your actions fully, even when you feel weak?
?
When you love Allah or fear Him, do you feel that love and fear are your own genuine responses, or do you sometimes feel they are forced upon you? Maturidi says that if power is absent, love and enmity become meaningless. How can you cultivate a love for Allah that is truly your own free choice?
?

Where does your sense of personal accountability come from – is it just a feeling, or does it reflect real freedom? How will you live differently today knowing that your choices truly matter?

Two Proofs from Human Experience
Maturidi presents two proofs from human psychology and supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first two proofs that the power for each type of act is distinct, based on the heart's ease and the nature of supplication.

Core Idea
Distinct acts require distinct powers, as shown by our reliance on Allah for each.

Maturidi begins his argument by drawing on two common human experiences: seeking refuge from evil and asking for infallibility. He observes that if infallibility could deviate, the heart would not find peace. He also notes that we ask for infallibility and success just as we ask for assistance, indicating they are one in reality. These observations lead to the conclusion that the power enabling belief is distinct from that enabling disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument.
2
Identify the first proof
Look for the part about seeking refuge and the heart's ease. Understand what it proves.
The proof relies on the psychological certainty of infallibility.
3
Identify the second proof
Find the part about supplication. Note the parallel between asking for infallibility/success and assistance/strengthening.
This shows they are identical in reality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After understanding both proofs, state in your own words why power must be distinct for each type.
Both proofs point to the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتعوذ منه ، ولو كان يكون بالعصمة زيغ لم يكن يطمئن القلب عند الوجود ، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر ، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما فى الحقيقة واحد . وأيضا أنه لا أحد يطلق القول فى الكافر أنه مرفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر ، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن ، ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان والآخر الخذلان . وأيضا أن القوة إذ هى لا تبقى وقتين ليصلح بها الفعلان ، ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد ، ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما ، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
وأيضا أن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اختيار كالنار في التحريق والثلج فى التبريد ، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار ، وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان ، والعدوان مع الكفران ، سببها مختلف على اختلافها ، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
He seeks refuge from it, and if there were in infallibility deviation, the heart would not be at ease at existence. So it is established that the power of each type of that is different from the power of the other type. And it is established, by the fact that one asks for infallibility and success just as one asks for assistance and strengthening, that they are in reality one. And also that no one says of the disbeliever that he is aided towards faith and infallible from disbelief, and no one refrains from it in the believer; it is established that the meaning of that is assistance towards faith, and the other is abandonment. And also that power, since it does not remain for two moments such that two acts could be performed by it, and there is no way to combine two contradictory acts in one moment, it is established that it is power for one of them, not for both, and that what is for both would remain to accommodate them, and there is no power except by Allah. And also that power cannot exist except with choice, like fire in burning and snow in cooling; it occurs by it that which has a nature necessarily. And that is like closeness with faith, and enmity with disbelief; their cause is different according to their differences. So the matter of power over the two matters is similar, and there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
This passage presents several proofs that the power for different types of acts — such as belief and disbelief, obedience and disobedience — are distinct and not one and the same. The author begins by noting that if infallibility were subject to deviation, the heart would not be at ease; this leads to the conclusion that the power for each type is different. Then he argues that we ask for infallibility and success (tawfīq) just as we ask for assistance and strengthening, showing that these are one and the same. Next, he observes that no one says the disbeliever is aided towards faith or infallible from disbelief, and no one refrains from that in the believer, establishing that the meaning of assistance is for faith and the other is abandonment (khidhlān). The third proof is that power does not remain for two moments, so it cannot enable two contradictory acts; it must be power for one of them, not both. Finally, he uses an analogy from nature: fire burns by necessity, snow cools by necessity; each operates by its nature, and likewise the cause for closeness (wilāya) with faith and enmity (ʿidwān) with disbelief differ. The same applies to power over two matters: it is specific to one. All power is from Allah.

Key Terms
عصمة
توفيق
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One power could enable both belief and disbelief.
Correction: Maturidi argues through these proofs that power is specific to each type of act; no single power can enable contradictory acts.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that one asks for infallibility and success just as one asks for assistance?
A) That they are different.
B) That they are in reality one.
C) That they are unrelated.
D) That only infallibility is asked.

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs show that our psychological and supplicatory experiences point to distinct powers for each type of act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek refuge from sin, do you truly feel your heart at ease, or are you relying on your own strength? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's power versus your own?
?
You ask Allah for both protection from sin and strength to obey. Do you see these as two separate requests or one unified plea for divine assistance? How should this understanding change the way you make duʿāʾ?
?
If a single power could lead to both sin and righteousness, your choices would be meaningless. In light of this passage, how does the distinct power for each act compel you to take personal responsibility for your faith?

Three Further Proofs from Speech, Time, and Nature
Maturidi gives three proofs from common speech, temporal nature of power, and natural analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's further proofs that power is specific to each act, including the argument from common speech, the temporal limitation of power, and the analogy with natural forces.

Core Idea
Power cannot enable contradictory acts; it is specific to one type, just as fire only burns.

Maturidi now provides three additional proofs. First, he points to common speech: no one says a disbeliever is aided to faith, but affirm assistance for the believer. Second, he argues that power does not last long enough for two acts, especially contradictory ones. Finally, he draws an analogy with natural forces like fire and snow, which act by necessity. Each cause is specific to its effect, so power over belief and disbelief must be distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage for lesson 2 and its translation.
Notice the three 'wa-ayḍan' markers.
2
Analyze the proof from common speech
How does Maturidi use what people actually say about believers and disbelievers? What does it establish?
This is a social linguistic proof.
3
Analyze the temporal proof
Why can't power last two moments? How does that prove specificity?
Think about the impossibility of combining opposites.
4
Analyze the analogy
What is the analogy with fire and snow? How does it illustrate distinct powers?
Natural forces act by their inherent nature; likewise, spiritual closeness and enmity have different causes.
5
Synthesize
Summarize how these three proofs together reinforce the conclusion that power for each type is distinct.

Power, Compulsion, and Moral Responsibility
Maturidi exposes the first consequence: loss of power leads to compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that power does not persist leads to compulsion and undermines moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Denying power at the moment of action makes every act compelled.

Maturidi begins by showing the ugly consequences of the Mu'tazila's view on power when examined closely. Although their doctrine sounds pleasant at first, it leads to the conclusion that actions occur without power, necessitating compulsion. Let's see how this unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim about power
Read the passage and find where they state that power does not remain for two moments and is not at the time of the act.
This is the key premise.
2
See the implication: the act occurs without power
Note Maturidi's deduction: if no power at the time, the act occurs without it, which implies compulsion (iḍṭirār).
3
Understand the conclusion: person becomes compelled
The loss of power makes the person compelled, yet they still call him friend or enemy—a contradiction.
This exposes the unsoundness of their view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر طرفا مما يبين قبح قول المعتزلة عند التحصيل ، وإن كان قولهم في الإطلاق قولا لذيذا فى السمع يشبه أن يكون حقا ، / والله الموفق وهو من أن قولهم أن القدرة لا تبقى وقتين ، وأنها ليست وقت الفعل ، فوقع الفعل في الحقيقة ولا قوة له وقت وجوده ، أوذلك علم الاضطرار ووقوع الفعل بالطبع ؛ ثم الدلالة أن حق مثله الاضطرار أن فقد جميع الأسباب التي بها الفعل لوقته يحيل الفعل " ويوصف صاحبه بالاضطرار ، ففوت القدرة التي لها الفعل أحق بذلك ، فصيروه مضطرا إلى ما يصير به وليا لله تعالى ، وعدوا له عند الاختيار .
ومما يوضح ذلك أيضا أن من قولهم : إن من أراد التحرك للوقت الثاني منه أنها تقع لا محالة ، ولا يقدر صرفها إلا بمنع من قبل غيره ، وذلك آية الضرورة ثم وجبت الولاية والعداوة بمثله ، وذلك وحش في العقل .
وأيضا أن من قولهم أن ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهى إلا على المجاز لما وإنما معناه أنه مأمور به بمعنى أن كان من قبل ليفعل فيه ، فإذا لم يكن يكن هو مأمورا به ولا منهيا لم يكن بالفعل مؤتمرا ولا مرتكبا النهى وقته ، و به تجب العداوة والولاية ، فصارا فى الحقيقة لا لطاعة ولا لمعصية ، أو لا أمر ولا نهى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
وعلى ذلك قولهم : إنه مأمور بالفعل فى الوقت الثاني ، وفى الثانى مأمور به الوقت الثالث ، كذلك أبدا ، فلا يفعل الذى أمر بكل وقت ، وليس بتارك للأمر ؛ لما ليس بمأمور وقت الترك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then mention a portion of what shows the ugliness of the Muʿtazila's statement upon investigation, although their statement in absolute terms is a pleasant saying to hear resembling truth. And Allah is the grantor of success. And it is from their statement that power does not remain for two moments, and that it is not at the time of the act, so the act occurs in reality and there is no power for it at the time of its existence—or that necessitates knowledge of compulsion and the occurrence of the act by nature. Then the indication is that the like of compulsion requires that when all the causes by which the act occurs for its time are lost, the act becomes impossible and its possessor is described as compelled. So the loss of the power by which the act occurs is more deserving of that. So they made him compelled to that by which he becomes a friend of Allah Almighty and an enemy of Him upon choice. And further clarifying that is also that from their statement: that whoever intends to move in the second moment, it occurs inevitably and he cannot avert it except by prevention from another; that is a sign of necessity. Then friendship and enmity become obligatory by the like of it, and that is monstrous in reason. And also from their statement: that there is no command or prohibition upon him at the time of the act except metaphorically; its meaning is that he is commanded in the sense that he was commanded before to do it. So if he is not commanded nor prohibited at that time, he is not at the time of the act obedient nor committing the prohibition. And by it friendship and enmity become obligatory; so they become in reality not for obedience nor for disobedience, or there is no command or prohibition. And there is no power except by Allah. And upon that is their statement: that he is commanded with the act in the second moment, and in the second he is commanded with it in the third moment, and so on always. So he does not do what he is commanded with each time, and he is not a rejector of the command because he is not commanded at the time of quitting. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
This passage exposes the ugly consequences of the Muʿtazila's position on power (qudra) when examined closely, although in absolute terms it sounds pleasant. The author explains their view: power does not persist for two moments and is not present at the time of the act. This implies that the act occurs without power, which necessitates compulsion (iḍṭirār) and that the act occurs by nature (ṭabʿ). The author argues that if all causes are lost, the act is impossible and the person is described as compelled. Since the Muʿtazila claim that power is lost, the person becomes compelled in his actions. Yet they call him a friend or enemy of Allah based on his choice, which is contradictory. The author clarifies with an example: if one intends to move in the second moment, the movement occurs inevitably and cannot be averted except by external prevention. This is a sign of necessity (ḍarūra), and making friendship and enmity obligatory based on such compulsion is monstrous in reason. Then the author addresses their claim that there is no command or prohibition at the time of the act except metaphorically. If a person is not commanded or prohibited at that moment, he is neither obedient nor disobedient. Thus, reward and blame become meaningless. Finally, the author discusses their statement that a person is commanded for a future act step by step. Since he is never commanded at the exact time of the act, he never does what he is commanded and is never considered a rejector of the command. This shows the absurdity of their position.

Key Terms
قدرة
اضطرار
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila view preserves free will and moral responsibility.
Correction: Actually, it leads to compulsion because the agent has no power at the moment of action, making all acts involuntary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Mu'tazila statement about power not remaining for two moments lead to?
A) That the act occurs by choice.
B) That the act occurs by compulsion (iḍṭirār).
C) That the act occurs with full power.
D) That the act is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view that power does not persist makes all actions compelled, negating moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own sin by feeling that you had no power to avoid it? How does this lesson's argument about compulsion challenge that excuse?
?
If you truly believe that every action you commit is with Allah's granting of power, how should that change your sense of accountability before Him tonight?
?
When you attribute your good deeds to yourself and your bad deeds to 'circumstances,' are you not falling into the same contradiction as the Mu'tazila—claiming choice while denying power?

The Inevitability of Movement
Maturidi uses an example to show the Mu'tazila view leads to necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila example of intending to move in the second moment reveals the necessity of action and the absurdity of assigning moral categories.

Core Idea
If movement is inevitable, then reward and blame based on it are irrational.

Maturidi presents a second argument clarifying the previous point. He uses the Mu'tazila's own example of intending to move in a future moment to show that the action occurs inevitably without choice, making friendship and enmity based on such actions monstrous.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example
Read the example: a person intends to move in the second moment. According to the Mu'tazila, the movement occurs inevitably unless prevented externally.
2
Identify the sign of necessity
The inevitability of the movement is a sign of necessity (ḍarūra), not free choice.
3
Recognize the consequence
If actions are necessary, then assigning friendship or enmity (reward/punishment) based on them is irrational—'monstrous in reason'.
This reinforces the absurdity of the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر طرفا مما يبين قبح قول المعتزلة عند التحصيل ، وإن كان قولهم في الإطلاق قولا لذيذا فى السمع يشبه أن يكون حقا ، / والله الموفق وهو من أن قولهم أن القدرة لا تبقى وقتين ، وأنها ليست وقت الفعل ، فوقع الفعل في الحقيقة ولا قوة له وقت وجوده ، أوذلك علم الاضطرار ووقوع الفعل بالطبع ؛ ثم الدلالة أن حق مثله الاضطرار أن فقد جميع الأسباب التي بها الفعل لوقته يحيل الفعل " ويوصف صاحبه بالاضطرار ، ففوت القدرة التي لها الفعل أحق بذلك ، فصيروه مضطرا إلى ما يصير به وليا لله تعالى ، وعدوا له عند الاختيار .
ومما يوضح ذلك أيضا أن من قولهم : إن من أراد التحرك للوقت الثاني منه أنها تقع لا محالة ، ولا يقدر صرفها إلا بمنع من قبل غيره ، وذلك آية الضرورة ثم وجبت الولاية والعداوة بمثله ، وذلك وحش في العقل .
وأيضا أن من قولهم أن ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهى إلا على المجاز لما وإنما معناه أنه مأمور به بمعنى أن كان من قبل ليفعل فيه ، فإذا لم يكن يكن هو مأمورا به ولا منهيا لم يكن بالفعل مؤتمرا ولا مرتكبا النهى وقته ، و به تجب العداوة والولاية ، فصارا فى الحقيقة لا لطاعة ولا لمعصية ، أو لا أمر ولا نهى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
وعلى ذلك قولهم : إنه مأمور بالفعل فى الوقت الثاني ، وفى الثانى مأمور به الوقت الثالث ، كذلك أبدا ، فلا يفعل الذى أمر بكل وقت ، وليس بتارك للأمر ؛ لما ليس بمأمور وقت الترك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then mention a portion of what shows the ugliness of the Muʿtazila's statement upon investigation, although their statement in absolute terms is a pleasant saying to hear resembling truth. And Allah is the grantor of success. And it is from their statement that power does not remain for two moments, and that it is not at the time of the act, so the act occurs in reality and there is no power for it at the time of its existence—or that necessitates knowledge of compulsion and the occurrence of the act by nature. Then the indication is that the like of compulsion requires that when all the causes by which the act occurs for its time are lost, the act becomes impossible and its possessor is described as compelled. So the loss of the power by which the act occurs is more deserving of that. So they made him compelled to that by which he becomes a friend of Allah Almighty and an enemy of Him upon choice. And further clarifying that is also that from their statement: that whoever intends to move in the second moment, it occurs inevitably and he cannot avert it except by prevention from another; that is a sign of necessity. Then friendship and enmity become obligatory by the like of it, and that is monstrous in reason. And also from their statement: that there is no command or prohibition upon him at the time of the act except metaphorically; its meaning is that he is commanded in the sense that he was commanded before to do it. So if he is not commanded nor prohibited at that time, he is not at the time of the act obedient nor committing the prohibition. And by it friendship and enmity become obligatory; so they become in reality not for obedience nor for disobedience, or there is no command or prohibition. And there is no power except by Allah. And upon that is their statement: that he is commanded with the act in the second moment, and in the second he is commanded with it in the third moment, and so on always. So he does not do what he is commanded with each time, and he is not a rejector of the command because he is not commanded at the time of quitting. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
This passage exposes the ugly consequences of the Muʿtazila's position on power (qudra) when examined closely, although in absolute terms it sounds pleasant. The author explains their view: power does not persist for two moments and is not present at the time of the act. This implies that the act occurs without power, which necessitates compulsion (iḍṭirār) and that the act occurs by nature (ṭabʿ). The author argues that if all causes are lost, the act is impossible and the person is described as compelled. Since the Muʿtazila claim that power is lost, the person becomes compelled in his actions. Yet they call him a friend or enemy of Allah based on his choice, which is contradictory. The author clarifies with an example: if one intends to move in the second moment, the movement occurs inevitably and cannot be averted except by external prevention. This is a sign of necessity (ḍarūra), and making friendship and enmity obligatory based on such compulsion is monstrous in reason. Then the author addresses their claim that there is no command or prohibition at the time of the act except metaphorically. If a person is not commanded or prohibited at that moment, he is neither obedient nor disobedient. Thus, reward and blame become meaningless. Finally, the author discusses their statement that a person is commanded for a future act step by step. Since he is never commanded at the exact time of the act, he never does what he is commanded and is never considered a rejector of the command. This shows the absurdity of their position.

Key Terms
ضرورة
ولاية
عداوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila example supports free will because the person intends the action.
Correction: The example actually shows the action is inevitable despite intention, indicating necessity, not free choice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the inevitability of the movement in the example signify?
A) Free choice.
B) A sign of necessity.
C) The absence of intention.
D) The power of the agent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila example of intended movement actually shows that actions are necessary, making moral responsibility irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take credit for a good deed, do you ever pause to consider that your ability and intention to do it were granted by Allah? How would acknowledging that dependence change the way you view your own piety?
?
Think of a time you excused a sin by saying 'It was my nature' or 'I couldn't help it.' What would Maturidi say about that excuse? Are you willing to stop hiding behind inevitability and truly take ownership before Allah?
?
If you truly believed that every action you perform is either directly willed by Allah or inevitably occurring, would your feeling of accountability toward Him grow weaker or stronger? What does your actual daily behavior reveal about your true belief?

Command, Prohibition, and the Regress of Obligation
Maturidi shows the Mu'tazila view nullifies obedience and leads to a regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila view on command and prohibition being metaphorical destroys the basis for obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
If commands are only metaphorical at the time of action, then no act can be obedient or disobedient.

Maturidi now tackles the Mu'tazila claim that there is no real command or prohibition at the moment of the act, only metaphorical. This has radical consequences: since the agent is not actually commanded at the time, he can never be obedient or disobedient, rendering moral categories empty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila view on command
They say there is no command or prohibition at the time of the act except metaphorically—the real command was before.
2
See the immediate implication
If no command or prohibition at the time, then the agent is not obedient or disobedient at that moment.
Thus, reward and blame based on that act are baseless.
3
Consider the regress problem
They also say a person is commanded for a future act step by step, so he never does what he is commanded at the exact time, and never leaves the command because he is not commanded at the time of leaving.
This shows the absurdity of their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

C) The agent is always disobedient.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view on command and prohibition being metaphorical destroys the possibility of genuine obedience and leads to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever think, 'I was not commanded at this moment'? How does this subtle mindset nullify your sense of accountability before Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila prioritized an abstract principle of justice over the reality of Allah's commands. In your own life, have you ever justified disobedience by appealing to a 'higher' principle, only to find that your practice became empty?
?
Maturidi exposes the regress: you are always commanded for the future but never act under a present command. Do you find yourself postponing obedience, telling yourself you will do it later, while never actually doing it now? What does that reveal about your heart?

Rational Principle Against Mu'tazila on Command and Capacity
Maturidi uses a rational principle to refute the Mu'tazila on command and capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that a command for a future time does not obligate immediate action, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite view on human capacity.

Core Idea
A command for the future does not obligate action in the present.

Maturidi begins by laying a rational principle: if you are commanded to do something tomorrow, you are not commanded to do it now. This simple observation, he argues, unravels the Mu'tazilite view on divine commands and human capacity. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording establish the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'فكذلك', 'فيجب', 'ثم'.
2
Identify the rational principle
The principle states: anyone commanded to do an act tomorrow is not commanded to do it now. Identify how Maturidi extends this to a near future time.
This principle is the foundation of the whole argument.
3
Trace the logical consequences
Work through the chain: if no command now, then no prohibition of its opposite now. Then the reality of command and prohibition is nullified. Then note the final point about capacity (qudra).
The argument leads to the conclusion of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligation of what cannot be done).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذى فى العقل دركه أن كل مأمور بالفعل للغد ليس بمأمور هو به للحال ، فكذلك يجب فى الوقت القريب ، فيجب أنَّ الذي أمر بالفعل للوقت الذى يتلوه ليس بمأمور به للحال فى العقل ، ثم ليس بمأمور به فى الوقت ، ولا منهي عن ضده، فيبطل حقيقة الأمر والنهي بما في العقل احتماله على قولهم، ويبطل قولهم بما في العقل دفعه ، وهم مع ذلك لا يجعلون له قدرة في ذلك الفعل فيكون تكليف ما لا يطاق على قولهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the rational principle comprehends it is that everyone commanded with an act for tomorrow is not commanded with it at present. So likewise is the case for the near time. So it is necessary that he who is commanded with an act for the time that follows it is not commanded with it at present in reason. Then he is not commanded with it at the time, nor prohibited from its opposite. So the reality of command and prohibition is nullified by what reason can accommodate in their statement, and their statement is nullified by what reason repels. And they, along with that, do not assign to him any power for that act, so it is obligation of what is not within capacity according to their statement. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 864

Commentary
This passage presents a rational principle (al-aṣl alladhī fī l-ʿaql) against the Muʿtazila. The author argues that if someone is commanded to perform an act tomorrow, he is not commanded to perform it now. The same applies to a near future time. Therefore, the person commanded to act at a later time is not commanded at the present moment in reason. Since he is not commanded at that time, nor prohibited from its opposite, the reality of command and prohibition is nullified according to what reason can accommodate from their statement. Moreover, reason rejects their statement. Additionally, the Muʿtazila do not assign any power (qudra) to that person for that act at that time, which results in imposing an obligation that cannot be fulfilled (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). This is a classic critique of the Muʿtazila view that power is created at the time of the act and does not persist.

Key Terms
أصل
عقل
أمر
نهي
قدرة
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a command for a future time implies immediate obligation.
Correction: The rational principle clarifies that obligation applies only at the appointed time, not before.

Checkpoint
What does the rational principle conclude about someone commanded to do an act at a future time?
A) He is commanded to do it now.
B) He is not commanded to do it now.
C) He is prohibited from doing it now.
D) He has power to do it now.

Key Takeaway
A command for a future time does not obligate immediate action; otherwise, it would lead to obligating the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat future religious obligations (e.g., prayer, charity) as if they are already due now, causing unnecessary stress? Or do you neglect them until the last moment? How does this rational principle help you balance urgency and patience?
?
When you lack capacity to perform a good deed at this moment—whether due to time, resources, or personal weakness—do you still feel guilt as if you are sinning? How does Maturidi's argument free you from false guilt and redirect your focus to what you can do now?
?
Consider a command from Allah that you have postponed for a future time. Are you using this rational principle as an excuse to delay obedience, or as a genuine discernment of the appropriate time? What concrete step will you take today toward fulfilling that command at its proper time?

Refutation of the Metaphorical Command View
Maturidi refutes the metaphorical command view with a regress argument and rational principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazili view of command and prohibition being merely metaphorical leads to logical absurdity and invalidates the reality of divine commands.

Core Idea
If command is only metaphorical at the moment of action, then no actual obedience or disobedience occurs.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi refutes a Mu'tazili position that divine command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the precise moment an action occurs. He shows that this view leads to an infinite regress and ultimately makes the concept of obligation meaningless. Maturidi uses rational principles to demonstrate the absurdity of this stance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili claim
Read the passage to identify the view being refuted: that command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the time of the act. Note that this means the command is considered as a past command, not a present one.
The phrase 'ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهى إلا على المجاز' captures their claim.
2
Follow the regress argument
Maturidi shows that if command applies only to future times, then at each present moment there is no command, leading to an infinite postponement. See how he describes the scenario of command for the next time, and then for the time after that.
The argument uses the example of being commanded for the second time, then third, etc.
3
Grasp the rational principle
The principle that a command for tomorrow is not a command for today is used to argue that the Mu'tazili view contradicts the intellect. Understand how this principle applies to the near time and invalidates the reality of command.
Maturidi says 'الأصل الذي في العقل دركه أن كل مأمور بالفعل للغد ليس بمأمور هو به للحال'.
4
Recognize the consequence of taklif ma la yutaq
Maturidi concludes that since the Mu'tazili do not assign power to the servant at the time of the act, their view implies obligating what cannot be borne, which is rationally impossible.
The phrase 'تكليف ما لا يطاق' is explicitly mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقوله المسلمون وأيضا أن من قولهم أن ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهى إلا على المجاز لما وإنما معناه أنه مأمور به بمعنى أن كان من قبل ليفعل فيه، فإذا لم يكن هو مأمورا به ولا منهيا لم يكن بالفعل مؤتمرا ولا مرتكبا النهى وقته، وبه تجب العداوة والولاية، فصارا في الحقيقة لا لطاعة ولا لمعصية، أو لا أمر ولا نهى، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ذلك قولهم: إنه مأمور بالفعل في الوقت الثاني، وفي الثاني مأمور به الوقت الثالث، كذلك أبدا، فلا يفعل الذي أمر بكل وقت، وليس بتارك للأمر؛ لما ليس بمأمور وقت الترك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الأصل الذي في العقل دركه أن كل مأمور بالفعل للغد ليس بمأمور هو به للحال، فكذلك يجب في الوقت القريب، فيجب أن الذي أمر بالفعل للوقت الذي يتلوه ليس بمأمور به للحال في العقل، ثم ليس بمأمور به في الوقت، ولا منهي عن ضده، فيبطل حقيقة الأمر والنهي بما في العقل احتماله على قولهم، ويبطل قولهم بما في العقل دفعه، وهم مع ذلك لا يجعلون له قدرة في ذلك الفعل فيكون تكليف ما لا يطاق على قولهم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Muslims say, and also from their statement that there is not upon him at the time of the act any command or prohibition except metaphorically, and its meaning is that he is commanded in the sense that he was previously to do it, so if he is not commanded nor forbidden, then he is not by the act obeying nor committing the prohibition at its time, and thereby enmity and friendship are necessitated, so they become in reality neither for obedience nor disobedience, or there is no command and no prohibition, and there is no power except with God. And upon that is their statement: He is commanded with the act in the second time, and in the second he is commanded with it in the third time, likewise forever, so he does not do that which he is commanded with every time, and he is not an abandoner of the command because he is not commanded at the time of abandonment, and there is no power except with God. Then the principle that the intellect grasps is that everyone commanded with an act for tomorrow is not commanded with it for the present, so likewise it must be in the near time, so it must be that the one who is commanded with the act for the time that follows is not commanded with it for the present in the intellect, then he is not commanded with it at the time, nor forbidden from its opposite, so the reality of command and prohibition is invalidated by what the intellect can entertain of it according to their statement, and their statement is invalidated by what the intellect repels, and they with that do not assign to him any power in that act, so it becomes an obligation of what cannot be borne according to their statement, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 864

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazili position regarding divine command and prohibition. Maturidi argues that if command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the time of the act—meaning that the command is merely a past command—then the reality of command is lost. He presents a regress: if a command for an act applies only to a future time, then at the actual time of the act there is no command or prohibition, so the act is neither obedience nor disobedience. This leads to an infinite regress where the commanded act is always postponed. The intellect's principle that a command for tomorrow is not a command for today shows that the Mu'tazili view invalidates the true meaning of command and prohibition. Moreover, since they do not grant power to the servant at the time of the act, it results in an obligation of what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq), which is absurd. The conclusion is that their position is rationally untenable.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
نهى (prohibition)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (obligation of the impossible)
القدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that command and prohibition are timeless, but here Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazili view leads to no command at the moment of action.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that the Mu'tazili metaphorical interpretation actually eliminates the existence of command and prohibition at the moment of action, which contradicts the rational understanding of divine obligation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue is the consequence of the Mu'tazili claim that command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the time of the act?
A) The act becomes obligatory.
B) The act becomes impossible.
C) The reality of command and prohibition is invalidated.
D) The servant gains power for the act.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazili metaphorical view of command leads to logical absurdity and contradicts the rational foundation of divine obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay an act of obedience, do you ever subconsciously treat God's command as if it is not currently binding? How does Maturidi's refutation of the metaphorical command view expose the self-deception in such delay, and what will you do today to act on a command you have been postponing?
?
If you believed that at the very moment of a sinful action there is no actual prohibition, would that weaken your resistance? How does this passage force you to recognize that God's command or prohibition is present and immediate, and how should that change the way you face temptation in the next hour?
?
Consider an obligation you feel you lack the strength to fulfill. Are you tempted to reinterpret the command as less than fully binding, effectively adopting a 'metaphorical command' view in your heart? What specific step will you take tonight to treat that command as real and to ask God for the power to obey?

The Maturidi Principle on Power for the Act
Maturidi states the disagreement with al-Husayn and gives his principle on power for the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that an act cannot exist without power in its agent, and that this distinguishes Maturidi from al-Husayn's view.

Core Idea
An act without power in its agent is meaningless and transfers to another.

Imam Maturidi begins by addressing a disagreement with al-Husayn al-Najjar regarding the capacity of the disbeliever to perform acts of obedience. While al-Husayn attributes everything except infallibility and divine success to the disbeliever, Maturidi focuses on the essential condition of power (qudra) for any act. In this passage, he lays down a foundational principle: the existence of an act without power in its performer invalidates the act and attributes it to another agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from disagreement to principle.
Maturidi uses a very concise style.
2
Identify the two positions
Find al-Husayn's view and the Maturidi response. What do they agree on? What is the only point of disagreement?
3
Understand the principle
Focus on the sentence starting with 'Then the principle according to us'. What does Maturidi say about act and power?
This is the key teaching of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى عندهم : ثم المسألة بينهم وبين الحسين لا معنى لها؛ لأن الحسين يقول: كل شيء يكون به فعل الطاعة كان مع الكافر سوى العصمة والتوفيق. وهم وافقوه بأنه لا يوصف بعصمة ولا توفيق، فحصل اختلافهم على تسميته قوة أو لا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الأصل عندنا في المسألة أن وجود الفعل ولا قوة لمن له الفعل عليه يبطل معنى الفعل ويصرفه إلى غيره، وكذلك وجود الفعل ممن هو جاهل به، وهو غير جائز، ثم كان الخطاب لازما بسبب العلم، وإن لم يكن حقيقته مما لو طلب يظفر به، فكذلك القدرة، والفاجر بالذي لا يلزمه الكلفة لفوت ما به يطاق كما لا يلزم المجنون لفوت ما يعلم، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The second according to them: Then the issue between them and al-Husayn has no meaning, because al-Husayn says: Everything by which the act of obedience occurs was with the disbeliever except for infallibility (isma) and success (tawfiq). And they agreed with him that he is not described with infallibility nor success, so their disagreement resulted concerning its naming as 'power' or not, and there is no power except with God. Then the principle according to us in the issue is that the existence of the act without power for the one to whom the act belongs invalidates the meaning of the act and turns it to another, and likewise the existence of the act from one who is ignorant of it, and that is not permissible, then the address (khitab) becomes necessary due to knowledge, even if its reality is not something sought, so likewise power (qudra), and the wicked person is not obligated with the burden due to the loss of that by which it is borne, just as the insane person is not obligated due to the loss of that by which he knows, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 864

Commentary
This passage discusses a secondary issue: the disagreement between the Maturidis and al-Husayn (likely al-Husayn al-Najjar) about the power (qudra) of the servant. Al-Husayn claims that everything required for an act of obedience, except infallibility and divine success, is present with the disbeliever. The Maturidis agree that the disbeliever is not described with infallibility or success, so the only difference is whether to call that capacity 'power' or not. Maturidi then states his own principle: the existence of an act without power in its agent invalidates the meaning of the act and transfers it to another agent. Similarly, an act cannot come from an ignorant agent. Therefore, the divine address (taklif) is dependent on knowledge and power. The wicked person (fajir) is not obligated with a burden that requires capacity he lacks, just as the insane are not obligated due to lack of understanding. This establishes that obligation is contingent on the presence of capacity.

Key Terms
الحسين
العصمة
التوفيق
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidi denies any power to the servant at all.
Correction: In this passage, Maturidi affirms that an act requires power in its agent; without it, the act is invalid. He does not deny that the servant has some power, but insists it is essential.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if an act occurs without power in the one to whom the act belongs?
A) The act is still valid.
B) The act is nullified and attributed to another.
C) The act becomes impossible.
D) The act becomes a sin.

Key Takeaway
An act requires power in the agent; without it, the act is invalid and attributed to another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you attribute it to your own power or do you recognize that true power belongs to Allah? How does Maturidi's principle challenge your assumption about your own capability?
?
Reflect on a sin you committed. Did you consider that you lacked divine success (tawfiq) in that moment? How does realizing that every act requires power from Allah change your attitude towards your failures?
?
Maturidi says the act of an ignorant agent is not permissible. In what areas of your life are you acting ignorantly, without seeking knowledge? What is preventing you from seeking the necessary knowledge before acting?

The Maturidi Principle on Obligation and Capacity
Maturidi explains the necessity of knowledge and power for obligation, using the example of the wicked and insane.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine obligation (taklif) depends on the presence of knowledge and power, and that those lacking capacity (like the wicked or insane) are not obligated.

Core Idea
Obligation requires both knowledge and power; without them, it is unjust.

Having established the necessity of power for any act, Imam Maturidi now extends the same logic to the divine address (khitab). He argues that just as knowledge is a prerequisite for the address being meaningful, so is power. The wicked person (fajir) who lacks the capacity to obey is not obligated, just as the insane are not obligated due to lack of understanding. This ensures that Allah's command is always just and within human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi connects knowledge and power.
2
Trace the analogy
Maturidi says the address is necessary due to knowledge. Then he says 'so likewise power'. What does this analogy compare?
Knowledge is to address as power is to obligation.
3
Apply to the wicked and insane
How does Maturidi's principle apply to the wicked and the insane? What do they lack?
The wicked lack power, the insane lack knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the wicked are fully obligated despite lacking capacity.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that the wicked person is not obligated due to loss of capacity, just as the insane are not obligated.

Checkpoint

A) Because he is sinful.
B) Because he lacks the capacity to bear it.
C) Because he is ignorant.
D) Because he denies Allah.

Key Takeaway
Divine obligation is contingent on the presence of both knowledge and power in the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever judge others for failing to meet obligations while ignoring that they may not have the knowledge or ability you have? How will you train yourself to check that assumption before you speak?
?

?
If you truly believed that Allah never burdens a soul beyond its capacity, how would your response to trials or failures change? What is stopping you from living that belief right now?

Al-Ka'bi's Limited Notion of Power
Maturidi introduces al-Ka'bi's claim and refutes it by critiquing his limited understanding of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that obligating the impossible is intuitively repugnant by showing that al-Ka'bi's conception of power is limited to physical health.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi's 'intuition' only works if power means physical health — but it is more.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, asserted that it is intuitively repugnant (qabih) for God to obligate what cannot be borne. Maturidi begins his refutation by questioning what 'power' really means. He argues that al-Ka'bi's notion of capacity is restricted to manifest physical health, ignoring other dimensions of power. This lesson unpacks that initial critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let al-Maturidi's words sink in without interpretation.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Find where al-Ka'bi states that obligating the impossible is repugnant by intuition. Note his reasoning.
The claim is in the first sentence.
3
Examine al-Maturidi's rebuttal
Notice how al-Maturidi critiques al-Ka'bi's definition of power: he says it only holds if power is just physical health. Then he gives counterexamples.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بل كلف الله صاحب يعلم أنه لا يستطيع'.
4
Understand the self-defeating argument
Al-Maturidi shows that al-Ka'bi's own claim about repugnance at the time of the act actually supports al-Maturidi's position, because it assumes no power at that time.
The phrase 'فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما ذكره الكعبى مما يبين وهمه في قضاياه. زعم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق قبيح في العقل بالبديهة، وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب يعلم أنه لا يستطيع، وكذلك تكليف ما يجهل مثله في البديهة. يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة وذلك وقت الفعل، فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيها ادعى. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل، وأما من ضيع القوة فهو حق أن يكلف مثله، ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم؛ إذ هي تحدث تباعا على قدر حرص لم يحدث يحدث بتضيعه؛ إذ العبادة واختياره وميله إليها، آثر بذله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم ذكر معنى يدل على سفهه فقال: لو جاز التفريق بين الله وبين ما يكون من غيره لجاز أن يكون الكذب من غيره يكون منه صدقا، فلا أدرى أى ٢٠
Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned that shows his error in his judgments. He claimed that obligating what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) is repugnant (qabih) in the intellect by intuition. This is only in the intellect that does not recognize capacity (taqa) other than manifest power (quwwa zahira) which is health (sihha). As for other than that, it is not as he says. Rather, God obligated one who knows that he cannot, and likewise obligating what is similar to ignorance in intuition. It is said to him: Likewise, obligating what cannot be borne for the time of the act is repugnant in the intellect, and what you claimed of repugnance is only in the intellects of those who deem impossible the existence of an act without power at the time of the act, so his statement becomes upon investigation repugnant in the intellect, if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except with God. As for the principle, obligating one from whom capacity has been withheld is invalid in the intellect, but as for one who squanders power, it is right to obligate him similarly. And if he were not obligated similarly, no one would be obligated except those who obey, and that is not the condition of trial (mihna). And there is no power except with God. And according to us, power (qudra) in the sound and healthy individual occurs successively according to the measure of eagerness and does not occur by squandering it; because worship and his choice and inclination toward it, he prefers its expenditure and chooses the act that he performs, and there is no power except with God. And upon such a consideration, according to us and according to them, is the matter of understanding and knowledge, and there is no power except with God. Then he mentioned something that indicates his foolishness, saying: If it were permissible to differentiate between God and what comes from other than Him, then it would be permissible for lying to come from other than Him and be truth from Him, so I do not know which...
Source pages p. 865

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, who claimed that it is intuitively repugnant (qabih) for God to obligate what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq). Maturidi argues that this intuition only holds for an intellect that understands power (qudra) as merely physical health (sihha). But there are other aspects of power; for example, God obligates someone knowing he cannot perform it. Furthermore, the claim of repugnance itself presupposes that the act cannot occur without power at the time of the act, which is actually the same point the Maturidis make. So the very claim is self-defeating. Then Maturidi distinguishes: it is indeed invalid to obligate one from whom capacity is withheld, but it is valid to obligate one who squanders his capacity. Otherwise, only the obedient would be obligated, which negates the purpose of trial (mihna). Maturidi then explains his view of power: it occurs in the healthy person successively based on eagerness and choice, not by squandering. Finally, he notes an absurd statement by al-Ka'bi that would allow lying from God, showing his error.

Key Terms
الكعبى (al-Ka'bi)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (obligation of the impossible)
قبيح (repugnant)
القوة (power)
الطاقة (capacity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bi believes that it is always repugnant for God to obligate the impossible, based on a naïve view of power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this only holds under a limited definition of power as physical health. There are other dimensions of capacity, and God's obligations can be just even when the person cannot perform.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the key flaw in al-Ka'bi's claim that obligating the impossible is intuitively repugnant?
A) Intuition is not a reliable source of knowledge in theology.
B) Al-Ka'bi's notion of power is limited to physical health.
C) It is actually possible for God to obligate the impossible in all cases.
D) The intellect cannot judge divine actions at all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation reveals that al-Ka'bi's intuition rests on an incomplete definition of power, and his own argument becomes self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought something God required of you was 'unfair' based on your limited sense of your own ability? How does Maturidi's refutation push you to trust God's wisdom even when you feel incapable? What is one area of obedience you have dismissed as 'impossible' that you now realize you should re-evaluate?
?
When you face a command that seems beyond your power, do you assume God expects less? How does this passage challenge you to rely on 'there is no power except with God' rather than on your own assessment of capacity?
?
Al-Ka'bi's argument seemed intuitive and reasonable to him, yet Maturidi showed it was flawed. What is one 'intuitive' belief you hold about God's justice or your own limitations that may actually be an incomplete understanding in need of deeper theological reflection?
