Capacity Withheld vs. Squandered
Maturidi distinguishes between capacity withheld and squandered, and explains the trial purpose and his view of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between one whose capacity is withheld and one who squanders power, and how this relates to the divine purpose of trial (mihna).

Core Idea
Obligating is just when capacity is squandered, not when withheld — that is the test.

Having critiqued al-Ka'bi's narrow notion of power, Maturidi now draws a crucial practical distinction. It is invalid to obligate someone from whom capacity is withheld, but valid to obligate one who squanders his capacity. Otherwise, only the obedient would be obligated, which defeats the purpose of trial. This lesson explores that distinction and Maturidi's own view of how power exists in humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing the shift from the earlier argument to the principle and the trial purpose.
The passage begins with 'وأما الأصل'.
2
Identify the principle and exception
Find the sentence that states that obligating one from whom capacity is withheld is invalid, but obligating one who squanders power is valid.
The distinction is in the first two sentences.
3
Understand the trial purpose
Note why Maturidi says that if the squanderer were not obligated, only the obedient would be obligated, and that is not the condition of trial.
The phrase 'وليس ذلك شرط المحنة' is key.
4
Grasp Maturidi's view of power
Read the explanation of power (qudra) in the sound individual: it occurs successively based on eagerness and choice, not by squandering.
This shows Maturidi's occasionalist-like view of divine creation of power.
5
Note the final absurdity
The final sentence shows al-Ka'bi making an absurd comparison about lying and truth, indicating his foolishness.
This is a rhetorical flourish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما ذكره الكعبى مما يبين وهمه في قضاياه. زعم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق قبيح في العقل بالبديهة، وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب يعلم أنه لا يستطيع، وكذلك تكليف ما يجهل مثله في البديهة. يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة وذلك وقت الفعل، فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيها ادعى. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل، وأما من ضيع القوة فهو حق أن يكلف مثله، ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم؛ إذ هي تحدث تباعا على قدر حرص لم يحدث يحدث بتضيعه؛ إذ العبادة واختياره وميله إليها، آثر بذله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم ذكر معنى يدل على سفهه فقال: لو جاز التفريق بين الله وبين ما يكون من غيره لجاز أن يكون الكذب من غيره يكون منه صدقا، فلا أدرى أى ٢٠
Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned that shows his error in his judgments. He claimed that obligating what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) is repugnant (qabih) in the intellect by intuition. This is only in the intellect that does not recognize capacity (taqa) other than manifest power (quwwa zahira) which is health (sihha). As for other than that, it is not as he says. Rather, God obligated one who knows that he cannot, and likewise obligating what is similar to ignorance in intuition. It is said to him: Likewise, obligating what cannot be borne for the time of the act is repugnant in the intellect, and what you claimed of repugnance is only in the intellects of those who deem impossible the existence of an act without power at the time of the act, so his statement becomes upon investigation repugnant in the intellect, if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except with God. As for the principle, obligating one from whom capacity has been withheld is invalid in the intellect, but as for one who squanders power, it is right to obligate him similarly. And if he were not obligated similarly, no one would be obligated except those who obey, and that is not the condition of trial (mihna). And there is no power except with God. And according to us, power (qudra) in the sound and healthy individual occurs successively according to the measure of eagerness and does not occur by squandering it; because worship and his choice and inclination toward it, he prefers its expenditure and chooses the act that he performs, and there is no power except with God. And upon such a consideration, according to us and according to them, is the matter of understanding and knowledge, and there is no power except with God. Then he mentioned something that indicates his foolishness, saying: If it were permissible to differentiate between God and what comes from other than Him, then it would be permissible for lying to come from other than Him and be truth from Him, so I do not know which...
Source pages p. 865

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, who claimed that it is intuitively repugnant (qabih) for God to obligate what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq). Maturidi argues that this intuition only holds for an intellect that understands power (qudra) as merely physical health (sihha). But there are other aspects of power; for example, God obligates someone knowing he cannot perform it. Furthermore, the claim of repugnance itself presupposes that the act cannot occur without power at the time of the act, which is actually the same point the Maturidis make. So the very claim is self-defeating. Then Maturidi distinguishes: it is indeed invalid to obligate one from whom capacity is withheld, but it is valid to obligate one who squanders his capacity. Otherwise, only the obedient would be obligated, which negates the purpose of trial (mihna). Maturidi then explains his view of power: it occurs in the healthy person successively based on eagerness and choice, not by squandering. Finally, he notes an absurd statement by al-Ka'bi that would allow lying from God, showing his error.

Key Terms
الطاقة (capacity)
القوة (power)
المحنة (trial)
التضييع (squandering)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi unconditionally allows obligating the impossible.
Correction: He only allows it for those who have capacity but squander it; not for those from whom capacity is withheld.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it valid to obligate someone who squanders their power?
A) Because power is always present in everyone.
B) Because otherwise only the obedient would be obligated, which negates the purpose of trial.
C) Because God can do whatever He wills without reason.
D) Because squandering power is a sin that deserves punishment.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's position: obligating the squanderer is just because they had the power but wasted it, fulfilling the purpose of trial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given countless capacities—time, health, intellect. Where in your life are you squandering these powers, and what would it look like to stop rationalizing and start expending them for the very trial God has placed you in?
?
If you truly believed that only the obedient would be obligated, would you still obey out of love or only out of fear? How does knowing that trial includes both options press you to examine your deepest intentions?
?
What one choice, inclination, or eagerness have you been suppressing today because it seemed too costly? Will you let that fear conserve your power or will you spend it in worship and righteousness, knowing that squandered power will be accounted for?

Who Can Be Justly Obligated?
Maturidi states the principle regarding obligation of one who wastes power versus one prevented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational principle that obligation is valid only for those who waste their own power, not for those prevented from capacity.

Core Idea
Obligation is a trial, not a favor for the obedient.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles a fundamental question of divine justice: who can be justly obligated? He distinguishes two cases—one prevented from ability, and one who wastes it—and argues that only the latter can be rightly commanded. The principle rests on the idea that obligation is a test (mihna), not a reward for anticipated obedience.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two cases
Read the passage and note the two scenarios: someone prevented from capacity (taqa) and someone who wastes his power (qudra). What does Maturidi say about each?
The terms taqa and qudra are closely related but distinct.
2
Follow the logical argument
Maturidi argues that if the waster were not obligated, then only the obedient would be obligated. Why does that negate the purpose of trial?
3
Reflect on the test (mihna)
Consider how this principle underscores that obligation is a genuine test of human choice and effort, not a guarantee of success or failure.
This connects to broader discussions of free will and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل ، وأما من ضيع القوة فهو حق أن يكلف مثله ، ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع ، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
As for the principle: obliging one who has been prevented from capacity (taqa) is invalid in reason. And as for one who has wasted power (qudra), it is right to oblige him like that. And if one like him were not obliged, then only one who obeys would be obliged, and that is not the condition of trial (mihna). And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 865

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle in Maturidi's theology of obligation (taklif) and divine justice. Maturidi distinguishes two cases: first, if a person is prevented from having capacity (mana‘ 'anhu al-taqa), then obliging him is invalid (fasid) in reason. Second, if a person has wasted his power (dayya‘a al-qudra), then it is right to oblige him. The reasoning is that if the latter were not obliged, then only those who already obey would be obligated, which would negate the purpose of trial (mihna) — testing humanity through commands and prohibitions. The passage underscores that obligation is not conditioned upon the absence of sin or the presence of guaranteed obedience; rather, it serves as a test.

Key Terms
طاقة
قوة
تكليف
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any impossible obligation is unjust; therefore God cannot oblige someone who fails to obey.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the person who wastes his own power (i.e., fails due to his own negligence) can be justly obligated, because obligation is a trial for human choice, not a reward for guaranteed obedience.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, obliging whom is valid in reason?
A) The one prevented from capacity.
B) The one who wastes his power.
C) The one who already obeys.
D) The one who is physically ill.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that the true test of obligation applies to those who waste their power, not those who are prevented from capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you wasted your power (time, health, knowledge) and then complained that an obligation was too hard? How does this lesson expose that excuse as invalid?
?
Do you secretly wish that obligation applied only to those who already obey? What does that reveal about your view of God's justice?
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except through God.' In what area of your life are you relying on your own wasted power instead of seeking His strength? What will you change today?

Maturidi's Doctrine: Power Arises with Effort in the Sound Person
Maturidi explains that power arises in the sound person according to effort and choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's view that human power (qudra) is not a static substrate but arises repeatedly in the sound person according to his eagerness and choice, while always dependent on Allah.

Core Idea
Human power is dynamic, arising from effort and choice, yet wholly dependent on Allah.

This lesson examines Imam Maturidi's positive doctrine on human power. He argues that power resides in the sound person and occurs repeatedly based on one's eagerness. The passage clarifies that power is not a fixed attribute but is connected to human choice and effort, while emphasizing that all power ultimately comes from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall statement.
Be aware of the unclear phrase that may affect interpretation.
2
Identify Maturidi's core claim
Notice that he says power occurs 'according to the degree of his eagerness' and is linked to choice and inclination.
3
Understand the role of eagerness (haras)
Reflect on how effort and desire are integral to power's occurrence.
Power is not a one-time creation but a recurring attribute.
4
Note the repeated tasbih
Observe how the phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' brackets the passage, underscoring divine dependence.
5
Apply to broader context
Consider how this view differs from static conceptions of power and how it relates to human responsibility.
This understanding applies also to knowledge and understanding.

وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم إذ هي تحدث تباعا على قدر حرص لم يحدث يحدث بتضيعه إذ فها العبادة واختياره وميله إليها آثر بذله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم ولا قوة إلا بالله
And according to us, power (qudra) is in the sound and healthy person, since it occurs repeatedly according to the degree of his eagerness; he does not do an act [؟] by wasting it, since in it are worship and his choice and his inclination towards it; he prefers to expend it and chooses the act that he performs. And there is no power except through God. And on such an estimation, according to us and them, is the matter of understanding and knowledge. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 865

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's positive doctrine on power (qudra). He states that power resides in the sound and healthy person (al-sahih al-salim). It arises repeatedly (tahduthu tibā‘an) according to the degree of one's eagerness (haras). Power is not a fixed entity but is connected to the person's choice (ikhtiyar) and inclination (mayl) towards the act. If one wastes his power, the act does not occur. Worship is associated with this choice and effort. This understanding applies to both the speaker and the opponent regarding matters of understanding and knowledge. The passage emphasizes the dependence of all power on God through the repeated tasbih.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
صحيح سليم (sahih salim)
حرص (haras)
اختيار (ikhtiyar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is a fixed, static substance that either exists or does not exist.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that power occurs repeatedly (tahduthu tibā‘an) according to the person's eagerness, highlighting its dynamic, effort-dependent nature.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Maturidi holds that human power is dynamic, arising repeatedly in the sound person according to his eagerness and choice, while always dependent on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi says there is no power except through Allah. In what area of your life are you relying on your own strength rather than Allah's? What act of worship or obedience have you been putting off because you think you lack the power? Will you take a step today, trusting Allah to give you the ability?

Three Answers to the Hypocrites' Excuse of Inability
Maturidi provides three answers to the hypocrites' excuse of inability and addresses a counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three logical responses to the hypocrites' claim that inability excuses them from jihad, and how he distinguishes between excusable and blameworthy inability.

Core Idea
Inability arising from one's own misuse of choice is not a valid excuse.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles an objection raised by the hypocrites who used the verse "If we had the ability, we would have gone out with you" to justify their failure to join jihad. He provides three nuanced distinctions showing that their excuse does not hold water. As we read, note how he differentiates between genuine lack of means and self-inflicted loss of ability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
The Arabic text has high OCR risk; some phrases may be unclear.
2
Identify the three answers
Find each of the three aspects (أوجه) and note how each refutes the hypocrites' excuse.
3
Understand the counter-objection
See the opponent's attempt to rejoin and how Maturidi's three answers apply here too.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Grasp the central idea that inability due to personal waste of power is different from genuine prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجاجه بقوله : لَوْ اسْتَطَعْنَا لَخَرَجْنَا مَعَكُمْ قد سنا ما عليه في ذلك ، وما يُظهر أن خصمه أشد لاتباع ذلك ومعرفته منه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم عارض بما لا يحقق لهم العذر بالفقر إلا بما لا يتهيأ لهم الفعل ، وذلك المعنى في فقد القدرة موجود قال / الشيخ رحمه الله : جوابه من أوجه ثلاثة ، أحدها أن الذي معه من المال لو لم يبلغه لم يعرض عليه وقوة فعل البلوغ لم تكن فلم يمنع الغرض ، فمثله أمر وجود الأمرين محال للحال" وعدمها. وأيضا أن الأمر المعتاد أن تحدث معه فا القوى تباعا على قدر ما يختاره العبد ويريد من الفعل ، فهي تحدث لا محالة هذه إلا أن تضيعها هو يصرف الاختيار إلى غير ما يفعل بها ، فبالتضييع عدم القدرة ، والأخرى بالمنع ؛ لذلك اختلفا . والثالث إجازة وجود الفعل في حال لا قدرة فيها ولا يجوز فى حال لا سبب به يحدث القدرة ، ثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس بنظير للآخر ، والله الموفق . ثم عارض بجواز الأمر بالخروج على أن يعطوا المال معه ، أجاز أبطل قوله ، وإن لم يجز ترك قوله . وزعم أنه إن أجاز أبطل قوله ، وإن لم يجز ترك قوله . فيجاب في هذا بالأوجه الثلاثة من التفريق بالمبلغ وغير المبلغ و بالأمر المعتاد ، وبما ينكر هو الفعل لوقت عدم الأسباب ولا ينكر لوقت عدم القوة ، ولما أحد الوجهين يعدم لا به والآخر لا . ولو كان يعلم أيضا حدوث الأملاك على التتابع بخبر الصادق لكان الجواب فيها لا يختلف ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما في المعارضة إذا حققت إحالته ، وهو أن تصريف المال الملك له لا يحتمل ، كالجركة مع خلق الله الجسم، لا حركة ضرورة ولا اختيار ، ونوع
And his argument with His saying: 'If we had the ability, we would have gone out with you' - we have already expounded on that, and what shows that his opponent is more severe in following that and knowing it than him. And there is no power except through God. Then he countered with that which does not verify their excuse by poverty except by that which makes the action impossible for them, and that meaning is found in the loss of power. The Sheikh, may God have mercy on him, said: The answer to it is from three aspects: One of them is that the one who had wealth - if he did not reach it, he was not offered it, and the power of reaching was not there, so the purpose was not prevented. The same is the matter of the existence of the two things being impossible for the state and their non-existence. Also, the normal manner is that powers occur successively according to what the servant chooses and intends of action, so they occur necessarily unless he wastes them by directing his choice to other than what they do. So by wasting, there is loss of power; the other is by prevention; therefore they differ. The third is the permissibility of the existence of an action in a state where there is no power, and it is not permissible in a state where there is no cause by which power occurs. It is established that one of the two aspects is not analogous to the other. And God is the Giver of success. Then he countered with the permissibility of commanding to go out on condition that they be given wealth with it. If he permitted, he invalidated his own statement; if he did not permit, he abandoned his statement. And he claimed that if he permitted, he invalidated his statement; if he did not permit, he abandoned his statement. So the answer to this is with the three aspects: the distinction between the one who got the amount and the one who did not, and the normal manner, and with what he himself denies the action for the time of absence of causes but does not deny it for the time of absence of power. And for one of the two aspects is non-existence not by it, and the other is not. And if he also knew the occurrence of ownerships in succession by the report of the truthful, the answer would not differ. And there is no power except through God. With what is in the counter-argument when its impossibility is verified, which is that the disposal of wealth is not possible for him, like [al-jarka?] with God's creation of the body, no motion by necessity and no choice, and a type.
Source pages p. 869

Commentary
This passage is a detailed response to an objection based on the Qur'anic verse 'If we had the ability, we would have gone out with you' (9:42), uttered by the hypocrites as an excuse for not joining the expedition of Tabuk. The author first notes that he has already explained this verse. The opponent (apparently a Mu'tazilite or similar) argues that the hypocrites' excuse of poverty and inability is valid. The author then gives three answers from Shaykh (= Maturidi himself). First answer: If someone had wealth but did not have the means to reach the destination, then the power to reach was absent, and the excuse stands. But this is analogous to the present case: the hypocrites lacked the means but not necessarily the power. Second answer: Normally, powers (quwā) arise successively according to the servant's choice and intention. If the servant misuses his choice, he wastes his power, resulting in inability due to his own fault, which is different from prevention by God. The two cases differ. Third answer: An action can occur in a state where there is no power if there is a cause that generates power, but not if there is no cause. Thus, the hypocrites' case is not analogous to the example of the one who lacked the means. After giving these three answers, the author addresses a counter-objection: if it is permissible to command the hypocrites to go out even if they were given wealth, the opponent's argument collapses; if not permissible, the opponent abandons his own claim. The response reiterates the three distinctions: the one who received the wealth vs. the one who did not, the normal manner of powers, and the distinction between absence of causes and absence of power. The passage concludes by noting that even if one knew by revelation (khabar al-ṣādiq) that ownerships occur in succession, the answer would remain the same. The final part discusses the impossibility of the opponent's analogy, involving a reference to motion (ḥaraka) and the creation of the body, though the text is somewhat corrupt.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
القدرة
العذر
الفقر
القوة
الاختيار
التضييع
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any kind of inability is a valid excuse for not performing an obligation.
Correction: Inability due to one's own misuse of choice (such as wasting resources or misdirecting intention) is blameworthy and does not excuse one from the obligation, unlike genuine lack of means.

Checkpoint
What is the first of the three answers to the hypocrites' excuse?
A) The hypocrites were lying.
B) The one who had wealth but did not reach it was not offered it, so the power of reaching was absent.
C) The Prophet commanded them anyway.
D) The hypocrites had no money.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's three answers show that inability caused by one's own misuse of choice is blameworthy, not excusable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you excused yourself from a religious duty by saying 'I can't' or 'I'm not able.' Now, be honest: was that inability truly beyond your control, or was it because you had wasted your capacity by choosing something else? How does Maturidi's second answer confront you with this reality?
?
If you truly believe that God gives powers according to your choices, what does it mean for the excuses you make today? Will you continue to hide behind 'I don't have the ability' when you know deep down you have misdirected your intention?
?
The hypocrites used poverty as an excuse, but Maturidi shows that the real issue was their choice not to go. Where in your life are you using a lack of resources (time, money, energy) as a smokescreen for a lack of will? What would it cost you to stop that deception?

Threefold Rejoinder to the Argument from Prevention
Maturidi gives three rejoinders to an opponent's argument about prevention being better.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's three rejoinders to the argument that prevention is always more beneficial, and how he defends divine justice in the face of human disobedience.

Core Idea
God's prevention and withholding are not the same; human choice and waste of power explain inequality.

The opponent argues that if prevention were not better, God would prevent all evil. Yet God does not prevent every sin. Does this mean God fails to do what is best? Imam Maturidi answers with three distinct arguments, showing that prevention is not always analogous to non-prevention, and that human responsibility plays a key role. Let's examine his responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the three-fold structure indicated by 'أحدها', 'وأيضا', 'والثالث'.
2
Identify the opponent's position
Before the three rejoinders, Maturidi summarizes the opponent's argument. Understand why they think prevention is always better.
The opponent uses the verse about hypocrites excusing themselves by poverty.
3
Analyze the first rejoinder
The first answer focuses on the wealth not reaching the threshold required for the act. The goal was not achievable from the start.
4
Analyze the second and third rejoinders
The second answer invokes the customary pattern of powers being granted according to choice. The third distinguishes between lack of capacity due to no cause vs. an act without capacity.
They show that the two scenarios (prevention and non-prevention) are not analogous.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these three rejoinders together defend God's wisdom and human accountability. Why does Maturidi emphasize that 'one face is not analogous to the other'?
This is the key takeaway.

ما كان غيره أصلح فى الدين : وإن قال : المنع ، فقد أقر أن المنع قد يكون أصلح ، فكل عاص لم يمنع عنه لم يفعل به الأصلح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . واحتجاجه بقوله : لَوْ اسْتَطَعْنَا لَخَرَجْنَا مَعَكُمْ قد سنا ما عليه في ذلك ، وما يُظهر أن خصمه أشد لاتباع ذلك ومعرفته منه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم عارض بما لا يحقق لهم العذر بالفقر إلا بما لا يتهيأ لهم الفعل ، وذلك المعنى في فقد القدرة موجود . قال / الشيخ رحمه الله : جوابه من أوجه ثلاثة ، أحدها أن الذي معه من المال لو لم يبلغه لم يعرض عليه وقوة فعل البلوغ لم تكن فلم يمنع الغرض ، فمثله أمر وجود الأمرين محال للحال" وعدمها. وأيضا أن الأمر المعتاد أن تحدث معه فا القوى تباعا على قدر ما يختاره العبد ويريد من الفعل ، فهي تحدث لا محالة هذه إلا أن تضيعها هو يصرف الاختيار إلى غير ما يفعل بها ، فبالتضييع عدم القدرة ، والأخرى بالمنع ؛ لذلك اختلفا . والثالث إجازة وجود الفعل في حال لا قدرة فيها ولا يجوز فى حال لا سبب به يحدث القدرة ، ثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس بنظير للآخر ، والله الموفق .
What other than it is more beneficial in religion? And if he says: prevention, then he has admitted that prevention may be more beneficial. So every disobedient person who was not prevented from [disobedience] was not treated with the most beneficial. And there is no power except with God. And his argument by His saying: 'If we were able, we would have gone out with you' (Quran 9:42) we have already explained what is upon that. And it shows that his opponent is more severe in following that and knowing it than him. And there is no power except with God. Then he objected with what does not validate their excuse of poverty except by what makes the act not possible for them, and that meaning is present in the lack of power. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: His answer is from three aspects. One of them: that what he has of wealth, if it did not reach him, it would not be presented to him, and the power to reach was not present, so the goal was not prevented. So its like is the matter of the existence of both things being impossible in the state and their non-existence. Also: that the customary matter is that powers occur successively according to what the servant chooses and intends of the act. So they occur inevitably except that he wastes them by turning his choice to other than what they are used for. So by wasting comes the lack of power, and the other [lack] is by prevention; therefore they differ. And the third: permitting the existence of the act in a state where there is no power, and it is not permitted in a state where there is no cause by which power is generated. It is established that one of the two aspects is not analogous to the other. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 869

Commentary
This passage presents an opponent's argument and Imam Maturidi's threefold rejoinder. The opponent claims that if prevention (man') were not beneficial, God would not prevent anyone; but God does prevent some from good, so prevention must be beneficial. Thus, God not preventing sinners from sin means He is not doing what is most beneficial for them. The opponent also cites the Quranic verse (9:42) where the hypocrites excuse themselves by poverty. Maturidi first summarizes the opponent's challenge: they argue that the excuse of poverty (lack of ability) does not excuse them because they had some means. Then Maturidi gives three answers. First, the wealth they had was insufficient to reach the goal (going out for battle); if it had been sufficient, God would have allowed them. So the goal was not actually achievable. Second, by the customary divine pattern, powers are granted successively according to the servant's choice. The servant wastes his power by using it for other purposes, leading to lack of power by his own doing, not by prevention. Third, it is possible to have an act without capacity in some cases (like acts of nature) but not when there is no cause that generates capacity. Thus, the two scenarios (lack of means vs. lack of power) are not analogous. The conclusion: one face (prevention) is not like the other (non-prevention).

Key Terms
المنع
القدرة
الأصلح
الأوجه الثلاثة
المبلغ
الأمر المعتاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prevention and non-prevention are necessarily analogous in all cases.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prevention (by God) and non-prevention (allowing sin) are not analogous because the circumstances differ: in one case the means are lacking, in the other the servant wastes his power by choice.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of the three answers Maturidi gives to the opponent's argument about prevention?
A) That God is unjust if He prevents good
B) That powers are granted according to the servant's choice, and waste causes lack of power
C) That prevention is always better than non-prevention
D) That the opponent has no excuse

Key Takeaway
God's prevention is not always analogous to non-prevention; human choice and the circumstances of capacity determine whether lack of power is due to divine withholding or human waste.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to do good, do you immediately assume God has prevented you, or do you examine where you have wasted your own power? Which is easier for your ego to accept?
?
Maturidi says powers occur according to what the servant chooses. Think of a recent moment you used your power for something trivial instead of something meaningful. How can you start channeling your capacities toward what truly matters, knowing that waste leads to lack?
?
The opponent's argument implies God must always prevent harm. But Maturidi says prevention can be beneficial. How does your view of God's wisdom change when you realize that your failures may not be His withholding but your own misdirection of choice? What will you do differently tomorrow?

Answering the Objection on Commanding the Poor with Wealth
Maturidi answers an objection about commanding the poor to go out with wealth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends divine justice by arguing that divine commands are not invalidated by foreseeing future provisions, and that power and act are not mutually exclusive.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the future does not change the nature of obligation or ability.

This passage continues a debate about whether God can command a poor person to go out for jihad without providing wealth. The opponent argues that such a command would be inconsistent. Maturidi responds by outlining three distinct aspects of the argument, then he delves deeper into the nature of power and action to show that divine commands are just and coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection setup
Understand the opponent's dilemma: if God commands the poor to go out while giving them wealth, that refutes the opponent; if He doesn't, the opponent abandons his own statement.
The opponent is trying to argue that poverty excuses from the command.
2
Identify the three aspects
Maturidi offers three aspects in answer: distinction between sufficient and insufficient means, the customary pattern, and that the opponent denies the act in lack of causes but not in lack of power.
These three aspects show the objection is not uniformly valid.
3
Understand the deeper point on power and act
Maturidi argues that power and act can coexist, using the analogy of motion by necessity with incapacity and choice with power. He refutes the idea that act occurs only in absence of power.
This reinforces that divine commands are not contradictory.
4
Reflect on the role of knowledge
Even if one knows by prophetic report that wealth will come successively, the answer remains the same. Knowledge does not change the nature of obligation or ability.
This counters the idea that foreknowledge changes the moral calculus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض بجواز الأمر بالخروج على أن يعطوا المال معه ، أجاز أبطل قوله ، وإن لم يجز ترك قوله . وزعم أنه إن ... فيجاب في هذا بالأوجه الثلاثة من التفريق بالمبلغ وغير المبلغ و بالأمر المعتاد ، وبما ينكر هو الفعل لوقت عدم الأسباب ولا ينكر لوقت عدم القوة ، ولما أحد الوجهين يعدم لا به والآخر لا . ولو كان يعلم أيضا حدوث الأملاك على التتابع بخبر الصادق لكان الجواب فيها لا يختلف ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما في المعارضة إذا حققت إحالته ، وهو أن تصريف المال الملك له لا يحتمل ، كالجركة مع خلق الله الجسم، لا حركة ضرورة ولا اختيار ، ، ونوع حركة الضرورة قد يكون مع العجز ومثله الاختيار مع القدرة ، على أن القدرة لو كانت بحيث لا يجامعها الفعل ليبطل أن يكون بها الفعل بل بعدمها يكون ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ألا ترى أن الأموال والأسباب مع قيامها بفعل على بقائها توصف بقدمها / وبحال إبقاء القدرة ، فمثل ذلك وصف التقدم ، والله أعلم .
Then he objected by permitting the command to go out while giving them the wealth with him. If he permits, he refutes his own statement, and if he does not permit, he abandons his statement. He claimed that he... So it is answered by the three aspects: the distinction between what reaches and what does not, and the customary matter, and by what he denies: the act occurs at the time of lack of causes but he does not deny it at the time of lack of power. And one of the two aspects is not nullified by it and the other is not. And even if he also knew the occurrence of properties successively by the report of the truthful, the answer therein would not differ. And there is no power except with God. Along with what in the objection, if you verify its impossibility, is that the disposal of wealth belongs to its owner and does not entail [anything], like motion with God's creation of the body: no motion by necessity or choice. And a type of motion by necessity may be with incapacity, and likewise choice with power. And if power were such that the act does not accompany it, then it would be invalid for the act to occur by it, but it occurs by its absence. And there is no power except with God. Do you not see that properties and causes, with their persistence by an act on their continuance, are described by their anteriority and by the state of preservation of power? So like that is the description of precedence. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 869

Commentary
This passage continues the debate. The opponent raises a new objection: if God commands the poor to go out (for jihad) and also gives them wealth, then either the command is permissible or not. If permissible, the opponent's earlier claim that poverty is an excuse is refuted. If not permissible, the opponent abandons his own position. Maturidi answers using the same three aspects: the distinction between sufficient and insufficient means, the customary pattern of powers, and the fact that the opponent himself denies the act at the time of lack of causes but does not deny it at the time of lack of power. He adds that even if one knew by prophetic report that wealth would come successively, the answer would still be the same. He then points out a deeper impossibility in the objection: the disposal of wealth is the owner's act and does not force anything, just as God's creation of the body does not force motion. Motion by necessity can coexist with incapacity, just as choice coexists with power. If power could not coexist with its act, then the act would be by the absence of power, not by power. He gives an example: properties and causes, although they persist through acts, are described as prior to the act, just as power is described as preceding the act. The point is to show that power and act are not mutually exclusive in a simple temporal sense.

Key Terms
المعارضة
الأسباب
القدرة
الحركة الضرورة
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge of future events changes the nature of obligation or excuse.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that even if one knew by a true report that wealth would come, the answer does not differ. Foreknowledge does not alter the moral or rational structure of the command.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if someone knew by prophetic report that wealth would come successively, would the answer to the objection differ?
A) Yes, because knowledge changes the situation
B) No, the answer remains the same
C) Only if the report is certain
D) It depends on the person

Key Takeaway
Divine commands are just and coherent; foreknowledge does not change obligation, and power and act are not mutually exclusive but coexist in a proper relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of Allah that seems beyond your current means, do you dismiss it as impossible, or do you take the first step trusting that power and act are not bound by your present limitations? What excuse have you been hiding behind that this lesson exposes?
?
Have you ever justified neglecting an obligation by saying, 'If Allah had willed, He would have made it easy for me'? How does Maturidi's distinction between knowledge of the future and the nature of the command challenge that excuse in your own life?
?
Think of a specific act of obedience you have delayed because you felt you lacked the power or resources. After understanding that power is a condition, not a concurrent cause, what is the one step you can take today that you have been avoiding? Will you commit to it now?

Prior Incapacity and the Coexistence of Power with Act
Maturidi refutes arguments against coexistence of power and act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that prior incapacity does not prevent action at the time of power, and that divine power coexists with its act, refuting objections based on God's knowledge of the act as existent.

Core Idea
Power and act are simultaneous; prior incapacity cannot negate future power.

Imam Maturidi continues his discussion on divine power and action by addressing two related claims: that prior incapacity prevents the act at the time of power, and that power cannot coexist with its act because God sees the act as already existent. Using symmetry, causal necessity, and a careful analysis of what it means for God to 'see' an act, Maturidi defends the orthodox Maturidi position that power is simultaneous with its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument on prior incapacity
Maturidi begins by showing that if prior incapacity prevented act at power time, then prior power would necessitate act at incapacity time—impossible, so the original is also impossible.
This is a symmetry argument.
2
Understand the consequence of lack of power causing act
If act occurred due to lack of power, then power would cause continuance of act, leading to absurdity.
This refutes the idea that absence of power is cause.
3
Analyze the opponent's objection about God's knowledge
The opponent claims power cannot be with the act because God sees the act as existent. Maturidi asks for clarification: does 'existent' mean completed or ongoing?
The distinction is crucial.
4
Follow Maturidi's response and analogy
Maturidi uses the example of throwing to show that cause and effect are simultaneous, and that God seeing the act does not preclude power coexisting.
The analogy shows power seen with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى المسألة سوى ما قدمنا ذكره من الأدلة أنه لو كان العجز المتقدم يمنع الفعل لوقت القدرة ليجب أن يكون القدرة المتقدمة توجب الفعل لوقت العجز ، وفى إحالة ذلك إحالة الأول ، ولو كان الفعل يقع لفقد القدرة لكان كلما دام [ت] دام الفعل ؛ إذ أسباب الأشياء لما هى لها كلما دامت أوجبت دوامها ، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالوجود معها . ثم زعم أن القدرة محال كونها مع النعل ؛ لأن الله يراه موجودا ، ومحال كون القدرة مع الفعل الموجود . قيل : عنيت بالوجود الفراغ منه أو هو فيه ؟ فإن قال : الفراغ منه ، بان كذبه عند من يعقل ، وأبطل قوله : يجوز أن يكون فى ذلك بالبدل وهو العجز معدوما ، ولم يجب القول بإحالة العجز مع المعدوم، وإن كان يراه معدوما ، يكن العدم متقضيا بل هو مشغول به. ثم يقال له : الله يواليه ويعاديه فعله أو قبله أو بعده؟ فإن قال : قبله ، أحاله ، وإن قال : بعده أبطل قوله : يراه موجودا ؛ لأنه يحقق وجود فعل العداوة والولاية ، ولا عداوة ولا ولاية ، وإن قال : في حاله ، قيل : صار السبب مع المسبب موجودا ولم ينف كون الفعل وإن كان يرى الولاية والعداوة موجودتين فمثله القدرة . وأيضا أنه على أي حال يراه يرى القدرة معه ، على ما نرى إلقاء الشيء وإخراجه مع خروج ذلك وإلقائه ، ولم يبطل حق الإلقاء والإخراج بما يرى الشيء على ما يراه ، فمثله الذي ذكرت . وإذ جاز أن يراه موجودا ، ومعه الأسباب كلها ، ولم يبعد ذلك ، / كما كذلك يجب أن فمثله القوة ، بل كذلك يجب أن يرى مع الأسباب
And in the question, besides what we have already mentioned of proofs, [it is argued] that if prior incapacity prevented the act at the time of power, then it would follow that prior power necessitates the act at the time of incapacity. And in the impossibility of that is the impossibility of the first. And if the act occurred due to lack of power, then whenever [power] continued, the act would continue; because the causes of things, as long as they continue, necessitate their continuance. And in that is the necessary claim of existence with them. Then he claimed that power is impossible to be with the act, because God sees it as existent, and it is impossible for power to be with the existent act. It was said: Do you mean by 'existence' its completion or while it is ongoing? If he says: its completion, then his falsehood is evident to the rational, and his statement is invalidated. [He claims] it is permissible that in that [case] by substitution, incapacity is non-existent, and he does not assert the impossibility of incapacity with the non-existent, even if he sees it as non-existent; non-existence is not required, rather it is occupied by it. Then it is said to him: Does God see His friendship and enmity [as] His act, or before it, or after it? If he says: before it, he makes it impossible; if he says: after it, he invalidates his statement that He sees it as existent, because he affirms the existence of the act of enmity and friendship, and there is no enmity nor friendship; and if he says: during it, it is said: the cause has become existent with the effect, and it does not negate the act. And even if he sees friendship and enmity as existent, similarly is power. And also that in whatever state he sees it, he sees power with it, just as we see the throwing of a thing and its ejection along with the exit of that and its throwing, and the right of throwing and ejection is not invalidated by what he sees the thing as he sees it. So similarly is what you mentioned. And since it is permissible that He sees it as existent, with all causes with it, and that is not far, so likewise it must be that power is seen with the causes.
Source pages p. 870

Commentary
This passage presents additional proofs on the relationship between incapacity, power, and action. First, Maturidi argues that if prior incapacity could prevent an act at the time when power is present, then by symmetry, prior power would necessitate the act at the time of incapacity. Since the latter is impossible, the former is also impossible. Second, if an act occurred because of lack of power, then whenever power persisted, the act would persist, because causes necessitate their effects. This would lead to the absurdity of the act always existing with power. The opponent then claims that power cannot coexist with its act because God sees the act as already existent. Maturidi asks for clarification: does 'existent' mean the act is completed or ongoing? If completed, the objection fails. The opponent might claim that incapacity is absent at that moment, but then he must explain why incapacity cannot coexist with the non-existent. Further, Maturidi asks about God's seeing His friendship and enmity: is the act seen before, during, or after? If before, it's impossible; if after, then the act is not seen as existent; if during, then cause and effect coexist. The same applies to power: power is seen with the act. Analogously, we see the act of throwing and the motion of the thrown object simultaneously; the act is not negated by the perception. Therefore, power can coexist with its act.

Key Terms
العجز المتقدم
القدرة المتقدمة
سبب
وجود
الفعل
العداوة
الولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's seeing an act as existent implies the act is completed and separate from power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God may see the act as ongoing, in which case power coexists with it.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence Maturidi mentions if prior incapacity could prevent the act at the time of power?
A) That God would be unjust
B) That prior power would necessitate the act at the time of incapacity
C) That power cannot coexist with the act
D) That the act would never occur

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends the coexistence of divine power and act through symmetry, causal necessity, and analysis of God's knowledge, showing that power is simultaneous with its effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power is fully present in every action you perform, or do you sometimes act as if your own ability is independent? How would it change your sense of reliance on Him if you internalized this coexistence?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by thinking 'God already knew it would happen, so it was out of my hands'? How does Maturidi's distinction between completed and ongoing acts challenge that excuse?
?
If God's power is simultaneous with every act you do, are you more or less accountable for your deeds? What does this mean for your daily choices and your desire to change?

Power over Faith Does Not Imply Power over God
Maturidi refutes the claim that power over faith would mean power over God, using the example of Pharaoh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the power to believe is not power over God, and how he refutes objections about divine foreknowledge.

Core Idea
Power is over the act, not over God, even when the act is faith.

Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if someone has power over faith, does that mean they have power over God? He answers by distinguishing the object of power. The discussion leads into deeper questions about divine knowledge and human ability, using Pharaoh as a key example. This lesson walks through the back-and-forth of the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation together to get an overall sense of the argument.
The OCR has some unclear words; focus on the main logical flow.
2
Identify the first objection and answer
Notice the objection that power over faith would imply power over God. Maturidi answers by clarifying that power is not identical to the act of faith.
3
Follow the second objection
The objector compares this to God's power to create, which does not nullify His knowledge. See how Maturidi treats this analogy.
4
Examine the third objection about absolute statements
The text mentions an absolute statement linking faith and knowledge. Consider how Maturidi responds by affirming God's complete knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال علم تم عارض نفسه بأمر فرعين إنه لو كان يقدر على الإيمان لكان يقدر على الله، وهذا في فرعون وكل من في علم الله أنه لا يؤمن. فأجاب بأن ذا لا يجب لأن القدرة غير الإيمان الذى هو المعلوم أنه لا يكون، ولو لزمنا ذلك في القوة ليلزمكم في الأمر. ثم عارض بقدرة الله على إنشاء العالم ليحال من غير أن يجوز الوصف بالقدرة على إبطال علمه، فمثله الأول. ثم عارض حسيناً بالإطلاق أنه أطلق بينه وبين الإيمان، أتقول في أنه أطلق فى إبطال علم الله ؟ ثم قال : الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون، فلو كان لم يكن يخرج من علم الله.
Refutation of knowledge: He objected to himself with two branches: that if he had power over faith, he would have power over God, and this applies to Pharaoh and everyone about whom Allah knows he will not believe. He answered that this does not necessarily follow because power is not the same as faith which is known not to occur, and if that follows for us regarding power, it would follow for you in the matter. Then he objected with Allah's power to create the world that it is impossible without allowing the description of power to nullify His knowledge, so it is like the first. Then he objected with Husayn's [؟] absolute statement that he made it absolute between him and faith, do you say that he made it absolute in nullifying Allah's knowledge? Then he said: Allah knows that if it were how it would be, so if it were, it would not leave Allah's knowledge.
Source pages p. 873

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. An opponent objects that if someone has power over faith, he would have power over God (since faith is from God), using Pharaoh as an example. The answer is that power and faith are distinct; the power is over the act, not over God. The objector then compares it to God's power to create the world, which does not nullify His knowledge. Another objection is raised about an absolute statement linking faith and knowledge of God. Maturidi concludes that God knows what would happen if a disbeliever believed, but that knowledge does not change; the disbeliever would not leave God's foreknowledge.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
الإيمان (al-iman)
علم الله (knowledge of Allah)
فرعون (Pharaoh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows someone will not believe, that person cannot have real power to believe.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between power (ability) and the actual occurrence; divine foreknowledge does not negate the reality of human power.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that power over faith does not imply power over God?
A) Because faith is the same as power
B) Because power is only over the act, and faith is the act, not God
C) Because God controls all power directly
D) Because faith does not exist

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that human power is over actions, not over God, and that divine foreknowledge does not remove that power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you have real power over your faith, or do you sometimes excuse your shortcomings by thinking 'Allah knows I'll never change'? What would it mean to live today as if your next choice is not already predetermined, but fully in your hands?
?
When you consider that Allah knows every detail of your life, including the sins you might commit, does that make you feel trapped or freed? How can you use your God-given power to act in a way that honors His knowledge without despairing of His mercy?
?
If you genuinely have power over your faith (as Maturidi argues), then every moment you choose not to worship or obey is a moment you have actively turned away. Are you ready to stop blaming 'fate' and start owning your choices before Allah?

Refuting the Claim of Freedom from Action
Maturidi refutes the claim that a powerful being can be eternally inactive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects the notion that a powerful being can remain without action forever.

Core Idea
A powerful being cannot be eternally free from action; true intellect rejects such a notion.

Imam Maturidi begins by addressing an opponent's claim that it is permissible for a powerful being to be completely without action at the time of its existence and then forever. He refutes this as a misunderstanding of intellect and dismisses further confused discussion. Let's read his direct response.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Identify what the opponent asserts: that a powerful being can be free from action at its time of existence and then never act again.
The opponent's statement is given in the first line.
2
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Note how Maturidi labels the opponent's view as wrong and argues that true intellect requires departing from such a mental state.
He also dismisses a subsequent discussion about knowledge as confused.
3
Reflect on the dismissal
Consider why Maturidi would skip the discussion on knowledge—he found it unhelpful and driven by confusion.
This shows his method of focusing on sound reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : في الصحيح السليم أنه يجوز أن يخلو عن الفعل وقت كونه، ثم لم أبدا.

قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وما يقوله خطأ، بل يجوز ذلك، ثم زعم أن ذلك معقول، وهو عقل من حق العقل الخروج عما حاله عقلا. ثم تكلم في العلم بما أظن أحدا تأمله إلا عرف أن الحيرة دفعته إليه، فتركته لقلة نفعه. ثم عارض نفسه بالذي قدر على الإيمان والكفر، فلم فعل أحدهما دون الآخر؟ فزعم أن ذا محال؛ لأنه لو كان لا يأتي إلا بواحد كان يكون مضطرا، وقد ثبت الاختيار. ثم عارض بمثله في الله، نقول: قد حاد عن جواب السؤال؛ إذ هو أنه كيف اختار ذا على ضده؟ وليس شرط الاختيار أن يفعل ما شاء، ولكن يختار الأولى به أن يفعل، فإذا فعل ما لا يعرف لماذا فعل ثبت أن لغيره في فعله تدبيرا، على ذلك خرج فعله، والله الموفق.

ومعارضته بالله سبحانه محال على القولين: على قولنا بأنه خالق بذاته فالقول به كالقول بأنه لم قدر وعلم؟ وعلى قوله إن ذلك أصلح في الدين، ولا يسأل من ذلك وصف فعله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

ونحن نحمد الله، قد أغنانا الله عن نحو هذا السؤال، لكن أحببت أن أذكرهما مقداره فما لا يرضى به سؤالا لضعفه؛ ليعلموا به قدره في المرضى به، والله الموفق.

ثم قال: أنه إذ صلحت قوة واحدة للإيمان وضده فلم لا صلح القول بالتقوية عليها، فدفع ذا بالأمر والنهى: وعارض بالسيف والدرهم وإن احتمل استعماله في قتل الولي وإنفاقه في شرى الخمر، لم يجز القول بالإعطاء لذلك. نقول: تمام
And he said: In the sound and correct view, it is permissible that it be free from action at the time of its existence, then not at all. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: What he says is wrong, rather that is permissible. Then he claimed that it is intelligible, and it is an intellect from which the right of the intellect is to depart from what its state is intellectually. Then he spoke about knowledge in a way that I think no one who contemplates it would fail to realize that confusion drove him to it, so I left it due to its lack of benefit. Then he opposed himself with the one who is capable of faith and disbelief: why did he do one rather than the other? He claimed that it is impossible, because if he only came with one, he would be compelled, and choice is established. Then he opposed with the like regarding God. We say: He deviated from answering the question, for it is: how did he choose this over its opposite? And the condition of choice is not that he does whatever he wills, but he chooses the most befitting for him to do. So if he does something whose reason is not known, it is proven that someone else has governance in his action, and on that his action proceeds. And God is the grantor of success. And his opposition regarding God the Exalted is impossible on both views: according to our view that He is the Creator by His Essence, to say that is like saying why did He not decree and know? And according to his view that it is more suitable in religion, and He is not asked about that, describing His action. And there is no strength except with God. And we praise God; God has sufficed us from this kind of question, but I wanted to mention it to the extent that it is not satisfactory as a question due to its weakness, so that they may know its value in the defective one. And God is the grantor of success. Then he said: Since one power suffices for faith and its opposite, why does the statement not suffice for empowering over it? He repelled this by command and prohibition and opposed with the sword and the dirham; although its use is possible in killing the friend and spending in buying wine, it is not permissible to say that the giving is for that. We say: complete.
Source pages p. 878

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim that a powerful being can remain without action for its entire duration, and it addresses the nature of choice between opposites. The opponent asserts that it is sound and correct to be free from action at the time of existence, then never. Imam Maturidi (referred to as 'the Shaykh') rejects this as wrong, noting that the opponent claims it is intelligible, but true intellect requires departing from such a mental state. The author dismisses a further discussion on knowledge as confused and unhelpful. The opponent then raises an objection about human choice: a person capable of faith and disbelief chooses one without compulsion, and this is taken as evidence of free choice. The opponent attempts to apply the same reasoning to God. Imam Maturidi responds that the opponent has deviated from the question, which is how God chooses one thing over its opposite. He clarifies that the condition of choice (ikhtiyār) is not merely doing whatever one wishes; rather, it is selecting what is most befitting (al-awlā) for oneself. If an agent does something for an unknown reason, that would imply someone else has control over his action. This leads to a discussion about divine choice. Imam Maturidi argues that comparing God to humans in this matter is impossible on both views: if God is Creator by His Essence, the question reduces to 'why did He not decree and know?' which is meaningless; if the opponent claims it is a matter of religious benefit, then God is not questioned about His actions. The author then concludes that God has sufficed them from such questions, but he mentions it to show its weakness. The final paragraph introduces a further objection about a single power sufficing for opposites and the analogy of a sword and dirham that can be used for good or evil, but the author cuts it short with 'complete', indicating the refutation is concluded.

Key Terms
الفعل
العقل
الحيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A powerful being can remain without action indefinitely without contradiction.
Correction: Imam Maturidi argues that this is not rationally sound; true intellect recognizes that such a state is not possible for a powerful agent.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what is the status of the opponent's claim that a powerful being can be free from action?
A) It is correct and sound.
B) It is wrong and intellectually unsound.
C) It is permissible but not preferred.
D) It is a matter of personal opinion.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi refutes the notion that a powerful being can be eternally inactive, affirming that true intellect rejects such an idea.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In moments of trial or waiting, do you ever feel that God's power is inactive or distant? How might Maturidi's insistence that true intellect rejects eternal inaction reshape your trust in God's constant involvement?
?
If God is never idle in His power, what does that demand from you in terms of your own use of the abilities He has given you? Examine one area where you have 'power' but have left it inactive—what step can you take today to align with the truth that power implies action?

The Nature of Choice Between Opposites
Maturidi defines true choice and refutes the opponent's analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's definition of true choice as selecting what is most befitting, not merely doing whatever one wills.

Core Idea
True choice is selecting what is most befitting, not arbitrary will.

The opponent raises an objection about human choice between faith and disbelief, then tries to apply the same to God. Imam Maturidi clarifies the nature of choice and argues that the opponent's comparison is misdirected. Let's delve into his explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's objection
The opponent asks: why does a person capable of faith and disbelief choose one without compulsion? He sees this as proof of free choice.
He then tries to apply this to God.
2
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi says the opponent deviates from the question: how does one choose this over its opposite? He then defines choice as selecting the most befitting.
This definition is crucial.
3
See the consequence
If an action is done for an unknown reason, it implies someone else's governance. This leads to a discussion about divine choice.
Maturidi then argues that comparing God to humans is impossible.

?

?
Think of a recent choice you made that you later regretted. Was it because you acted arbitrarily, without seeking what was most befitting? How would your decision change if you had defined choice as 'selecting what is most befitting' rather than 'doing whatever you will'?
?

Are there areas of your life where you act without knowing why—habits, reactions, or routines? Could it be that you are letting others (society, culture, your own desires) govern your actions? How can you reclaim true agency by making choices based on divine guidance?

The Impossibility of Comparing Divine Choice to Human Choice
Maturidi argues that comparing divine and human choice is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi holds that comparing God's choice to human choice is impossible on both theological views.

Core Idea
God's choice is incomparable to human choice due to His unique nature.

After defining choice, Imam Maturidi explicitly argues that comparing God's actions to human actions is impossible, whether one holds that God creates by His essence or that He acts for religious benefit. He then briefly addresses a further analogy about a single power sufficing for opposites. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the impossibility argument
Maturidi states that the opponent's comparison to God is impossible on both views: if God is Creator by His essence, the question is meaningless; if it's about religious benefit, God is not questioned.
This is a key theological point.
2
Understand the sufficiency argument
The opponent then asks: if one power suffices for both faith and its opposite, why not say the power is given for both? Maturidi counters with the example of a sword and dirham that can be used for good or evil.
The passage ends abruptly with 'complete'.
3
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Maturidi says God has sufficed them from such questions, but he mentions it to show its weakness.
This shows his pedagogical intent.

According to Imam Maturidi, what is one of the reasons why comparing God's choice to human choice is impossible?
A) Because God is compelled by His nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decree of God, do you find yourself complaining or questioning His wisdom as if He were a human decision-maker? What will you do today to submit completely to His choice, knowing He is the Creator by essence?
?
The sword in the hand of a murderer is not given for murder, but the murderer misuses it. When you sin, do you blame your God-given abilities or take full responsibility for your choice? How will you train yourself to own your actions and repent sincerely?
?
Imam Maturidi says God has sufficed us from such questions. Are you truly content with God's decree in your life, or is there a hidden resentment? Before you sleep tonight, examine your heart: is there any complaint against Allah? Commit to uprooting it and trusting His perfection.

8 min
Refuting the Objection on Power for Faith and Disbelief
Maturidi refutes an objection about why one chooses faith or disbelief when having power for both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the objection that having power over both faith and disbelief renders choice meaningless, and you will learn the definition of choice (ikhtiyar) as selecting what is most appropriate.

Core Idea
Choice is not about doing anything you want; it is about selecting what is most fitting.

This passage tackles a subtle theological objection: if God gives a person equal power to believe or disbelieve, why does he end up choosing one? The objector claims that if there were free choice, both options should be equally possible in actuality. Imam Maturidi refutes this by clarifying the true nature of choice—it is not raw power but a selection of the better option. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part: the objector asks why, if a person has power over both faith and disbelief, he does one rather than the other. The objector thinks that if he only does one, he is compelled, so choice disappears.
Notice the objector assumes that having power means both outcomes are equally achievable at the moment of action.
2
Identify the flawed assumption
The objector then applies the same reasoning to God, but Maturidi says he has deviated from the answer. Find where Maturidi says 'قد حاد عن جواب السؤال' – he has veered off from answering the question.
The error is in assuming that choice requires the ability to do anything at will.
3
Learn the Maturidi correction
Maturidi states that the condition of choice is not doing what one wills, but choosing the better (الأولى) for oneself to do. This is the key definition.
This distinguishes human choice from divine will.
4
Grasp the implication
If someone performs an action without knowing why, it indicates that another has control over his action. This closes the objection.
Thus, the objector's analogy with God fails because human choice is about selecting the better, not infinite power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالذى قدر على الإيمان والكفر، فلم فعل أحدهما دون الآخر؟ فزعم أن ذا محال؛ لأنه لو كان لا يأتى إلا بواحد كان يكون مضطرا، وقد ثبت الاختيار. ثم عارض بمثله فى الله، نقول: قد حاد عن جواب السؤال؛ إذ هو أنه كيف اختار ذا على ضده؟ وليس شرط الاختيار أن يفعل ما شاء، ولكن يختار الأولى به أن يفعل، فإذا فعل ما لا يعرف لماذا فعل ثبت أن لغيره في فعله تدبيرا، على ذلك خرج فعله، والله الموفق.
Then he opposed himself with the one who has power over faith and disbelief: why did he do one without the other? He claimed that this is impossible, because if he only came with one, he would be compelled, and choice has been established. Then he opposed with its like concerning God. We say: He has deviated from the answer to the question, that is, how did he choose this over its opposite? The condition of choice is not that he does what he wills, but he chooses the better for him to do. So if he does something whose reason he does not know, it is established that another has a role in his action; on that his action proceeds. And God grants success.
Source pages p. 878

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector argues: if a person is given power over both faith and disbelief, why does he actually choose only one? If he could only do one, he would be compelled—but we know choice exists. The author responds that the objector has missed the point. Choice does not mean the ability to do whatever one wills; it means selecting what is most appropriate (al-awla) for oneself. If someone performs an action for no known reason, that suggests external direction. Thus, the objector's analogy with God is invalid.

Key Terms
القدرة
الاختيار
الأولى
الإيمان
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that having power over two opposites means both should be equally realizable, and if one is chosen, choice is absent or compelled.
Correction: Choice is about selecting the better option, not about equal realizability. The presence of choice does not require that both outcomes be actual; it only requires the capacity to select the best.

Checkpoint
What is the author's definition of choice (ikhtiyar) in this passage?
A) The ability to do whatever one wishes
B) The act of selecting what is most appropriate (al-awla)
C) The absence of compulsion
D) Having power over both alternatives

Key Takeaway
Choice is not raw ability but the selection of what is most appropriate; an action without known reason indicates external direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says choice is about selecting what is better for oneself. When you chose to disobey God today, did you truly believe that sin was better for you? Or did you choose without reflecting on what is actually better? What does that reveal about your understanding of your own good?
?
Maturidi points out that if you do something without knowing the reason, it shows someone else is directing your action. Think of a decision you made recently that you cannot fully justify. Are you comfortable with the idea that God may be behind that choice? If not, what does that discomfort say about your trust in His wisdom?
?
The objector assumed that having power over both options means you should be able to choose either freely. But you know you often choose what you later regret. Why do you think you do not always choose what is better? Is it because you don't know what's better, or because you ignore that knowledge? Commit to one change that will help you align your choices with what is truly better.
