Coercion Nullifies Obedience and Faith
Maturidi refutes coercion using logic and Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that divine will cannot be coercion because coercion would eliminate the very concept of obedience and faith.

Core Idea
Coercion destroys the meaning of obedience and faith.

Maturidi begins his refutation by analyzing the implications of coercion. If divine will were coercive, then obedience and belief would lose their meaning. He then cites Quranic verses to show that God's will leaves room for human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argumentative style.
2
Identify the initial argument about inference and coercion
Maturidi argues that if coercion were recognized in non-necessary matters, it would nullify direct knowledge. Understand this foundational point.
3
Understand how coercion negates obedience
Coercion eliminates all obedience and compliance, making the phrase 'if He willed' meaningless. See how this is the core of the refutation.
4
Examine the Quranic verses cited
Look at the verses 'ولو شاء ربك' and 'ولو شئنا لآتينا'. Maturidi uses them to show that God's will does not compel faith; rather, it leaves room for choice and does not nullify His promise.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that faith involves human choice, and the will of coercion cannot override divine truth.
This directly counters the Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستدلال، وذلك نوع ما لا يحتمل الاضطرار، ولو جاز ولو جاز علم الاضطرار فيما ليس في الخلقة احتماله الجاز نفى علم الاضطرار فيما كان ذلك طريقه، فيبطل علم العيان، ما كان ذلك طاعة وائتمارا، والجبر يسقط ذلك كله، فيصير في التحصيل كأنه قال: لو شاء لمنعكم عن الإيمان وعن ملة واحدة، وهذا القول، وإنما أخبر أنه لو شاء لجمعكم على الهدى، وقد آمن بعضهم بالاختيار، ولو كان جبر البقية لم يكن ليجمعهم، ولكن يمنعهم عما أبوا به من دينه وطاعته، وذلك بعيد وحش. وأيضاً أنه لا صنع للخلق في موضع الجبر والقهر، وإنما يرجع ذلك إلى إيمان الخلق، وكل جوهر بخلقته مؤمن مهتد، بل به هداية كثير من الخلق، فإذا ذلك قد شاء وقد كان بالقول بلو شاء لا معنى له، وعلى ذلك قوله: "وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَنْ فِي الأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا". وأيضاً مما يبطل تأويل القسر قوله: "وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا وَلَكِنْ حَقَّ الْقَوْلُ مِنِّي لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ"، ومشيئة الجبر لا تسقط ما ذكر أنه حق، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

وقال الله: "مَنْ يَشَأِ اللَّهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلْهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيم"، وعند المعتزلة إنه شاء جعل الكل على ذلك، وهو عز وجل وعد أن يكون الذي شاء كذلك فلم يكن. وقال عز وجل: "وَلَا تَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذَلِكَ غَدًا إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ"، فلا يخلو هذا الشيء من أن يكون في الخيرات، فهو لغو على قولهم؛ لأنه قد شاء، وإذا لم يكن أيضاً يصير كأنه مأمور بالقول الكذب؛ لأنه أمر أن يقول كذا ولم يكن كذا، وإن كان شراً لا يشاء [ه]، فذكره عندهم لا معنى له، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The inference, and that is a type that does not necessitate coercion, and if it were permissible that knowledge of coercion exists in what is not in nature its possibility, it would negate knowledge of coercion in what is its way, thus nullifying direct knowledge. What was that obedience and compliance, and coercion eliminates all of that, so it becomes in effect as if He said: If He willed, He would prevent you from belief and from one religion, and this statement, and indeed He informed that if He willed He would have gathered you to guidance, and some believed by choice, and if it were coercion of the rest, He would not gather them, but He would prevent them from what they refused of His religion and obedience, and that is far and repugnant. And also that there is no act of creation in the place of coercion and domination, rather that returns to the faith of creation, and every substance by its creation is believing and guided, indeed by it is guidance of many of creation, so that He has willed and it was; the statement 'if He willed' has no meaning. And upon that is His saying: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them together.' And also among what invalidates the interpretation of compulsion is His saying: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me has been justified: I will surely fill Hell.' And the will of coercion does not nullify what He mentioned as truth, and there is no power except by Allah.

And Allah said: 'Whom Allah wills He sends astray, and whom He wills He places on a straight path.' And according to the Mu'tazila, He willed to make all upon that, and He, mighty and majestic, promised that what He willed would be so, but it was not. And He, mighty and majestic, said: 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow," except if Allah wills.' So this thing cannot be free from being among the good deeds, so it is nonsense according to their statement, because He has willed, and if it is not also, it becomes as if he is commanded to say a lie, because he is commanded to say so and it was not so. And if it is evil, He does not will it, so its mention according to them has no meaning. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 887

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili interpretation of divine will as coercion (jabr). Maturidi begins by discussing the nature of inference and coercion, arguing that if coercion were recognized in matters not naturally necessitated, it would negate direct knowledge. He then explains that if divine will were coercive, it would eliminate all obedience and compliance, making the statement 'if He willed' meaningless. He cites the Quranic verse 'ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا' to show that God's will does not compel faith; rather, some believe by choice, and if God had compelled the rest, He would not have gathered them in faith but would have prevented their disobedience. He also uses the verse 'ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها' to argue that the will of coercion cannot nullify the truth of His promise to fill Hell. Next, he addresses the verse 'من يشأ الله يضلله' and notes that the Mu'tazila claim God willed to guide all but failed. He then discusses the verse 'ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك غدا إلا أن يشاء الله', arguing that if the thing is good, the conditional 'except if Allah wills' is meaningless if God has already willed it; if it is evil, God does not will it, so the exception is also meaningless. Thus, he concludes that the Mu'tazili position leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
مشيئة
جبر
اختيار
هداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'ولو شاء ربك' implies that God forces people to believe or disbelieve.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the verse actually shows that God could have compelled faith but chose not to, leaving room for human choice.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the verse 'ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا' prove about divine will?
A) God compels all to believe
B) God could have compelled all but chose not to, leaving room for human choice
C) God's will is ineffective
D) God does not will faith

Key Takeaway
Divine will is not coercion; it allows for human choice, and coercion would negate the meaning of obedience and faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever feel you are doing them simply because you are 'supposed to' rather than out of genuine choice? How does Maturidi's argument that coercion nullifies obedience challenge you to examine the sincerity of your actions? Will you commit today to re-evaluate one routine act of worship and ask yourself if it stems from free choice or mere habit?
?
This lesson shows that God's will allows for human choice even in faith. When you face a trial that tests your belief, do you blame God as if He forced it, or do you see it as a space for you to choose submission? Which perspective aligns with the lesson, and how would your response to the next trial change if you truly believed God is not coercing you?
?
Maturidi insists that if God had coerced, obedience would be meaningless. Think of a person you find difficult to love or forgive. Are you waiting for God to 'force' you to feel differently, or are you willing to choose goodness toward them? What specific step will you take today to exercise your choice in a way that reflects the lesson's teaching?

The Absurdity of the Mu'tazili Interpretation
Maturidi uses two verses to show Mu'tazili inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verses 'من يشأ الله يضلله' and 'ولا تقولن لشيء' to expose the logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazili claim that divine will is coercion.

Core Idea
Mu'tazili interpretation leads to absurdities when applied to these verses.

Maturidi continues his refutation by addressing two more Quranic verses that the Mu'tazila misinterpret. He shows that their interpretation implies that God's will is either ineffective or commands lying, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the second paragraph
Read the Arabic passage from 'وقال الله' to the end. Then read the translation.
This paragraph contains two distinct arguments.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili claim about 'من يشأ الله يضلله'
The Mu'tazila claim that God willed to guide all people, but Maturidi points out that God promised that some would go astray, contradicting their claim.
3
See the contradiction in their position
If God's will were coercive, then His promise to fill Hell would be broken. The fact that Hell will be filled shows His will is not coercive.
4
Examine the verse 'ولا تقولن لشيء'
Maturidi analyzes three possibilities: if the thing is good, the exception is meaningless; if it is not, it commands lying; if evil, God does not will it. All lead to absurdity.
5
Conclude the refutation
The Mu'tazili interpretation leads to logical contradictions, thus the correct understanding is that divine will does not coerce human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستدلال، وذلك نوع ما لا يحتمل الاضطرار، ولو جاز ولو جاز علم الاضطرار فيما ليس في الخلقة احتماله الجاز نفى علم الاضطرار فيما كان ذلك طريقه، فيبطل علم العيان، ما كان ذلك طاعة وائتمارا، والجبر يسقط ذلك كله، فيصير في التحصيل كأنه قال: لو شاء لمنعكم عن الإيمان وعن ملة واحدة، وهذا القول، وإنما أخبر أنه لو شاء لجمعكم على الهدى، وقد آمن بعضهم بالاختيار، ولو كان جبر البقية لم يكن ليجمعهم، ولكن يمنعهم عما أبوا به من دينه وطاعته، وذلك بعيد وحش. وأيضاً أنه لا صنع للخلق في موضع الجبر والقهر، وإنما يرجع ذلك إلى إيمان الخلق، وكل جوهر بخلقته مؤمن مهتد، بل به هداية كثير من الخلق، فإذا ذلك قد شاء وقد كان بالقول بلو شاء لا معنى له، وعلى ذلك قوله: "وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَنْ فِي الأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا". وأيضاً مما يبطل تأويل القسر قوله: "وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا وَلَكِنْ حَقَّ الْقَوْلُ مِنِّي لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ"، ومشيئة الجبر لا تسقط ما ذكر أنه حق، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

وقال الله: "مَنْ يَشَأِ اللَّهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلْهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيم"، وعند المعتزلة إنه شاء جعل الكل على ذلك، وهو عز وجل وعد أن يكون الذي شاء كذلك فلم يكن. وقال عز وجل: "وَلَا تَقُولَنَّ لِشَيْءٍ إِنِّي فَاعِلٌ ذَلِكَ غَدًا إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ"، فلا يخلو هذا الشيء من أن يكون في الخيرات، فهو لغو على قولهم؛ لأنه قد شاء، وإذا لم يكن أيضاً يصير كأنه مأمور بالقول الكذب؛ لأنه أمر أن يقول كذا ولم يكن كذا، وإن كان شراً لا يشاء [ه]، فذكره عندهم لا معنى له، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The inference, and that is a type that does not necessitate coercion, and if it were permissible that knowledge of coercion exists in what is not in nature its possibility, it would negate knowledge of coercion in what is its way, thus nullifying direct knowledge. What was that obedience and compliance, and coercion eliminates all of that, so it becomes in effect as if He said: If He willed, He would prevent you from belief and from one religion, and this statement, and indeed He informed that if He willed He would have gathered you to guidance, and some believed by choice, and if it were coercion of the rest, He would not gather them, but He would prevent them from what they refused of His religion and obedience, and that is far and repugnant. And also that there is no act of creation in the place of coercion and domination, rather that returns to the faith of creation, and every substance by its creation is believing and guided, indeed by it is guidance of many of creation, so that He has willed and it was; the statement 'if He willed' has no meaning. And upon that is His saying: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them together.' And also among what invalidates the interpretation of compulsion is His saying: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me has been justified: I will surely fill Hell.' And the will of coercion does not nullify what He mentioned as truth, and there is no power except by Allah.

And Allah said: 'Whom Allah wills He sends astray, and whom He wills He places on a straight path.' And according to the Mu'tazila, He willed to make all upon that, and He, mighty and majestic, promised that what He willed would be so, but it was not. And He, mighty and majestic, said: 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow," except if Allah wills.' So this thing cannot be free from being among the good deeds, so it is nonsense according to their statement, because He has willed, and if it is not also, it becomes as if he is commanded to say a lie, because he is commanded to say so and it was not so. And if it is evil, He does not will it, so its mention according to them has no meaning. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 887

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili interpretation of divine will as coercion (jabr). Maturidi begins by discussing the nature of inference and coercion, arguing that if coercion were recognized in matters not naturally necessitated, it would negate direct knowledge. He then explains that if divine will were coercive, it would eliminate all obedience and compliance, making the statement 'if He willed' meaningless. He cites the Quranic verse 'ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا' to show that God's will does not compel faith; rather, some believe by choice, and if God had compelled the rest, He would not have gathered them in faith but would have prevented their disobedience. He also uses the verse 'ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها' to argue that the will of coercion cannot nullify the truth of His promise to fill Hell. Next, he addresses the verse 'من يشأ الله يضلله' and notes that the Mu'tazila claim God willed to guide all but failed. He then discusses the verse 'ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك غدا إلا أن يشاء الله', arguing that if the thing is good, the conditional 'except if Allah wills' is meaningless if God has already willed it; if it is evil, God does not will it, so the exception is also meaningless. Thus, he concludes that the Mu'tazili position leads to absurdity.

جبر
اختيار
هداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that the verse 'من يشأ الله يضلله' means God wills to guide everyone but fails.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the verse shows God wills both guidance and misguidance, and His promise of Hell confirms that some are not guided.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the problem with the Mu'tazili interpretation of the verse 'ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك غدا إلا أن يشاء الله' when the thing is good?
A) It commands lying
B) The exception is meaningless because God has already willed it
C) It implies God does not will good deeds
D) It contradicts the previous verse

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili interpretation of divine will as coercion leads to logical absurdities when applied to key Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'in sha Allah' about tomorrow's plans, do you genuinely feel your powerlessness and acknowledge that only Allah's will makes it happen? Or have you reduced it to a cultural habit? What change will you make today to truly mean it from your heart?
?
The verse 'Whom Allah wills He sends astray' reminds us that guidance is not by our own merit. Are you relying on your own efforts and knowledge to stay guided, or are you turning to Allah in humility? In what specific area of your life are you most prideful about your faith? Commit to one act of humility today.
?
The Mu'tazila tried to rationalize away God's absolute will to protect human free will. In your life, do you ever rationalize your sins by attributing them to qadr? Where are you blaming fate instead of taking responsibility? Make a firm resolve to own your choices and repent.

Prophetic Narratives Refuting Mu'tazili Interpretation of Will
Maturidi presents three Quranic proofs against Mu'tazili view on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quranic narratives about Nuh, Musa, and the destruction of cities to refute the Mu'tazili claim that God's will only encompasses guidance.

Core Idea
God's will is sovereign over both guidance and misguidance.

Imam Maturidi now turns to explicit Quranic evidence to counter the Mu'tazili reinterpretation of divine will. He cites three key passages: the verse about intending destruction before transgression occurs, the words of Prophet Nuh about God willing to lead astray, and the prayer of Prophet Musa about Pharaoh's wealth leading to misguidance. Each proof demonstrates that God's will encompasses all that occurs, challenging the Mu'tazili restriction of His will to good alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the three Quranic proofs
Read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the three distinct proofs: the destruction verse, Nuh's statement, and Musa's prayer.
Pay attention to the flow of Maturidi's argument as he moves from one proof to the next.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili position refuted
For each proof, identify what the Mu'tazila claim (e.g., that God does not will misguidance or that He only wills obedience). Maturidi explicitly counters these misinterpretations.
The Mu'tazili view is that God's will is limited to good and guidance.
3
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
Consider how the three proofs together establish that God's will includes all events, whether believers see them as good or evil. This is a cornerstone of Maturidi theology.
This lesson connects to the broader doctrine of qadar (divine decree).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال تعالى: "وَإِذَا أَرَدْنَا أَنْ نُهْلِكَ قَرْيَةً أَمَرْنَا مُتْرَفِيهَا فَفَسَقُوا فِيهَا"، أخبر أنه يريد إهلاك قوم وقرية بفسق أهلها قبل أن يكون منهم الفسق، فلو لم [؟] كما علم أن يكون، ولكن أراد أن تكون الطاعة ويهلكهم، كان يرد كون الفسق منهم يكون ذلك جوراً، فثبت أنه أراد الذي كان منهم أو علم ذلك. وقال نوح لقومه: "وَلَا يَنْفَعُكُمْ نُصْحِي إِنْ أَرَدْتُ أَنْ أَنْصَحَ لَكُمْ إِنْ كَانَ اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ أَنْ يُغْوِيَكُمْ"، وقلت: لا يريد ذلك، وصرفت كلام نوح إلى ما لا يحتمله وهم البشر، وَقَالَ مُوسَى: "رَبَّنَا إِنَّكَ آتَيْتَ فِرْعَوْنَ وَمَلَأَهُ زِينَةً وَأَمْوَالًا فِي الْحَيَاةِ الدُّنْيَا رَبَّنَا لِيَضِلُّوا عَنْ سَبِيلِك"، وأنتم تقولون لم يؤتهم لذلك ولكن آتاهم ليهتدوا. وقال تعالى:
And He, exalted, said: 'And when We intend to destroy a city, We command its affluent ones, and they commit transgression therein.' He informed that He intends to destroy a people and a city by the transgression of its inhabitants before transgression occurs from them. So if He did not [؟] as He knew it would be, but He intended that obedience be and He destroys them, [then] would the occurrence of transgression from them be considered injustice? So it is established that He intended what occurred from them or He knew that. And Nuh said to his people: 'And my advice will not benefit you if I intend to advise you if Allah intends to lead you astray.' And you (Mu'tazila) say: He does not will that, and you turned Nuh's speech to what it cannot bear of human fancy. And Musa said: 'Our Lord, indeed You have given Pharaoh and his establishment adornment and wealth in the worldly life, our Lord, that they may mislead from Your way.' And you (Mu'tazila) say: He did not give them that for that purpose, but He gave them that they might be guided. And He, exalted, said: [incomplete].
Source pages p. 888

Commentary
This passage uses Quranic narratives to refute the Mu'tazili interpretation of divine will. Maturidi first cites the verse 'وإذا أردنا أن نهلك قرية' to show that God intends destruction due to transgression that will occur. He argues that if God intended obedience and then destroyed them, it would be unjust; thus God's will encompasses what occurs, whether good or evil. Then he cites Prophet Nuh's statement to his people: 'إن كان الله يريد أن يغويكم' (if Allah intends to lead you astray). The Mu'tazila interpret this as saying God does not will misguidance, but Maturidi says they twist Nuh's words beyond their apparent meaning. Finally, he cites Prophet Musa's prayer that Pharaoh's wealth leads people astray, while the Mu'tazila claim God gave that wealth for guidance. Thus, Maturidi demonstrates that the Quranic evidence supports the view that God's will includes both guidance and misguidance.

Key Terms
إرادة
ضلال
هلاك
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God's will only encompasses guidance and goodness, and that He never wills misguidance or evil.
Correction: Imam Maturidi refutes this by citing Quranic verses where God explicitly states He intends destruction (based on transgression), Nuh mentions God's will to lead astray, and Musa prays for wealth to cause misguidance. These show that God's will is absolute and includes all occurrences.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Quranic evidence from the prophets demonstrates that God's will is not limited to guidance but encompasses all that occurs, including misguidance and destruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your guidance is entirely from God, or do you secretly attribute it to your own intelligence or effort? How would your humility change if you fully internalized that your faith is a gift of His will?
?
When you encounter people who are misguided or rebellious, do you feel anger or compassion? How does knowing that God's will includes their misguidance affect your attitude toward them?
?
Are you willing to submit to God's will even when it brings hardship or seems unjust, trusting that His will is sovereign and good? What specific area of your life do you struggle to surrender to His decree?

The Dilemma of God's Will and His Promise
Maturidi presents a rational dilemma to refute denial of God's universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that forces the opponent to either affirm God's universal will or accuse Him of lying.

Core Idea
Either God intends to fulfill His promise or to break it—no third option.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful dialectical argument against those who deny that God's will encompasses all things. He forces them into a choice: either God intends to fulfill His promises and threats, or He intends to break them. The first leads to affirming His justice, the second to accusing Him of lying—an obvious absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' claim
Read the opening where Maturidi cites Quranic verses showing God's will over specific outcomes, and the opponents deny that God willed them.
Notice how Maturidi uses the Quran to establish that God's will is mentioned in the text.
2
Understand the dilemma
Maturidi presents a choice: either God intends to fulfill His promise or to break it. Work through each option and its consequences.
The second option implies God intends lying, which is absurd.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
See how the only consistent position is that God's will encompasses all that occurs, including the fulfillment of His threats.
This conclusion is supported by the verse that God does not intend injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يُطَهِّرَ قُلُوبَهُم، وأنتم تقولون: بل أراد الله ذلك وقال: وَمَنْ يُرِدْ اللَّهُ فِتْنَتَهُ، وأنتم تقولون: لم يردها، أو تقولون هذه شحنة وأنى كان رسول الله يريد أو يتمنى أن لا يكون حتى يقال له: فَلَنْ تَمْلِكَ لَهُ مِنَ اللهِ شَيْئًا. وقال الله تعالى: وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُم خَيْرٌ لأنفُسِهِم. وقال تعالى: وَلَا تُعْجِبْكَ أَمْوَالُهُم وَأَوْلَادُهُمْ. أخبر ما يريد بما أعطى، وهم يقولون: لا يريد. فما يقال لأمثال هؤلاء إلا ما قيل لليهود والنصارى: أَأَنْتُمْ أَعْلَمُ أَمْ اللَّهُ. وقال الله جل ثناؤه: الأَمْلان حقب الجنَّةِ والنَّاسِ أَجْمَعِينَ، فنقول لهم: أراد الله عز وجل أن يفي بما وعد هذا، مهم و جهنم من أو أراد أن يكذب وعده ويبطل وعيده. فإن قالوا بالثانى أعظموا القول ووصفوه بإرادة السفه والكذب، وكفى بهذا القول خزيا، وإن قالوا: أراد أن يفى به قيل: أراد أن يفى به، وهو يريد أن يطيعوه، فيفى وهم يطيعون له أو يعصون فإن قال بالأول فهو جورٌ أراد؛ لأن فعله جور، فإرادة كونه إرادة فعل الجور أن يكون له فعلا. وقال الله تعالى: وَمَا اللهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلعِبَادِ، وإن قال بالثاني أذعنوا للحق، وقالوا بالعدل، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال الله تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللهُ أَلَّا يَجْعَلَ لَهُمْ حَظًّا فِي الآخِرَةِ، ومن أراد أن يكون منه كل خير فقد أراد له الحظ في الآخرة. وقال تعالى: تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا والله يُرِيدُ الآخِرَةَ، وقال تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ أَنْ يُخَفِّفَ عَنْكُمْ، فأراد للمؤمنين هذا فكان ذلك، وللكافرين الأول فكان ذلك، ولا يجوز أن الأول وهم مطيعون، ثبت أنه أراد أن يكون منهم ما قد كان، وبالله العصمة. وقال الله تعالى: وَمَا تَشَاءُون إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ الله، فإذا كانوا لا يشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله لا يجوز إذا شاء الله أن لا يشاءوا، ويشاءون وإن لم يشأ، فإن ذلك آية الكذب الذي هو قبح الله في العقول، وبالله المعونة والتوفيق. ثم قول المسلمين المتوارث بينهم: ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لا يكون، على غير اضطراب قلب لأحد أو توهم غير، وعلى غير سبق الوهم إلى خلاف لما عليه المشيئة المعروفة التي لديها يقع فعل الاختيار والاضطرار جميعا. على أنه لو جاز أن يشاء شيئا لا يكون، ويشاء أن لا يكون فيكون، لم يكن الكون بالأول أن يكون من صفات الربوبية من الثاني، ولكل موضع، بل لو قيل: إن ذا يغلب على الأول عندهم، لم يبعد، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا تعارفهم فى الوعد إذا حذروا الخلف إن شاء الله، وفى اليمين إذا خافوا الحنث قالوا ذلك. ثبت أن عقد جملة المسلمين واحد قبل تمويه المعتزلة، وهو / كمال قال: كل مولود يولد على الفطرة إلا أن أبويه يهودانه وينصرانه ويمجسانه بين الخلقة، يوجب شهادة ودلالة وحدانية الله تعالى حتى يجى التلبيس ممن وذكر، وكذلك امر المشيئة عند الجميع قبل تلبيس المعتزلة، ولا قوة الا بالله. وأيضا الموجود في عرف الخلق من الدعاء بإرادة اليسر والخير لهم على طمأنينة القلب بحقيقة ذلك لو كانت ثمة ارادة، ولا قوة الا بالله.
Those are the ones for whom God did not intend to purify their hearts, and you say: Rather God intended that, and He said: And whomever God intends to try, and you say: He did not intend it, or you say this is a burden and how could the Messenger of God intend or wish that it not be until it was said to him: Then you will not possess for him against God anything. And God Almighty said: And let not those who disbelieve think that Our prolonging of their lives is good for themselves. And He said: And let not their wealth and children impress you. He informed what He intends by what He gave, and they say: He does not intend. So nothing is said to such people except what was said to the Jews and Christians: Are you more knowing or God? And God, exalted is His mention, said: The promise of Paradise and of all people, so we say to them: God Almighty intended to fulfill what He promised this, important, and Hell from, or He intended to break His promise and nullify His threat. So if they say the second, they magnify the statement and describe Him with intending foolishness and lying, and sufficient is this statement as disgrace. And if they say: He intended to fulfill it, it is said: He intended to fulfill it, and He intends that they obey Him, so He fulfills while they obey Him or disobey. So if he says the first, it is injustice that He intended, because His act is injustice, so the intention of its being is intending the act of injustice to be His act. And God Almighty said: And God does not intend injustice for the servants. And if he says the second, they submit to the truth, and say with justice, and there is no power except with God. And God Almighty said: God intends not to give them a share in the Hereafter, and whoever intends that all good be from him has intended for him a share in the Hereafter. And He said: You desire the goods of this world, but God desires the Hereafter. And He said: God intends to lighten your burden. So He intended for the believers this, and that happened, and for the disbelievers the first, and that happened, and it is not permissible that the first while they are obedient, it is established that He intended that from them what had already been, and with God is protection. And God Almighty said: And you do not will except that God wills. So if they do not will except that God wills, it is not permissible, when God wills that they do not will, and they will even if He did not will, for that is a sign of lying which is ugliness from God in the intellects, and with God is assistance and success. Then the transmitted saying of the Muslims among themselves: Whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not, without disturbance of heart for anyone or other illusion, and without the mind preceding to contrary of what the known will is upon which occur both the act of choice and compulsion. For if it were permissible that He will something and it not be, and will that something not be and it be, then the existence of the first would not be more deserving of being from the attributes of lordship than the second, and each has a place. Rather, if it were said: This overcomes the first according to them, it would not be far-fetched, and there is no power except with God. And also their custom in promises when they fear breaking them saying 'if God wills', and in oaths when they fear perjury they say that. It is established that the consensus of all Muslims is one before the obfuscation of the Mu'tazila, and it is / Kamal said: Every child is born upon the fitra except that his parents make him a Jew, Christian, or Magian, necessitates testimony and evidence of the oneness of God Almighty until the confusion comes from whom was mentioned, and likewise the matter of the will is with all before the obfuscation of the Mu'tazila, and there is no power except with God. And also the existing in the custom of creation of supplication for ease and good for them with tranquility of heart regarding the reality of that if there were will, and there is no power except with God.

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of those who deny that God's will is universal, particularly the Mu'tazila. The author (Imam Maturidi) begins by citing Quranic verses that affirm God's will over specific outcomes (e.g., refraining from purifying hearts, intending trial, promising Paradise and Hell). He then presents the opponents' view: they claim God does not will such things. Maturidi forces a dilemma: either God intends to fulfill His promises and threats, or He intends to break them. To claim He intends to break them is to accuse Him of foolishness and lying—an absurdity. If they claim He intends to fulfill them, then He also intends that people obey, yet people sometimes disobey; but the fulfillment occurs regardless. If they say God intends injustice (by punishing), that contradicts the verse 'God does not intend injustice for the servants.' So the only consistent position is to affirm that God's will encompasses all that occurs. Maturidi then cites more verses showing that God wills specific outcomes for believers and disbelievers, and that human will is subordinate to God's will. He emphasize the universal Muslim maxim 'Whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not,' which applies to both voluntary and involuntary acts. He argues that if God could will something that does not happen, then the will would not be a necessary attribute of lordship. He further supports this by pointing to common practices: people say 'if God wills' when promising or swearing, showing their belief that outcomes depend on God's will. He concludes that before the Mu'tazila's obfuscation, the consensus of Muslims was firm on this, just as it was on the hadith of the fitra. Thus, Maturidi uses scriptural, rational, and communal arguments to affirm the universality of divine will.

Misconception: Some think that God does not will to punish disbelievers or fulfill His threats.
Correction: Maturidi shows that denying this leads to attributing lying to God, which is absurd. God's will includes the fulfillment of His promises and threats.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what is the logical consequence of denying that God wills to fulfill His promise of punishment?

D) Humans have independent will.

Key Takeaway
God's will is universal; to deny it leads to the absurd conclusion that God intends to lie.

The Sunni Consensus on God's Universal Will
Maturidi uses consensus, custom, and fitra to affirm God's universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal Muslim maxim 'Whatever God wills is' and how it is supported by the fitra and communal practice.

Core Idea
The Muslim consensus before the Mu'tazila held that God's will covers all that occurs.

After presenting the rational dilemma, Imam Maturidi turns to the transmitted consensus of the Muslim community. He cites the well-known maxim 'Whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not,' and argues that this has been the understanding of all Muslims before the Mu'tazila introduced confusion. He also uses the practice of saying 'if God wills' and the hadith of the fitra to reinforce the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim and its implications
Read the passage where Maturidi states the universal Muslim maxim. Note that it applies to both voluntary and involuntary acts.
The maxim is 'ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لا يكون'.
2
Examine the practical evidence
Maturidi points to the custom of saying 'if God wills' in promises and oaths. See how this reflects belief that outcomes depend on God's will.
Even opponents used this phrase, showing common acceptance.
3
Understand the fitra argument
The hadith 'Every child is born upon the fitra' is used to show that belief in God's oneness and universal will is innate, only corrupted by later influences.
This connects to the consensus before Mu'tazila obfuscation.

Those are the ones for whom God did not intend to purify their hearts, and you say: Rather God intended that, and He said: And whomever God intends to try, and you say: He did not intend it, or you say this is a burden and how could the Messenger of God intend or wish that it not be until it was said to him: Then you will not possess for him against God anything. And if they say: He intended to fulfill it, it is said: He intended to fulfill it, and He intends that they obey Him, so He fulfills while they obey Him or disobey. So He intended for the believers this, and that happened, and for the disbelievers the first, and that happened, and it is not permissible that the first while they are obedient, it is established that He intended that from them what had already been, and with God is protection. And God Almighty said: And you do not will except that God wills. So if they do not will except that God wills, it is not permissible, when God wills that they do not will, and they will even if He did not will, for that is a sign of lying which is ugliness from God in the intellects, and with God is assistance and success. Then the transmitted saying of the Muslims among themselves: Whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not, without disturbance of heart for anyone or other illusion, and without the mind preceding to contrary of what the known will is upon which occur both the act of choice and compulsion. For if it were permissible that He will something and it not be, and will that something not be and it be, then the existence of the first would not be more deserving of being from the attributes of lordship than the second, and each has a place. Rather, if it were said: This overcomes the first according to them, it would not be far-fetched, and there is no power except with God. And also their custom in promises when they fear breaking them saying 'if God wills', and in oaths when they fear perjury they say that. And also the existing in the custom of creation of supplication for ease and good for them with tranquility of heart regarding the reality of that if there were will, and there is no power except with God.

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of those who deny that God's will is universal, particularly the Mu'tazila. The author (Imam Maturidi) begins by citing Quranic verses that affirm God's will over specific outcomes (e.g., refraining from purifying hearts, intending trial, promising Paradise and Hell). He then presents the opponents' view: they claim God does not will such things. Maturidi forces a dilemma: either God intends to fulfill His promises and threats, or He intends to break them. To claim He intends to break them is to accuse Him of foolishness and lying—an absurdity. If they claim He intends to fulfill them, then He also intends that people obey, yet people sometimes disobey; but the fulfillment occurs regardless. If they say God intends injustice (by punishing), that contradicts the verse 'God does not intend injustice for the servants.' So the only consistent position is to affirm that God's will encompasses all that occurs. Maturidi then cites more verses showing that God wills specific outcomes for believers and disbelievers, and that human will is subordinate to God's will. He emphasize the universal Muslim maxim 'Whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not,' which applies to both voluntary and involuntary acts. He argues that if God could will something that does not happen, then the will would not be a necessary attribute of lordship. He further supports this by pointing to common practices: people say 'if God wills' when promising or swearing, showing their belief that outcomes depend on God's will. He concludes that before the Mu'tazila's obfuscation, the consensus of Muslims was firm on this, just as it was on the hadith of the fitra. Thus, Maturidi uses scriptural, rational, and communal arguments to affirm the universality of divine will.

God's Will Free from Weakness
First argument: analogy from the witnessed world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God's will cannot involve weakness, using the analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
God's will is not like human wishful thinking; it is free from weakness.

This lesson examines Maturidi's first argument that God cannot will friendship with those who choose enmity against Him, as such a will would imply weakness. Maturidi uses an observation from human experience to establish a principle that applies to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
The phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' is a closing reminder.
2
Identify the analogy from the witnessed world
Maturidi observes that in human experience, someone who wants to befriend an enemy does so out of weakness or senility. This is the premise.
3
Apply the analogy to God
Since God cannot have weakness, He cannot will friendship with Iblis and those who choose enmity against Him. This is the conclusion.
The conclusion is drawn directly from the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الذي عليه أمر الشاهد أن كل من أراد موالاة من يختار عداوته، كان يكون عن ضعف وخرف، فلا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى يريد مولاة إبليس والذين اختاروا عداوته، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا أن شرط كل من فعله اختيار الإرادة، وكل من فعله الاضطرار أنه غير مريد لذلك، فلو كان الله لفعل العبد غير مريد ليكون على ما كان، يكون مضطرا؛ ولذلك لا يجوز أن يكون لأحد في فعل غيره إرادة؛ لما لا يحتمل خروجه على ما يريد، وسموا ذلك تمنيا، فعلى ذلك لو توهم كون شيء لم يرده الله كانت إرادته تخرج مخرج التمنى. وأيضا أنه لو جعل لنا أن نبوة نبي يقول، يكون ذلك معصية لنا أن نريده، من حيث يكون آية، وإن لم يكن أن يعصى، فمثله إذ علم الله أنه أخبر عن ذلك. وأنه أن لا يكون، كان له أن لا يريد في الحكمة، على أنه لا يختلف أن ليس للعبد أن يسأل الله هداية بشر يعلم علم من يعلم أنه لا يهتدى نحو إبليس لم يكن له أن يقول: اللهم اهده؛ لما يعلم أنه لا يكون، ثم محال أن يكون علينا إرادة ذلك، وإذ لم يكن علينا إرادة ما أنه لا يكون لم يجز أن يقال ذلك على الله؛ إذ كونها علينا إنما يكون إذا جهلنا بحاله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also that what is the case in the witnessed world is that whoever wants to befriend one who chooses enmity against him, he would be due to weakness and senility; so it is not permissible that Allah, exalted, wills the friendship of Iblis and those who chose enmity against Him. And there is no power except with Allah. And also that the condition of everyone who does an act by choice is will, and everyone who does it by compulsion is not willing it; so if Allah were to cause the servant to act without willing it, in order that it be as it was, He would be compelled; and therefore it is not permissible for anyone to have will in the action of another, because it is not possible for it to occur contrary to what he wills. And they called that wishfulness (tamannī). So accordingly, if one supposes the existence of something that Allah did not will, His will would come out as wishfulness. And also that if it were made for us that the prophecy of a prophet says, that would be a disobedience for us to will it, inasmuch as it is a sign, even if it is not that one disobeys. So the like of it is when Allah knows that He informed about that. And that it does not occur, He would have [the right] not to will it in wisdom, upon that it is not disputed that the servant cannot ask Allah for guidance for a person whom he knows that he knows that he will not be guided, like Iblis; he should not say: O Allah, guide him, because he knows that it will not be. Then it is impossible for us to will that. And since there is no will upon us for what does not occur, it is not permissible to say that about Allah; because its being upon us only occurs when we are ignorant of his state. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 892

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments establishing that God's will (irādah / mashī'a) is not like human will. The first argument uses the witnessed state: anyone who seeks friendship with an enemy is weak, so God—free from weakness—cannot will friendship with Iblis and his followers. The second argument distinguishes between voluntary and compulsory action: if God forced an act without willing it, He would be compelled, which is impossible. Thus, God's will must be efficacious; if something does not occur, it cannot be that He truly willed it, or else His will would be mere wishful thinking (tamannī). The third argument applies this to prophecy: if God informed about something that does not happen, then His will regarding that would be meaningless. Similarly, the servant cannot pray for guidance for someone known to be misguided (like Iblis) because it is known that God will not guide him. Therefore, it is impossible to attribute to God a will for something that does not occur. The overall conclusion is that God's will is always effective and never reflects weakness or wishful thinking.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will can be ineffective like human wishes.
Correction: This passage shows that such a will would imply weakness, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first argument, why cannot God will the friendship of Iblis?
A) Because Iblis is not worthy.
B) Because it would imply weakness and senility.
C) Because God only wills good.
D) Because Iblis is a creation.

Key Takeaway
God's will is never tainted by weakness or senility, as shown by the analogy from human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider an area in your life where you have been 'wishing' for something good but not acting on it, perhaps because you feel weak or uncertain. How does the truth that God's will is never weak or senile call you to stop using human weakness as an excuse? What concrete step can you take today to rely on His will instead of your own hesitation?

God's Will Cannot Be Compelled or Wishful
Second argument: compulsion and wishful thinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's will is always effective and cannot be mere wishful thinking or caused by compulsion.

Core Idea
God's will is always effective; if it were not, it would be wishful thinking or compulsion.

This lesson covers Maturidi's second argument, which distinguishes between voluntary and compulsory action. If God forced an act without willing it, He would be compelled, which is impossible. Moreover, if something does not occur, God's will cannot be for it, or else it would be mere wishful thinking (tamannī).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the terms for voluntary and compulsory action.
2
Understand the condition of voluntary vs. compulsory action
Maturidi states that a voluntary act requires will, while a compulsory act lacks will. If God made an act without will, He would be compelled.
This is a logical implication.
3
Apply to God's will being ineffective
If something does not occur, it cannot be that God truly willed it, because then His will would have been wishful thinking (tamannī).
Tamannī is wishful thinking without effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الذي عليه أمر الشاهد أن كل من أراد موالاة من يختار عداوته، كان يكون عن ضعف وخرف، فلا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى يريد مولاة إبليس والذين اختاروا عداوته، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا أن شرط كل من فعله اختيار الإرادة، وكل من فعله الاضطرار أنه غير مريد لذلك، فلو كان الله لفعل العبد غير مريد ليكون على ما كان، يكون مضطرا؛ ولذلك لا يجوز أن يكون لأحد في فعل غيره إرادة؛ لما لا يحتمل خروجه على ما يريد، وسموا ذلك تمنيا، فعلى ذلك لو توهم كون شيء لم يرده الله كانت إرادته تخرج مخرج التمنى. وأيضا أنه لو جعل لنا أن نبوة نبي يقول، يكون ذلك معصية لنا أن نريده، من حيث يكون آية، وإن لم يكن أن يعصى، فمثله إذ علم الله أنه أخبر عن ذلك. وأنه أن لا يكون، كان له أن لا يريد في الحكمة، على أنه لا يختلف أن ليس للعبد أن يسأل الله هداية بشر يعلم علم من يعلم أنه لا يهتدى نحو إبليس لم يكن له أن يقول: اللهم اهده؛ لما يعلم أنه لا يكون، ثم محال أن يكون علينا إرادة ذلك، وإذ لم يكن علينا إرادة ما أنه لا يكون لم يجز أن يقال ذلك على الله؛ إذ كونها علينا إنما يكون إذا جهلنا بحاله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also that what is the case in the witnessed world is that whoever wants to befriend one who chooses enmity against him, he would be due to weakness and senility; so it is not permissible that Allah, exalted, wills the friendship of Iblis and those who chose enmity against Him. And there is no power except with Allah. And also that the condition of everyone who does an act by choice is will, and everyone who does it by compulsion is not willing it; so if Allah were to cause the servant to act without willing it, in order that it be as it was, He would be compelled; and therefore it is not permissible for anyone to have will in the action of another, because it is not possible for it to occur contrary to what he wills. And they called that wishfulness (tamannī). So accordingly, if one supposes the existence of something that Allah did not will, His will would come out as wishfulness. And also that if it were made for us that the prophecy of a prophet says, that would be a disobedience for us to will it, inasmuch as it is a sign, even if it is not that one disobeys. So the like of it is when Allah knows that He informed about that. And that it does not occur, He would have [the right] not to will it in wisdom, upon that it is not disputed that the servant cannot ask Allah for guidance for a person whom he knows that he knows that he will not be guided, like Iblis; he should not say: O Allah, guide him, because he knows that it will not be. Then it is impossible for us to will that. And since there is no will upon us for what does not occur, it is not permissible to say that about Allah; because its being upon us only occurs when we are ignorant of his state. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 892

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments establishing that God's will (irādah / mashī'a) is not like human will. The first argument uses the witnessed state: anyone who seeks friendship with an enemy is weak, so God—free from weakness—cannot will friendship with Iblis and his followers. The second argument distinguishes between voluntary and compulsory action: if God forced an act without willing it, He would be compelled, which is impossible. Thus, God's will must be efficacious; if something does not occur, it cannot be that He truly willed it, or else His will would be mere wishful thinking (tamannī). The third argument applies this to prophecy: if God informed about something that does not happen, then His will regarding that would be meaningless. Similarly, the servant cannot pray for guidance for someone known to be misguided (like Iblis) because it is known that God will not guide him. Therefore, it is impossible to attribute to God a will for something that does not occur. The overall conclusion is that God's will is always effective and never reflects weakness or wishful thinking.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الاضطرار
التمني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will can be ineffective like human wishes.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if something does not occur, God cannot have willed it, otherwise His will would be wishful thinking.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi call an ineffective will?
A) Irada
B) Tamannī
C) Ijtihad
D) Qada

Key Takeaway
God's will is always effective; if it were not, it would be wishful thinking, which cannot be attributed to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a wish, do you realize that your will is not effective like Allah's? How should this reality change your reliance on Allah rather than on your own plans?
?
Do you ever think that something happens contrary to Allah's will? How does this passage correct your perspective?
?
If Allah's will is always effective, what does that imply about your own desires? Are you content with whatever Allah wills, or do you sometimes wish otherwise? How will you train your heart to submit to His will?

Prophecy and Prayer: God's Will Is Not for the Impossible
Third argument: prophecy and prayer show God's will is only for what occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's will cannot be for things that are known not to occur, as illustrated by prophecy and prayer for guidance.

Core Idea
God's will only applies to what actually occurs; it cannot be for what is known not to happen.

This lesson covers Maturidi's third argument, which uses the examples of prophecy and prayer. If God informs that something will not happen, then His will cannot be for it. Similarly, the servant cannot pray for guidance for someone known to be misguided, because it is impossible for that to occur. This reinforces that God's will is always aligned with reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the text carefully, noting the examples of prophecy and prayer.
2
Understand the prophecy example
If God tells a prophet something that does not happen, then it would be disobedience to will it. But God knows it will not happen, so He does not will it.
This is because God's will is based on knowledge.
3
Understand the prayer example
The servant cannot pray for guidance for someone like Iblis, because he knows it will not happen. Therefore, it is impossible for the servant to will it, and even more so for God.
This shows that will for the impossible is not valid.

The Dilemma of Willing Insult: A Maturidi Argument
Maturidi constructs a rhetorical dilemma about willing insult to show moral relativity based on intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a rhetorical question about insulting the Prophet to expose the flaw in the Mu'tazila equation of will with creation.

Core Idea
Willing an act can be praiseworthy from one aspect and blameworthy from another.

This passage presents a clever rhetorical argument. Maturidi asks about someone who wants the insult of the Prophet to be as severe as the insult of Iblis—that person would be a transgressor. But if someone wants the insult to be a great matter, that person is praiseworthy. The dilemma shows that the same act of willing can have different moral valuations depending on intention, undermining the Mu'tazila view that will is simply identical to the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the rhetorical question
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the opening question. Notice the comparison between insulting the Prophet and insulting Iblis.
This sets up a moral dichotomy.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Find the two hypothetical cases: wanting the insult to be equal to Iblis (blameworthy) and wanting it to be incomparable (praiseworthy).
This creates a logical trap.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that one can will an act of disbelief from an aspect that does not bear blame. Grasp how this refutes the Mu'tazila.
The key is that intent matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نسأل عمن يريد أن يكون شتم رسول الله مثل شتم إبليس في المرتبة والمأتم، أليس هو بمتعد سفيه كافر؟ لا بد من: بلى. فيقال: من أراد أن يكون شتم رسول الله أمرا عظيما لا يبلغه شتم أحد من الخلق أن يكون محمودا؟ لا بد من: بلى. قيل: فمن يريد أن يكون الشتم منه كذلك؛ إذ محال كونه أحد أعظم ولا بأصغر ولا نحو ذلك، فلا بد أن يقول: مين كافر، وفي ذلك جواز إرادة فعل الكفر من وجه لا يحتمل الذم من ذلك الوجه، وبالله التوفيق. ثم الأصل الذي هو معتمد المعتزلة أن إرادة الله ليست غير خلقه وأن تأويلها على ما فسر الكعبي ليس غير أنه لم يغلب ولم يضطر في فعله، وهذا المعنى قد أعطوه جميعا في فعل العباد، فإنكارهم الإرادة وهذا معناها بعد الإعطاء في الجملة، والله الموفق. لا من لا معنى له.
Then we ask: If someone wants that the insult of the Messenger of Allah be like the insult of Iblis in rank and [occasion of] mourning, is he not transgressing, foolish, disbelieving? It is unavoidable: Yes. Then it is said: Whoever wants that the insult of the Messenger of Allah be a great matter that the insult of any of creation does not reach, is he not praiseworthy? It is unavoidable: Yes. Then it is said: So whoever wants the insult from him to be like that? Since it is impossible that it is greater or smaller or the like, he must say: [he is] a disbeliever? And in that is the permissibility of willing the act of disbelief from an aspect that does not bear blame from that aspect. And with Allah is success. Then the principle upon which the Mu'tazila rely is that the will of Allah is not other than His creation, and its interpretation according to what al-Ka'bī explained is nothing other than that He was not overcome nor compelled in His action. And they have all given this meaning in the actions of servants. So their denial of will after granting its meaning in general? And Allah is the grantor of success. No, for that has no meaning.
Source pages p. 892

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position on divine will. Maturidi begins with a rhetorical question: if someone wants the insult of the Prophet to be as severe as Iblis's insult (in rank and mourning), that person would be a transgressing, foolish disbeliever. Conversely, if someone wants the insult to be a great matter that no other insult reaches, he is praiseworthy. The dilemma is that if one wills the same act of insult, it must be either greater, smaller, or equal—impossible, so the only conclusion is that such a person is a disbeliever. This shows that willing an act of disbelief can be blameworthy from one aspect but not from another. Maturidi then states the Mu'tazila principle: God's will is nothing other than His creation, and al-Ka'bī interprets it as 'not being overcome or compelled' in action. The Mu'tazila have granted this meaning for human actions, yet they deny a distinct attribute of will for God. Maturidi rejects this as meaningless ('No, for that has no meaning').

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإرادة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that will is identical to the act, so any act of willing disbelief is inherently blameworthy.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same act of willing can be praiseworthy or blameworthy depending on the intention behind it, thus will is not reducible to act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is willing the Prophet's insult to be equal to Iblis's insult blameworthy?
A) Because the act of insult is inherently bad.
B) Because the intention is to equate the Prophet with Iblis.
C) Because it is impossible.
D) Because the Prophet is greater.

Key Takeaway
Willing an act can be praiseworthy from one aspect and blameworthy from another, undermining the Mu'tazila equation of will with creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your own actions, do you consider the intention behind them as seriously as the outward act? What would it change if you examined every intention as Maturidi does in this passage?
?
Have you ever condemned someone’s action without understanding their intention, only to later realize your judgment was hasty? How does this lesson challenge you to pause before judging others—or yourself?
?
If willing an act of disbelief can be permissible from one aspect, what does that mean for your struggle with sins you commit? Can you separate the act from the intention and find aspects that are not blameworthy? Repent, but also reflect: where is your heart truly directed?

The Mu'tazila Principle: Will as Non-Compulsion
Maturidi exposes the Mu'tazila principle and its internal inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view that God's will is nothing but His creation, and Maturidi's critique that this reduces will to a meaningless concept.

Core Idea
If will is identical to creation, then 'will' loses its distinct meaning.

After the rhetorical argument, Maturidi states the foundational principle of the Mu'tazila: God's will is not other than His creation. He cites al-Ka'bī's interpretation that will simply means not being overcome or compelled. Maturidi then points out that the Mu'tazila themselves grant this meaning for human actions. Yet they deny a distinct attribute of will for God. This inconsistency, Maturidi argues, renders their denial meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and interpretation
Focus on the sentence stating the Mu'tazila principle and al-Ka'bī's interpretation. Understand that they equate will with non-compulsion.
Al-Ka'bī was a leading Mu'tazili scholar.
2
Identify the inconsistency
Notice that Maturidi says the Mu'tazila have granted this meaning (non-compulsion) for human actions, yet they deny a distinct divine will.
This is a contradiction.
3
Grasp Maturidi's conclusion
Maturidi ends with a dismissive 'No, for that has no meaning.' Understand why he finds the Mu'tazila position incoherent.
If will is just non-compulsion, then it is not a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المعتزلة
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe divine will is a distinct attribute.
Correction: They equate will with creation, reducing it to a non-attribute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bī's interpretation, what does God's will mean?
A) A distinct eternal attribute.
B) Not being overcome or compelled in action.
C) The act of creation itself.
D) A created thing.

The Mu'tazila reduce divine will to non-compulsion, but then deny any distinct attribute, leading to a meaningless position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced God's will to a mere absence of force, thinking it passive rather than an active, distinct attribute? When you try to reconcile trials with God's goodness, do you subconsciously treat His will as just 'not being compelled' rather than an intentional, creative act? Commit today to affirming that God's will is real, prior, and active in every detail of your life, especially the ones you cannot explain.
?
You likely accept that your own choices are not forced—do you extend that same meaning of 'will' to God but then refuse to acknowledge it as a real attribute? Examine your heart: have you ever been comfortable with a theology that denies something to God that you take for granted in yourself? Pledge to embrace God's attributes as He has described them, without reduction.
?
When you read the phrase 'And Allah is the grantor of success,' Maturidi ends with that. Do you truly rely on God's will for your success, or do you functionally live as though your own will is the ultimate cause? Reflect on one decision this week where you acted as if your will alone sufficed. Repent and actively attribute every outcome to His distinct, creative will.

Maturidi's Two Responses on God's Will for Disbelief
Maturidi presents two ways to answer about God's will for disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two ways to respond when asked about God's will for the acts of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Context matters: affirm God's will absolutely unless the questioner is obstinate.

When asked why God wills disbelief, a theologian must consider the questioner's intent. Maturidi outlines two approaches: one for sincere inquiry and one for those seeking argument. This lesson presents those two aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two aspects Maturidi mentions.
The passage uses the word 'وجهان' to signal the two aspects.
2
Identify the two aspects
List the two ways Maturidi describes. Note the conditions for each: absolute affirmation vs. absolute response if the questioner's intent is unclear or malicious.
The second aspect includes a caution: 'وهو منع أن منفي' (it is a prohibition that it be negated).
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why a theologian might withhold a direct answer. What does this teach about handling sensitive theological questions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل عندنا إذا سئلنا عن مشيئة الله فعل الكفرة على ما كان وجهان: أحدهما القول بذلك في الإطلاق على ما عرف من الإرادة في ذلك، والثاني الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي أن يريد التعنت في ذلك، وهو منع أن منفي يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيها يتعارف، أحدها التمني، وذلك عن الله في كل شيء، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، فذلك منفي عن الله في كل فعل يذم فاعله، والثالث الرضا به والقبول له، وذلك كذلك أيضا في كل فعل يذم عليه، والرابع تأويله نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده، وهذا يقول ذلك، وقد أجمع على معناه، فمن أنكر ذلك بعد إعطاء معناه فهو قدر المشيئة على غير حقيقة المراد منها، وهو عندنا لازم؛ إذ هو لكل شيء خالق، وقد ثبت وصفه في ما يخلقه أنه غير مضطر إليه ولا يكره عليه، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the principle with us, if we are asked about the will of Allah regarding the action of the disbelievers as it occurs, is two aspects: One of them is to say that in the absolute sense, according to what is known of will in that regard. And the second is to be absolute if the intent of the questioner is not understood, or one fears that he intends obstinacy in that. And it is a prohibition that it be negated. It is said that will (mashī'a) has meanings that are known: one of them is wishfulness (tamannī), and that is negated from Allah in everything. The second is command and calling to it, and that is negated from Allah in every action that is blameworthy for its doer. The third is being pleased with it and accepting it, and that is likewise also in every blameworthy action. The fourth is its interpretation as the negation of being overcome and the act's occurrence according to what He determines and wills. And this is what is said, and there is consensus on its meaning. So whoever denies that after granting its meaning, then he ascribes will to other than its true intended meaning, and that is necessary for us, because He is the Creator of everything, and His description has been established regarding what He creates that He is not compelled nor forced to it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 893

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's own position on God's will (mashī'a) regarding the acts of disbelievers. He gives two aspects of answering when asked about God's will for disbelief: first, to affirm it in an absolute sense according to the known meaning of will; second, to be absolute if the questioner's intent is unclear or malicious. Then he lists the four linguistic meanings of will to clarify what is and is not attributable to God. The first meaning is wishfulness (tamannī), which is denied for God in everything. The second is command and invitation, denied for any blameworthy act. The third is pleasure and acceptance, also denied for blameworthy acts. The fourth is the interpretation of will as 'negation of being overcome' (nafy al-ghalabah) and that the act occurs according to God's determination and will—this is the meaning that is affirmed by consensus. Denying this after understanding it amounts to a misattribution of will. Maturidi grounds this in God's being the Creator of all things, and His attribute of not being compelled or forced in His creation.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإطلاق
التعنت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume theologians must always give a direct affirmative answer about God's will for evil actions without considering the questioner.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if the questioner is obstinate or the intent is unclear, one may simply affirm God's will absolutely without further elaboration.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, when should the theologian adopt the second aspect (absolute response when the questioner's intent is not understood)?
A) Always, as a general rule.
B) Only when the questioner is sincere.
C) When the questioner's intent is unknown or he appears obstinate.
D) Never; the first aspect is always sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that the answer about God's will depends on the questioner's sincerity and understanding, with a cautious approach for unclear or hostile inquiries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone questions God's will for disbelief with a hostile attitude, do you instinctively qualify your answer to avoid discomfort? What does this lesson demand of you in such moments?
?
Think of a specific conversation where you avoided affirming a tenet of faith because you feared the other person's reaction. How would you handle it differently now, considering the two responses Maturidi outlines?
?
If you truly internalized that negating God's will for disbelief is forbidden (منع أن منفي), what would change in your daily conversations about qadar?

The Four Linguistic Meanings of Will (Mashī'a)
Maturidi enumerates the four linguistic meanings of will and establishes which is attributable to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four linguistic meanings of will and which one applies to God's relation to creation.

Core Idea
Only one meaning of 'will' is attributed to God: that He is never overcome and determines all that occurs.

The term 'will' can mean different things. Maturidi lists four meanings and shows that only the fourth—negation of being overcome—is affirmed for God in relation to His creation. This lesson clarifies the distinction and its theological importance.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration passage
Read the Arabic passage that lists the four meanings. Follow the structure: 'أحدها... والثاني... والثالث... والرابع...'.
Note that each meaning is followed by its applicability or negation for God.
2
Identify the four meanings
Write down the four meanings: wishfulness, command/calling, pleasure/acceptance, and negation of being overcome. For each, note whether it is affirmed or negated for God and for which actions.
The first three are negated for God in certain contexts; the fourth is affirmed for all actions.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the final part that states consensus on the fourth meaning and that denying it misattributes will. Reflect on the reason: God is Creator and not compelled.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider a scenario: When someone says 'God willed that person to disbelieve,' which meaning of will is intended? What would be incorrect meanings?
The intended meaning is the fourth: God's determination without being overcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Before you pray tonight, thank God that His will is rooted in absolute power and wisdom, not weakness.
?
If God's will for all things means He is not compelled, then your free choices are truly yours—yet they occur within His non-coercive determination. Does this make you more humble or more anxious? Commit to memorizing the phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with God) and recite it whenever you feel proud of your own strength.

Willing the Act vs. Willing the Sin
Maturidi distinguishes willing an act from willing its sinfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between willing an act that happens to be sinful and willing the sin itself, and why this matters for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Willing a sinful act is not the same as willing the sin.

This short but powerful passage from Imam Maturidi clarifies a subtle but essential point in Islamic theology: the difference between God's will for an action and the moral quality of that action. Understanding this distinction protects against misunderstanding divine decree and human responsibility. Let's examine Maturidi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, an example, an application to God.
The closing phrase is a prayer, not part of the argument.
2
Identify Maturidi's key distinction
Look for the phrase that separates 'willing an act that is disobedience' from 'willing the disobedience itself'. How does the example of believers illustrate this?
3
Apply the distinction to God's will
Consider why this distinction is necessary: if God willed a sinful act as an act, does that mean He wills sin? Maturidi says no. Reflect on the implications for divine justice.
This clarifies that God's will does not imply approval of sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه يجوز إرادة فعل هو معصية لا على قصد عصيان، وكذلك معاصي المؤمنين كلها كانت على أفعال من عاصيهم، وإن لم يريدوا معصية الله، بل لو أرادوا لكفروا، فهذا يبين أن إرادة فعل يكون من فاعله معصية لا يكون كإرادة المعصية، فمثله إرادة الله فعل الكافر ليكون منه معصية، أو فعله الشتم ليكون مشتما قبيحا لا يكون كإرادة الشتم والمعصية، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It is permissible to will an act that is disobedience without intending disobedience, and likewise all the disobediences of the believers were upon acts of those who disobeyed, even if they did not intend disobedience to God; rather, if they intended it they would have disbelieved. This clarifies that willing an act that becomes a disobedience from its doer is not like willing the disobedience. Similarly, God's willing the act of the disbeliever to be from him a disobedience, or His willing the act of reviling to be a foul reviling, is not like willing reviling and disobedience. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction. Maturidi begins by stating that it is permissible to will an act that is a sin without intending the sin itself. He illustrates this by noting that the sins of believers are actions that they commit without intending to disobey God; if they intended that disobedience, they would become disbelievers. This shows that willing an act that turns out to be a sin from its doer is not equivalent to willing the sin itself. Maturidi then applies this to God: when God wills the disbeliever's act of disbelief or the act of reviling, He does not will the rebellion or the foulness itself, but rather the action in which those qualities inhere. The passage closes with a phrase seeking divine aid.

Key Terms
إرادة
معصية
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God willing an act that is sinful implies God wills sin itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God wills the act as an occurrence, not the sin as rebellion. The sin is a quality attributed to the human agent's intention, not to God's will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is willing an act that is a sin not the same as willing the sin?
A) Because God does not will anything that is evil.
B) Because the sin is attributed to the human agent, not to God.
C) Because the intention of the doer is different from God's intention.
D) Because God only wills good.

Key Takeaway
God wills the occurrence of actions that are sinful, but He does not will the sin as rebellion against Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you committed where you did not intend to disobey God. How does Maturidi's distinction change the way you see that moment? Will you now approach your repentance differently, focusing on the act's harm rather than labeling yourself a rebel?
?
This lesson says that if a believer intended to disobey God, they would become a disbeliever. Search your heart: have you ever deliberately intended to rebel against God? If so, what does that mean for your faith? What step will you take tonight to reaffirm your intention to obey?
?
God wills the actions of disbelievers but not their rebellion. How does that affect your view of non-Muslims? Can you see their actions as separate from their rebellion? Will you change how you pray for them, asking God to remove the rebellion while still ordaining their actions?
