Iblis's Will vs God's Will: Distinguishing Will from Love
Maturidi addresses the objection that Iblis's will opposed God's will, and answers with love and pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi resolves the apparent conflict between God's universal will and His specific love for obedience, by distinguishing divine attributes of will, love, and pleasure.

Core Idea
God wills all that happens, but loves only the good.

One of the most challenging theological questions is how God's will relates to evil. The objection raised against Maturidi is that Iblis's will to cause disbelief seemed to override God's will for obedience. Maturidi's response is not to deny God's sovereignty but to clarify the distinct meanings of divine will, love, and pleasure. In this passage, you will see his precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the challenge: Iblis willed disbelief for most people, but God willed obedience. Disbelief prevailed, so Iblis's will seems to override God's will.
Notice the wording: 'Iblis's will in God's kingdom and authority is more remote from God's will.'
2
Identify Maturidi's distinction
Maturidi answers by invoking pleasure (rida), love (mahabba), and wrath (sakht). He separates will (irada) from these attributes.
Will is a condition for an act's existence; pleasure and love relate to its moral quality after it occurs.
3
Grasp the implication
God wills all actions (including disbelief) in terms of occurrence, but He loves and is pleased only with faith and obedience. Thus, there is no conflict: God's will is universal, His love is specific.
This solves the objection without compromising divine sovereignty or goodness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض بما كان كفر أكثر عباد الله بأن أراد إبليس، والله أراد منهم الطاعة، فصارت إرادة إبليس في ملك الله وسلطانه أبعد من إرادة الله، فأجاب بالرضا والمحبة والسخط، وقد بينا نحن الفصل بين الأمرين، على أنه يكون فعل يرضى به المرء ويسخط من غير أن كان وقت فعله، ومحال ذلك في الإرادة ثبت أنها شرط الفعل فيما يظهر التعجيز إذ لا يخلو عنها فعل المختار. وأيضا إنا لا نقول بأن الله يحب من يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أو يرضى منه لأنهما يحبان بالفعل، فمن لا يفعل بالقول به بعيد، وأما الإرادة فقد بينا، والله أعلم.
Then he was objected with what caused the disbelief of most of God's servants: that Iblis willed, and God willed obedience from them, so Iblis's will in God's kingdom and authority is more remote from God's will. He answered with pleasure, love, and wrath. And we have clarified the distinction between the two matters: that an act occurs with which one is pleased or displeased without it being at the time of its doing, and that is impossible for will, as it is established that will is a condition of the act in what shows the inability, for the act of the free agent is not free from it. Also, we do not say that God loves one whom He knows will not believe, or is pleased with him, because these are [based on] actual love and pleasure. So one who does not act [in accordance with what is said] is far from the statement. As for will, we have clarified it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer, followed by a further clarification. The objection states that since Iblis willed disbelief for most people and God willed obedience, yet disbelief prevailed, Iblis's will seems to have overcome God's will. Maturidi answers by distinguishing three concepts: divine will (irada), divine love (mahabba), and divine pleasure (rida). God wills all occurrences, but He loves only obedience and is pleased only with faith. Pleasure and wrath can be connected to an act after it occurs, based on its moral quality, but will is a condition for the very occurrence of the act. Moreover, God does not love a person whom He knows will not believe, because love is based on actual faith. Thus, the apparent triumph of Iblis's will is only in terms of occurrence, not in terms of divine approval.

Key Terms
إرادة
محبة
رضا
سخط
إبليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will and His love are the same attribute.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are distinct: will governs the occurrence of events; love and pleasure are based on the moral quality of actions.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi reconcile God's universal will with His specific love for obedience?
A) By saying God's will is ineffective.
B) By distinguishing between will and love/pleasure.
C) By claiming God does not will evil.
D) By asserting that Iblis has independent power.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all events, but His love and pleasure are reserved for obedience, resolving the apparent conflict between divine sovereignty and moral evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by thinking 'God willed it, so it's not my fault'? How does this lesson's distinction between God's will and His love confront that excuse? Will you now repent more earnestly, knowing God does not love that sin?
?
Consider a trial or hardship you are facing. Do you believe God is pleased with you in that moment even if you are not actively obeying? How does the teaching that pleasure follows actual obedience change your reliance on God's mercy? Will you strive to obey even in difficulty to seek His pleasure?
?
You know someone who rejects faith. Do you pray for their guidance with the assumption that God loves them? According to this passage, God does not love the disbeliever. Does that lessen your urgency or increase it? How will you persist in calling them to truth, knowing God's will allows their disbelief but His love is for believers only?

Iblis's Will is Wish, Not Effective
Maturidi explains that Iblis's will is mere wish, while God's is effective, and al-Faqih responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Iblis's will is considered mere wishful thinking (tamanni) and not a true effective will (irada), and how this distinction preserves God's absolute power and the nature of belief.

Core Idea
Iblis's will is a powerless wish, not a rival to God's effective will.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid tackles a subtle point about the nature of Iblis's will. It is not a true will that can compel, but a mere wish. The commentator al-Faqih then draws out an important implication: if Iblis's will is just a wish, how could it ever overcome God's will? Their discussion also warns against the idea that God forces belief by compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage with its translation to get the full argument. Notice how Maturidi first states the claim, then al-Faqih responds.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Distinguish tamanni from irada
Identify the key terms 'تمني' (tamanni = wishful thinking) and 'إرادة' (irada = will). Notice that Maturidi says Iblis's will is tamanni, and al-Faqih explains that irada can compel (توجب الغلبة) but tamanni cannot.
3
Understand al-Faqih's question
Al-Faqih asks: if Iblis's will is mere wish, how did his wish overcome God's will? This highlights the absurdity of thinking Iblis's will challenges divine will.
4
Reject compulsion in belief
Al-Faqih also refutes the notion that God compels belief by force. Such an idea stems from confusion (حيرة) and desolation (وحشة). True belief cannot be forced.
This connects to the broader discussion on free will and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: إرادة إبليس هي التمني، ولو أراد العباد ما كفروا، والله يقدر على منعهم بالقهر. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: قلنا له قد صدقت، والإرادة قد توجب الغلبة والتمني لا، فكيف غلب تمني عدوه على إرادته؟ وقوله: يقدر ويقهر، وهذا النوع إنما هو أثر الحيرة والوحشة، ولا يجوز الإيمان بالقسر بوجه، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: Iblis's will is wishful thinking (tamanni), and if the servants willed, they would not disbelieve, and God is able to prevent them with force. Al-Faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: We said to him: You have spoken the truth. Will may entail compulsion, but wishful thinking does not. So how can the wish of His enemy overcome His will? And His statement: 'He is able and compels' – this type is only an effect of confusion and desolation, and it is not permissible to believe in compulsion in any way. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 899

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on Iblis, now with a direct statement by Maturidi and a response by the commentator al-Faqih. Maturidi asserts that Iblis's so-called 'will' is actually tamanni, a wishful desire, not true irada. Proof: if the servants had true will, they would not disbelieve, but they do. God, in contrast, has full power to prevent them by force. Al-Faqih affirms this, noting that true will can compel, whereas wishful thinking does not. He then asks how a mere wish could overcome God's effective will. The idea that God compels belief by force is rejected as stemming from confusion and desolation; belief by compulsion is not valid.

Key Terms
تمني
إرادة
غلبة
قهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iblis's will is a real effective will that can rival God's will.
Correction: Iblis's will is mere wishful thinking (tamanni) without effect; it cannot overcome God's true will.

Checkpoint
According to al-Faqih, why does Iblis's will not challenge God's will?
A) Because Iblis has no free will.
B) Because Iblis's will is mere wishful thinking without effect.
C) Because God can overpower Iblis.
D) Because Iblis does not actually will anything.

Key Takeaway
Iblis's will is mere wishful thinking and cannot challenge God's effective will; belief cannot be compelled by force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a spiritual goal you have. Is it a mere wish (tamannī) that you hope will happen, or a true will (irāda) that drives you to act? What would change if you treated it as an effective will, trusting in God's power while taking responsibility?
?
When you see people around you disbelieving or sinning, do you ever feel frustrated or try to force them to change? This lesson says faith cannot be compelled. How can you invite others to truth without falling into qasr?
?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with God.' In what area of your life have you been relying on your own power rather than Allah's? How does this statement redirect your heart toward true reliance?

Why Doesn't God Prevent Sin if He Disapproves?
Maturidi answers an objection about God's ability to prevent sin without willing it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's not preventing sin does not contradict His will or power.

Core Idea
God's will includes the act; prevention is not required.

This lesson addresses a common objection against divine justice: if God disapproves of sin, why doesn't He prevent it? Maturidi explains that God's will encompasses the act, and prevention is not a necessary consequence of will. He also considers wise management and the nature of compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the objection and response.
Notice the structure: objection, counter, two aspects, then further clarification.
2
Identify the two aspects
The passage mentions two reasons why prevention may not occur: God does not will the act, or prevention is not permissible due to wise management. Find these in the text.
These are not separate cases but complementary explanations.
3
Understand the shaykh's elaboration
Maturidi adds that in human experience, if someone has power and does not prevent, it doesn't imply inability. He also clarifies when prevention is necessary and when it is not.
Focus on the distinction between compulsion and permissible prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: فإن قيل: رأيت حكيما يقدر على منع عبده عن أمر لا يريده ولا يمنحه؟ فعارض بالجبر، وذا خطأ؛ لأنه عندنا يريده، وليس المنع من شرط ما يراد. ثم فإنه يعد لوجهين لا يريده ولا يجوز له المنع لضرب من التدبير. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: إن كنت على الشاهد تقدره فلا تجاده، إلا أن لا يقدر عليه أو هو لم يرد الفعل به. قال: ومنها ما يجب المنع، فدل أن المنع إن وجب وجب لعلة لا لعينه، وما يذكر من العلة فإن كانت أوجبت الاضطرار، فهو الذي قيل لا يقدر عليه، وإن كان لا يوجب، وقد يملك القهر لا بالتعدي، فهو لا يسعه عندنا، وهو خارج من العرف، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: If it is said: Do you see a wise person able to prevent his servant from a thing he does not want but does not prevent him? He countered with jabr (compulsion), and that is an error; because according to us He wills it, and prevention is not a condition of what is willed. Then he considers two aspects: He does not will it and it is not permissible for Him to prevent it due to a type of management. Al-Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: If you were upon the witness [i.e., in the phenomenal world] you would deem him capable, but do not argue with him, unless he is not able or he did not will the act by it. He said: Among them is what must be prevented, so it indicates that if prevention is necessary, it is necessary for a cause not for its essence. And what is mentioned of the cause—if it necessitated constraint, then it is that which is said: he is not able over it. And if it does not necessitate, and he may possess compulsion without transgression, then it is not within his capacity according to us, and it is outside of custom. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 899

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objection compares God to a human master who has the power to prevent his servant from doing something he does not want, yet fails to do so—implying weakness. Maturidi first counters by noting that the objection wrongly assumes compulsion (jabr), but in our view God does will the act, so prevention is not required. He then considers two scenarios: either God does not will the act, yet still does not prevent it because of some wise management. Al-Shaykh (Maturidi himself) adds that in experienced matters, if someone has power and does not prevent, it doesn't mean inability; unless the person lacks power or did not will the act. The passage further discusses cases where prevention is necessary—this necessity arises from a cause, not from the act itself. If the cause forces constraint, then the person is said to be unable; if not, and he uses compulsion rightfully, it is beyond normal capacity. The overall point is to defend that God's not preventing sin does not contradict His will or power.

Key Terms
منع
جبر
قهر
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's ability to prevent sin means He must do so if He disapproves.
Correction: Maturidi explains that God's will includes the act, and prevention is not a condition of will. God may have wise reasons not to prevent.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi argue against the claim that God should prevent sin if He doesn't will it?
A) By saying God is not able to prevent all sins.
B) By stating that God does will the act, and prevention is not required.
C) By claiming that human free will overrides God's will.
D) By saying that God only wills good.

Key Takeaway
God's not preventing sin does not contradict His will; prevention depends on wisdom, not on will alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe sin occurring in the world or in your own life, do you ever question God's wisdom or care because He does not prevent it? How does the Maturidi argument challenge that reaction and call you to trust His management?
?
Is there a specific sin or hardship you wish God had prevented, but He didn't? What does this lesson teach you about His will and your need to submit to His wise decree, even when you don't understand it?
?
If you truly believe that God's not preventing sin is part of His perfect wisdom, how should that change the way you respond to your own failures and the failures of others? Are you willing to let go of your expectation that God must act as you think best?

Guidance and Misguidance as Reward and Punishment
Maturidi's interpretation of guidance/misguidance as reward/punishment based on prior actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's interpretation that God's guidance and misguidance are not arbitrary but are based on prior human obedience or disobedience.

Core Idea
Guidance is a reward; misguidance is a consequence—never arbitrary.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection against divine justice: if God guides some and misguides others, does that not make Him arbitrary? Maturidi explains that the Qur'anic verses actually describe a system of reward and punishment based on prior human obedience or disobedience. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi opens with an answer to an objection.
The OCR suggests 'كا' might be 'كذلك'. Read with that in mind.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objection is that God seems to guide or misguide whomever He wills arbitrarily. Maturidi responds by explaining that guidance follows obedience and misguidance follows disobedience.
He cites verse 47:17 as support.
3
Grasp the key principle
Maturidi states that God does not do this 'initially' (ibtidā'an) without prior deserving. This establishes a principle of just proportionality.
This refutes the misconception of arbitrary divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في جواب ما عورض بقوله: فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ، أن تأويله معروف وهو أن من أطاعه أتان من لطائفه ما لا يقدر عليه غيره وسماه بالأسماء الشريفة وحكم له بالأحكام الرفيع ثوابا لطاعته ليزداد له الرغبة كقوله: وَالَّذِينَ اهْتَدَوْا زَادَهُمْ هُدًى، ومن عصاه منع منه ما ذكر فيضيق صدره كا ولا يفعل بأحد ذلك ابتداء كالآية التي ذكرتها. وقوله: وَمَا يُضِلُّ بِهِ إِلَّا الفاسقين، ثم قال: فلا يجوز ذلك ابتداء من غير استحقاق العداوة والولاية.
And he said in answer to what was objected with His saying: 'Whomever Allah wills to guide, He expands his breast to Islam' – that its interpretation is known, and it is that whoever obeys Him, there comes to him from His subtle favors what no other can, and He named him with the noble names and judged for him the high judgments as a reward for his obedience, to increase his desire, as His saying: 'And those who are guided, He increases them in guidance.' And whoever disobeys Him, He prevents from him what was mentioned, so his breast is constricted… and He does not do that to anyone as an initial act, like the verse you mentioned. And His saying: 'But He does not lead astray by it except the defiantly disobedient' – then he said: So that is not permissible initially without prior deserving of enmity and friendship.
Source pages p. 899

Commentary
This passage is a Qur'anic interpretation addressing an objection based on verses that attribute guidance and misguidance directly to God. Maturidi explains that His guidance is a reward for previous obedience: God grants subtle favours to those who obey, calling them by noble names and giving them high ranks to increase their desire, as in 47:17. Conversely, misguidance is a withholding of these favours from those who disobey, resulting in constriction of the heart. Importantly, God does not begin with such treatment without prior deserving; it is based on the person's prior state of enmity or friendship. The verse 2:26 about leading astray only the defiantly disobedient supports this: misguidance is not arbitrary but follows the person's own corruption.

Key Terms
هدى
ضلال
ابتدا
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God arbitrarily guides some people and misguides others without any reason or prior action.
Correction: Maturidi shows that guidance follows obedience and misguidance follows disobedience; neither is done 'initially' without prior deserving.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi interpret verses that say God guides or misguides whom He wills?
A) As arbitrary divine decree.
B) As based on prior obedience or disobedience of the person.
C) As referring to the creation of faith.
D) As metaphorical only.

Key Takeaway
God's guidance and misguidance are based on prior human action, not arbitrary will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience spiritual difficulty or feel distant from Allah, do you immediately examine your own actions for disobedience, or do you assume it is an arbitrary test? What would it mean for you to treat your spiritual state as a direct consequence of your choices?
?
Are there areas of your life where you expect Allah's special guidance and favor without actually striving to obey Him? How can you realign your expectations with the principle that guidance is a reward for prior obedience?
?
Have you ever blamed Allah for 'leading you astray' when you deliberately chose sin? How does understanding misguidance as a deserved consequence of your own corruption change your view of divine justice and your personal responsibility?

Abu Hanifa's Two Questions Against Qadariyya
Abu Hanifa refutes Qadariyya with two questions about God's foreknowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa's two sequential questions expose the logical inconsistency of the Qadariyya position and force them to affirm both God's foreknowledge and will.

Core Idea
Two questions expose the inner contradiction of denying predestination.

Abu Hanifa uses a powerful dialectical tool: a chain of two simple questions that forces the opponent to either contradict themselves or concede the truth. This passage shows how Islamic theology uses rational argument to defend core beliefs. Let's walk through his method step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of Abu Hanifa's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the two 'edges' (جرفين) structure.
2
Trace the two questions
Identify the two sequential questions. The first is about God's knowledge of the future, the second about His will to execute that knowledge. See how each answer leads to the next step.
3
Understand the dilemma
Consider the consequences of each possible answer. The Qadariyya are forced into a dilemma: either they deny God's knowledge (blasphemy) or they admit that God wills everything as He knows it, thus affirming predestination.
This shows the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: بيننا وبين القدرية الكلام في جرفين: أن نسألهم: هل علم الله ما يكون أبدا على ما يكون؟ فإن قالوا: لا، كفروا؛ لأنهم جهلوا، وإن قالوا: نعم، قيل: شاء أن ينفذ علمه كما علم أو لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قالوا بأن الله شاء أن يكون جاهلا، ومن ذلك فليس بحكيم، وإن قالوا: نعم، أقروا بأنه شاء أن يكون كل شيء كما شاء.
And Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, said: Between us and the Qadariyya is speech in two edges: that we ask them: Did God know what will be forever as it will be? If they say: No, they disbelieve; because they made [Him] ignorant. If they say: Yes, it is said: Did He will to execute His knowledge as He knew or not? If they say: No, they said that God willed to be ignorant, and from that He is not wise. If they say: Yes, they affirm that He willed everything to be as He willed.
Source pages p. 902

Commentary
This passage presents a concise refutation of the Qadariyya (those who deny divine predestination of actions) attributed to Abu Hanifa. The refutation consists of two sequential questions. First: Does God know the future as it will occur? If they deny that, they have blasphemed because they attribute ignorance to God. If they affirm it, then the second question: Does God will to execute what He knows? If they say no, that would mean God wills to be ignorant (since His knowledge would not be realized), which contradicts His wisdom. If they say yes, they admit that God wills everything to be as He knows it will be, thus affirming predestination. This argument forces the Qadariyya into a dilemma: either they deny God's knowledge (kufr) or they affirm His will and thereby concede the point.

Key Terms
القدرية (Qadariyya)
علم الله (God's knowledge)
مشيئة (will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qadariyya can accept God's foreknowledge but deny His will for human actions, especially evil.
Correction: Abu Hanifa's argument shows that if they affirm God's foreknowledge, they must also affirm He wills to execute that knowledge. Denying will after affirming knowledge leads to the absurd conclusion that God wills to be ignorant.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Qadariyya say God did not will to execute His knowledge?
A) They become believers.
B) They attribute ignorance to God.
C) They affirm God's wisdom.
D) They admit human free will.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's two questions demonstrate that denying divine predestination inevitably leads to either denying God's knowledge or attributing ignorance to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life, including your mistakes and struggles, occurs by God's will and knowledge? If yes, why do you sometimes resist His decree with anger or despair? What would change if you fully internalized that His will always executes His perfect knowledge?
?
When you face a situation where you feel God's will contradicts your own desires, do you ever catch yourself thinking that God did not know or will this difficulty? How does Abu Hanifa's argument expose the danger of such thoughts? What practical step will you take this week to submit to God's will in that area?
?
Abu Hanifa's second question forces you to choose: either God willed everything exactly as He knew, or He willed ignorance. Can you honestly say you accept that God wills everything—even the things you dislike—without falling into the Qadariyya's error? Commit to repeating this dua tonight: 'I believe in Qadar, its good and bad, from Allah.'

Abu Hanifa's Two-Question Argument Against the Qadariyya
Abu Hanifa presents a two-question argument to refute Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hanifa's dialectical argument against the Qadariyya and how it refutes their denial of predestination through a logical reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Two questions expose the contradiction in denying divine will.

Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) presents a powerful argument that corners the Qadariyya by forcing them to either affirm God's perfect knowledge and will or fall into blasphemy. This passage shows how a simple logical structure can refute a theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise dialectical style of Abu Hanifa.
The argument uses only two questions.
2
Follow the two-question structure
Identify the first question about Allah's knowledge and the second about His will. See how the answers lead to opposite conclusions.
Each possible answer forces the opponent into a dilemma.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why denying either knowledge or will leads to blasphemy. Understand how this establishes the doctrine of predestination (qadar) from the opponent's concessions.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو حنيفه رحمه الله: بيننا وبين القدرية الكلام في جرفين: أن نسألهم: هل عليم الله ما يكون أبدا على ما يكون؟ فإن قالوا: لا، كفروا؛ لأنهم جهلوا، وإن قالوا: نعم، قيل: شاء أن ينفذ علمه كما علم أو لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قالوا بأن الله شاء أن يكون جاهلا، ومن ذلك فليس بحكيم، وإن قالوا: نعم، أقروا بأنه شاء أن يكون كل شيء كما علم أن يكون. فهذا الذي تقرر عندى من المحكى عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، لا أنى ذكرته بلفظه، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Between us and the Qadariyya the debate is on two sides: that we ask them: 'Did Allah know what will always be as it will be?' If they say 'No,' they disbelieve because they made Him ignorant. And if they say 'Yes,' it is said: 'Did He will to execute His knowledge as He knew or not?' If they say 'No,' they say that Allah willed to be ignorant, and thus He is not wise. And if they say 'Yes,' they affirm that He willed everything to be as He knew it would be. This is what is established with me from the narration of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), not that I mentioned it in his very words, and there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 902

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument attributed to Abu Hanifa against the Qadariyya (those who deny predestination). The author states that the debate with the Qadariyya revolves around two points (or two sides). The argument proceeds by asking two successive questions. First: 'Did Allah know what will always be as it will be?' If the Qadari deny God's foreknowledge, they commit blasphemy by attributing ignorance to Him. If they affirm His knowledge, the second question follows: 'Did He will to execute His knowledge as He knew or not?' If they say God did not will to enact His knowledge, that implies God willed to be ignorant, which contradicts His wisdom. If they say He did will it, they must admit that God willed everything to occur exactly as He knew it. Thus, the argument forces the opponent to accept that God's will is effective and corresponds to His knowledge. The author clarifies that this is the gist of what was narrated from Abu Hanifa, not necessarily his exact wording, and ends with the phrase 'there is no power except through Allah.'

Key Terms
القدرية
علم الله
مشيئة الله
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular or relies on assuming predestination.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it forces the opponent to admit that denying God's will leads to attributing ignorance or lack of wisdom to Him. The opponent's own affirmation of God's knowledge leads logically to accepting His will.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what follows if the Qadariyya say that God did not will to execute His knowledge?
A) They affirm God's wisdom.
B) They imply that God willed to be ignorant.
C) They accept predestination.
D) They disbelieve in God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's two-question argument forces the Qadariyya to admit that Allah's will is effective and corresponds to His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah’s knowledge and will encompass every action you take—including your sins—would you still make the same excuses for your disobedience? What would change about your repentance if you saw it as part of His decree? Commit to one specific area where you will actively submit to His will today.
?
When you face trials, do you sometimes resent Allah’s decree as if He were unaware of your pain? How does Abu Hanifa’s argument expose that such resentment implies ignorance on Allah’s part? Will you surrender that bitterness and trust that His will is wise, even when you don’t understand?
?
The Qadariyya tried to protect human freedom but ended up denying divine wisdom. In your life, where do you resist Allah’s decree because you think you know better than Him? Are you willing to admit that His knowledge and will are perfect, and let go of your pride? Take a moment to recite, 'I believe in Qadar, its good and its bad,' and mean it.

Qada, Qadar, and Will Rooted in Creation of Actions
Maturidi connects the questions of decree and will to the creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi links the concepts of divine decree (qada), predestination (qadar), and divine will to the single foundation of the creation of actions.

Core Idea
Decree, predestination, and will all spring from the creation of actions.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi opens a discussion on qada and qadar by grounding them in a shared foundation: the creation of actions. Rather than treating decree and will as separate puzzles, he shows that once we accept that Allah creates all actions, decree, predestination, and will follow by necessity. Read on to see how he establishes this link and why the truth does not depend on eloquent wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the passage once in Arabic to appreciate its style, then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify the foundational principle
Locate the sentence where Maturidi states the principle: both the question of decree and will are about the creation of actions. Underline it.
This is the key to the entire argument.
3
Trace the logical implications
Notice how he says 'if that is established, then these are established.' What are 'these'? They are decree, predestination, and will. See how creation of actions leads to each.
He also mentions good and evil as part of predestination.
4
Consider the final remark on proofs
Read the last sentence about truth and words. What is Maturidi warning against?
He emphasises that truth stands on evidence, not language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في القضاء والقدر الأصل عندنا أن هذه المسألة ومسألة الإرادة كلها في خلق الأفعال ، إن ثبت ذلك ثبت هذه ؛ إذ خلق الأفعال يثبت القضاء يكونها والقدر على ما عليها من حسن وقبح ، ويوجب أن يكون مريدا لها أن تكون خلقا له ، وقال بينا فى هذا ما نرجو به الكفاية لمن أكرم بالهداية ، لكن الناس أفردوا التكلم في مسألة منها فاتبعناهم فى الفعل لما احتمل أن يكونوا أرادوا أن الحق قد يظهر بنوره لمن تأمل بأي لفظ الألفاظ ليعلم أن الحق لا صار حقا للسان لنوع من البيان، لكن صار حقا بما له من الأدلة والبراهين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
A question concerning al-qada' and al-qadar. The principle with us is that this question and the question of will are both about the creation of actions. If that is established, then these are established; for the creation of actions establishes al-qada' of their occurring and al-qadar upon what they have of good and evil, and necessitates that He is willing them to be a creation of Him. And he said: We have explained in this what we hope is sufficient for whoever is honored with guidance. But people have spoken separately about one question of them, so we followed them in the act, because it is possible that they intended that the truth may appear by its light to whoever contemplates with any wording of words, so that they know that the truth does not become true by the tongue through a kind of explanation, but rather it becomes true by what it has of proofs and evidences, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 904

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of qada' and qadar (divine decree and predestination) and connects it to the earlier discussion of will. The author states the foundational principle: both this question and the question of will are fundamentally about the creation of actions (khalq al-af'al). If the creation of actions is established, then qada' and qadar are established, because the creation of actions implies that God decrees their occurrence and predetermines them in terms of good and evil, and it also necessitates that God wills them as His creation. The author notes that he has already given sufficient explanation earlier. However, since people have treated each question separately, he follows their approach, because the truth may become clear through any expression, but truth is not true because of eloquent language; rather it is true by virtue of its proofs and evidences.

Key Terms
قضاء (decree)
قدر (predestination)
خلق الأفعال (creation of actions)
إرادة (will)
حسن وقبح (good and evil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qada and qadar are separate from the issue of divine will.
Correction: The passage shows that qada, qadar, and will are all connected through the creation of actions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between the question of qada'/qadar and the question of will?
A) They are unrelated
B) They are both about the creation of actions
C) Qada and qadar are about events, will is about commands
D) Will is more fundamental than qada

Key Takeaway
Maturidi grounds divine decree, predestination, and will in the single reality that Allah creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action of yours is created by Allah and subject to His decree, how does that change the way you view your own choices and your responsibility before Him? What do you need to change in your heart today because of this truth?
?
The passage says truth does not become true by eloquent speech but by proofs. Have you ever been swayed by beautiful words rather than the evidence of the Quran and Sunnah? What is one area where you need to seek the truth through proofs rather than appealing rhetoric?
?
Knowing that your will is tied to Allah's creation of actions, do you truly submit to His decree when things go wrong, or do you resist? What is one situation where you need to increase your faith in qada and qadar?

Qada and Qadar Are Rooted in the Creation of Actions
Maturidi establishes that qada and qadar depend on the creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that qada and qadar are inseparable from the doctrine of the creation of actions.

Core Idea
Qada and qadar stand or fall with the creation of actions.

Maturidi opens this passage by firmly anchoring the topics of divine decree (qada) and destiny (qadar) to the creation of actions. He argues that if one accepts that God creates all actions, then qada and qadar follow necessarily. This lesson unpacks that central claim and its implications, showing why these doctrines are not separate issues but one integrated truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how Maturidi starts by stating the principle (al-aṣl) of his school.
The Arabic uses a conditional structure: 'if that is established, this is established.'
2
Identify the main thesis
The key claim is that the question of qada/qadar and the question of divine will are both about the creation of actions. Maturidi treats them as one topic.
This unifies what some might treat as separate issues.
3
Trace the logical connection
Maturidi explains that establishing creation of actions automatically establishes qada (decree upon them) and qadar (their measure of good/evil), and also necessitates that God wills them. Follow this threefold implication.
4
Reflect on the methodological note
Maturidi remarks that truth is not tied to specific wording but to proofs. He explains why he follows others in discussing 'action' specifically—because truth can appear through any expression.
This shows Maturidi's concern with methodology and clarity.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you have grasped the core relationship between creation of actions and qada/qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أراد مسألة في القضاء والقدر الأصل عندنا أن هذه المسألة ومسألة الإرادة كلها في خلق الأفعال ، إن ثبت ذلك ثبت هذه ؛ إذ خلق الأفعال يثبت القضاء يكونها والقدر على ما عليها من حسن وقبح ، ويوجب أن يكون مريدا لها أن تكون خلقا له ، وقال بينا فى هذا ما نرجو به الكفاية لمن أكرم بالهداية ، لكن الناس أفردوا التكلم في مسألة منها فاتبعناهم فى الفعل لما احتمل أن يكونوا أرادوا أن الحق قد يظهر بنوره لمن تأمل بأي لفظ الألفاظ ليعلم أن الحق لا صار حقا للسان لنوع من البيان، لكن صار حقا بما له من الأدلة والبراهين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
He intended (to discuss) the question of qadā' and qadar. The principle with us is that this question and the question of will are both about the creation of actions. If that is established, this is established; for the creation of actions establishes qadā' upon them and qadar upon what they are of good and evil, and necessitates that He be willing for them to be a creation of His. He said: We have explained in this what we hope is sufficient for one who is honored with guidance. But people have singled out speech on one part of it, so we followed them in action, because it might be that they intended that the truth may appear by its light to one who contemplates it in whatever wording, so that one knows that the truth is not truth due to the tongue for a type of elucidation, but is truth due to its proofs and evidences. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 904

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement linking the topics of qadā' (divine decree) and qadar (destiny) to the creation of actions (khalq al-af‘āl). Maturidi says that his school holds that both qadā/qadar and the question of divine will (irāda) are essentially about the creation of actions. If the creation of actions is proven, then qadā' and qadar are also proven. Why? Because creating actions means that God has decreed them (qadā') and measured them (qadar) with their qualities of good and evil. It also entails that God wills them to be His creation. Maturidi notes that he has already provided sufficient explanation on this. However, people (theologians) have isolated one part of the topic to discuss, so he follows their approach in discussing 'action' because perhaps they intended that the truth may become clear through any wording to a contemplative person, thereby showing that truth is not true because of eloquent speech but because of its own proofs and evidences.

Key Terms
قضاء (qadā')
قدر (qadar)
خلق الأفعال (khalq al-af‘āl)
إرادة (irāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might separate qada and qadar from the creation of actions, treating them as independent topics.
Correction: Maturidi insists that qada, qadar, and divine will are all about the creation of actions; if creation of actions is proven, they are automatically proven.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between the question of qada/qadar and the creation of actions?
A) They are independent topics that must be studied separately.
B) They are both about the creation of actions, and if creation is established, qada/qadar are established.
C) Qada/qadar are about reward and punishment only, while creation of actions is about power.
D) They are unrelated and have no logical connection.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi makes qada and qadar logically dependent on the creation of actions, so that one cannot affirm divine decree without affirming that God creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson claims that qada and qadar stand or fall with the creation of actions. In which areas of your life do you find yourself separating God's decree from His creative power—perhaps believing that your good deeds are from Him but your sins are your own? How does accepting that God creates every action—good or evil—change your attitude toward repentance and gratitude?
?
The passage ends by saying truth is not due to eloquence but proofs. Have you ever been swayed by eloquent arguments about qadar that contradicted the clear proof that God creates all actions? What would it take for you to submit to this truth even when it challenges your sense of autonomy?
?
If every action you perform is created by God, then even your next breath is a created act. Does this make you feel a sense of total dependence on Him, or does it unsettle you? How can you live today with the constant awareness that your actions are being decreed and measured by the One who created them?

Divine Decree

6 min
Two Aspects of Qadar and Proof of Divine Control
Maturidi defines qadar's two aspects and argues human actions are from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two aspects of qadar (divine measure) and how they establish that human actions are determined by Allah.

Core Idea
Qadar has two faces: the measure of creation and the clarity of occurrence.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid lays the cornerstone for the Sunni understanding of divine destiny. He distinguishes two complementary aspects of qadar: one concerns the inherent nature of each created thing, the other clarifies the circumstantial details of events. These two aspects together form the basis for a rational and scriptural proof that human actions fall within Allah's sovereign measure.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read Maturidi's definition of qadar carefully. Notice the two aspects he introduces.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the first aspect (al-ḥadd)
The first aspect is the 'limit' or measure upon which each thing is created. See how Maturidi links it to Quran 54:49.
Allah says: 'Indeed, all things We created with a measure.'
3
Understand the second aspect (al-bayān)
The second aspect clarifies how each thing occurs in time, place, and consequence. It relates to the prophetic hadith about qadar, its good and evil.
This aspect covers reward and punishment.
4
Follow the proof from both aspects
Maturidi argues that human actions cannot be self-produced because they exceed human comprehension in quality (first aspect) and circumstances (second aspect). Hence, they are from Allah.
Notice the reasoning: if humans cannot fully measure or know their actions, they cannot be independent.
5
Reflect on the Quranic support
Examine the additional verses about measuring journeys and decreeing Lot's wife to see how qadar permeates all aspects of existence.
These verses reinforce both aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القدر فهو على وجهين: أحدهما الحد الذي عليه يخرج الشيء، وهو جعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر، من حسن أو قبيح، من حكمة أو سفه، وهو تأويل الحكمة أن يجعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه، ويصيب حكمة الله، في كل شيء الأولى به، وعلى مثل هذا قوله: إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ. والثاني بيان ما عليه يقع كل شيء من زمان ومكان، وحق وباطل، وما له من الثواب والعقاب، وعلى مثل أحد هذين المروى عن رسول الله عند سؤال جبريل عليه السلام إياه عن الإيمان أن قرن ما ذكرنا بالقدر خيره وشره. فالأول نحو خلق الشيء على ما هو عليه قائم، ذلك في أفعال الخلق من خروجها على ما لا يبلغه أوهامهم من الحسن والقبح ولا يقدرها عقولهم، فثبت أنها خرجت ذلك بالله سبحانه، والثانى أيضا لا يحتمل منهم تقدير أفعالهم من الزمان والمكان ولا يبلغه علمهم، فمن ذلك الوجه أيضا لا يحتمل أن يكون، وهي غير خارجة سهام الله [؟]، وقال الله تعالى: وَقَدَّرْنَا فِيهَا السَّيْرَ، وقال: إِلَّا امْرَأَتَهُ قَدَّرْنَا إِنَّهَا لَمِنَ الْغَابِرِينَ، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for al-qadar (divine measure/destiny), it is of two aspects: one is the limit (al-ḥadd) upon which the thing comes forth, and it is the making of each thing as it is, whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, wise or foolish; it is the interpretation of wisdom that He makes each thing as it is, and that He hits the mark of Allah's wisdom in each thing with what is most appropriate. And similar to this is His saying: 'Indeed, all things We created with a measure (bi-qadar).' The second is the clarification of how each thing occurs regarding time and place, truth and falsehood, and what it has of reward and punishment. And similar to one of these two is what has been related from the Messenger of Allah, when Gabriel asked him about faith, that he linked what we mentioned with al-qadar, its good and its evil. The first [aspect] is like the creation of the thing as it is established – that is, regarding the actions of creation, that they come forth in a way that their imaginations cannot reach in terms of beauty and ugliness, nor can their intellects measure them. So it is established that they come forth from Allah, exalted. The second [aspect] also does not admit of them measuring their actions regarding time and place, nor does their knowledge reach that. So from that aspect too it is not possible [that they are independent], and they are not outside the arrows/decisions [؟] of Allah. And Allah says: 'And We measured (wa qaddarnā) in it the journeys,' and He says: 'Except his wife – We decreed (qaddarnā) that she would be among those who remain behind.' And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 905

Commentary
This passage is a definition of qadar (القدر) followed by a proof for divine predestination of human actions. Maturidi distinguishes two aspects of qadar. The first aspect (الوجه الأول) is the 'limit' (al-ḥadd) or measure upon which a thing is created – Allah makes each thing as it is, with its specific qualities (good/evil, beautiful/ugly, wise/foolish), which is the true meaning of wisdom. He supports this with Quran 54:49: 'Indeed, all things We created with a measure.' The second aspect is the 'clarification' (bayān) of how each thing occurs in terms of time, place, truth/falsehood, and resulting reward or punishment. This relates to the prophetic hadith about faith and qadar, its good and evil. Maturidi then uses both aspects to prove that human actions are from Allah. For the first aspect: human actions come forth in a way that human minds cannot fully grasp in terms of beauty and ugliness – they cannot measure or produce them by themselves, so they must be from Allah. For the second aspect: humans cannot determine the time and place of their actions nor fully know them, therefore these actions cannot be independent of Allah's determination. He cites Quranic verses about measuring journeys in the land and about decreeing Lot's wife's fate to further illustrate qadar.

Key Terms
القدر
الحد
البيان
الخلق
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qadar is only about the inherent measure (first aspect) and not about the clarifying details (second aspect)
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes both aspects are essential; the second aspect clarifies the temporal and spatial context of events, rooted in hadith.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which aspect of qadar proves that human actions are from Allah because humans cannot fully comprehend their beauty or ugliness?
A) The second aspect (clarification of time and place)
B) The first aspect (the limit upon which a thing is created)
C) Both aspects equally
D) Neither aspect

Key Takeaway
Qadar encompasses both the intrinsic nature of creation and the circumstantial details of events, together demonstrating that all human actions fall under Allah's decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believed that even the goodness or evil of your actions is measured by Allah's wisdom—not by your own judgment—how would that change the way you evaluate your successes and failures? Name one recent action you judged as 'good' and ask yourself: was that your measure or Allah's?
?
You cannot determine the time, place, or consequences of your very next breath. How then can you claim ownership over any action? What would it look like to surrender that illusion today—perhaps in a specific decision you're about to make?
?
The hadith of Gabriel links belief in qadar to the core of faith. In what area of your life do you secretly resent or resist Allah's decree—a personal outcome, a relationship, or a missed opportunity? Will you trust His wisdom over your own, starting now?

The Two Meanings of Qadar (Divine Decree)
Maturidi defines the two meanings of qadar and provides proofs from the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qadar (divine decree) has two distinct meanings in Islamic theology: the limit by which everything is created and the specification of when and where events occur.

Core Idea
Qadar is not one thing; it has two meanings that clarify God's sovereignty over creation.

Maturidi begins by clarifying the term 'qadar', which is often misunderstood. He distinguishes between the measure by which each thing is created and the appointed time and place for events. This passage lays the foundation for understanding divine decree in Sunni theology and provides Quranic proof for both aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully.
Start by reading the Arabic text once. Notice that it immediately divides qadar into two aspects. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The Arabic uses 'أما' (as for) to introduce the topic.
2
Identify the two aspects of qadar.
Maturidi states the first aspect is 'al-hadd' (the limit) by which a thing emerges, and the second is 'bayan' (clarification) of time, place, and consequences. Write them down.
3
Examine the Quranic verses.
Look at the verse 'إنا كل شيء خلقناه بقدر' for the first aspect and the verses about journey and Lot's wife for the second. Understand how each supports the respective meaning.
The verses are quoted as proof texts.
4
Reflect on the theological implication.
Maturidi explains that both aspects show human acts are from God—their quality and timing are beyond human capacity. This reinforces divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القدر فهو على وجهين : أحدهما الحد الذى عليه يخرج الشي، وهو جعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر ، من حسن أو قيح ، من أو سفه ، وهو تأويل الحكمة أن يجعل كل شيء على ما هو عليه ، ويصيب حكمة الله ، في كل شيء الأولى به ، وعلى مثل هذا قوله : " إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ * والثاني بيان ما عليه يقع كل شيء من زمان ومكان ، وحق وباطل ، وما له من الثواب والعقاب ، وعلى مثل أحد هذين المروى عن رسول الله عند سؤال جبريل عليه السلام إياه عن الإيمان أن قرن ما ذكرنا بالقدر خيره وشره من فالأول نحو خلق الشيء على ما هو عليه قائم ، ذلك في أفعال الخلق من خروجها على ما لا يبلغه أوهامهم من الحسن والقبح ولا يقدرها عقولهم ، فثبت أنها خرجت ذلك بالله سبحانه ، والثانى أيضا لا يحتمل منهم تقدير أفعالهم من الزمان والمكان ولا يبلغه علمهم ، فمن ذلك الوجه أيضا لا يحتمل أن يكون ، وهي غير خارجة سهام الله ، وقال الله تعالى : " وقَدَّرْنَا فِيها السَّيْرَ " ، وقال : " إِلَّا امْرَأَتَهُ قَدَّرْنَا إِنَّهَا عن لمن الغَابِرِينَ " ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
As for al-qadar, it is of two aspects: one of them is the limit upon which a thing emerges, and it is making everything as it is of good or evil, of beauty or ugliness, of [؟] or foolishness, and it is the interpretation of wisdom that He makes everything as it is, and the wisdom of God hits upon what is most fitting for everything. And similar to this is His statement: 'Indeed, We have created everything by a measure.' The second is the clarification of upon what everything occurs, of time and place, truth and falsehood, and what it has of reward and punishment. And similar to one of these two is what is narrated from the Messenger of God when Gabriel asked him about faith, that he linked what we mentioned with qadar, its good and evil. So the first is like the creation of a thing upon what it is established. That is in the acts of creation, their emergence upon what their imaginations do not reach of beauty and ugliness, and their intellects do not measure it. So it is established that they emerged that by God the Exalted. And the second also does not admit of their measuring their acts in time and place, and their knowledge does not reach it. So from that aspect also it cannot be that they are not of the arrows of God. And God the Exalted said: 'And We measured in it the journey.' And He said: 'Except his wife, We decreed that she would be of those who remain behind.' And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 906

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qadar' (divine measure/decree) as having two distinct meanings. The first meaning is the limit or measure by which each thing is created, encompassing its nature of good or evil, beauty or ugliness, and so on. This is the expression of divine wisdom placing everything in its proper state, supported by the Quranic verse 'We have created everything by a measure' (54:49). The second meaning is the specification of the time, place, and consequences (reward or punishment) for every occurrence. This is linked to the famous hadith of Gabriel where the Prophet mentioned belief in qadar, both its good and evil. Then the author explains that the first aspect shows that human acts, in their quality of goodness or ugliness, are beyond human imagination and intellect to determine, so it is established that they are from God. Similarly, the second aspect—the timing and location of actions—is beyond human knowledge, so they cannot be independent of God's determination. Two Quranic verses are cited: one about the journey (34:18) and one about Lot's wife (15:60) to illustrate divine predetermination. The passage ends with the formula 'there is no power except with God.'

الحد
الحكمة
الحسن والقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qadar refers only to one thing, such as predestination of events.
Correction: Maturidi shows that qadar has two distinct meanings: the limit of creation and the specification of occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two aspects of qadar?
A) The first is the decree of good, the second is the decree of evil.
B) The first is the measure by which things are created, the second is the manifestation of their occurrence in time and place.
C) The first is divine knowledge, the second is divine power.
D) The first is fate, the second is free will.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's division of qadar into two aspects clarifies God's complete control over creation and events, affirming that all things occur by His measure and decree.

?
If qadar includes both good and evil by divine wisdom, how does that affect your attitude towards hardships you face? Do you accept them as part of God's wise decree, or do you resist?

Pleased with Divine Decree: The Hadith and Its Correct Meaning
Maturidi explains the hadith about being pleased with Allah's decree and relates it to disbelief being the servant's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of being pleased with Allah's decree, particularly that it involves recognizing disbelief as ugly and that disbelief is the servant's own act.

Core Idea
Being pleased with Allah's decree means acknowledging disbelief as evil, not approving of it.

This lesson examines a hadith Qudsi about contentment with divine decree. Maturidi clarifies that being pleased with God's decree does not mean accepting disbelief as good; rather, it means acknowledging its ugliness and that it incurs punishment. We will explore the hadith, its interpretation, and the critical distinction between God's decree and the servant's act.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its translation
Read the Arabic hadith carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the warning: whoever is not pleased with God's decree and does not endure His trial should seek another lord.
This is a hadith Qudsi, so the words are from Allah but narrated by the Prophet.
2
Understand the faqih's interpretation
The faqih explains that being pleased with His decree means knowing that disbelief is ugly and deserving of punishment unless repented. Rejecting this knowledge is itself disbelief.
Interpretation is based on the hadith's warning.
3
Grasp the distinction between decree and act
Disbelief and its ugliness are the act of the servant, not God's decree. God's decree is the reality of the act (i.e., its existence), not its moral quality.
This preserves God's justice and human responsibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that God's decree is what was described: the reality of the act. The phrase 'there is no power except with God' reminds us of reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بما روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال ، قال تعالى : خبره من لم يرض بقضائى ولم يصبر على بلائى فليتخذ ربا سواى . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : هذا مثل الأول ، وإن الرضا بقضائه أن تعلم بأن الكفر مضمحل قبيح وأنه شر وفساد وأنه يوجب مقت صاحبه وتعذيبه إلا أن يتوب ، فمن لم يرض بهذا فهو كافر ، فيكون على ما جاء به الخبر . على أن الكفر والقبح / هو فعل العبد ، ومحال أن يكون هو قضاؤه ، فثبت أن قضاء الله هو ما ذكرت مما عليه حقيقة الفعل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And he argued with what was narrated from the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) that He, Exalted, said: '[?] whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not bear patiently with My trial, let him seek a lord other than Me.' The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: This is like the first, and being pleased with His decree is that you know that disbelief is abominable, ugly, and that it is evil and corruption, and that it necessitates the wrath of its possessor and his punishment unless he repents. So whoever is not pleased with this is a disbeliever, and thus it is according to what the report came with. Moreover, disbelief and ugliness are the act of the servant, and it is impossible that it be His decree. So it is established that God's decree is what I mentioned of that upon which the reality of the act rests. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage begins with a hadith Qudsi in which Allah declares that whoever is not content with His decree and does not endure His trial should seek another lord. The faqih (the author or a commentator) explains that being pleased with God's decree means recognizing that disbelief is abominable and evil, and that it incurs divine wrath and punishment if not repented. Thus, rejecting this recognition is itself disbelief, matching the warning of the hadith. Then a key point is made: disbelief and its ugliness are the act of the servant, not God's decree. Therefore, God's decree is what was previously described—the reality of the act, not the act's moral quality. The phrase 'there is no power except with God' closes the argument.

Key Terms
الرضا بالقضاء
الكفر
فعل العبد
القضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being pleased with God's decree means approving of disbelief itself or feeling happy about evil happening.
Correction: It actually means accepting that disbelief is wrong and deserves punishment, not approving of its occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to the faqih, what does being pleased with Allah's decree (al-rida bi qada'ihi) mean?
A) Accepting everything that happens, including evil.
B) Knowing that disbelief is ugly and deserves punishment.
C) Passively submitting to fate.
D) Not questioning any calamity.

Key Takeaway
God's decree pertains to the reality of acts, not their moral quality; disbelief is the servant's act, not God's decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a sin or disbelief in the world, do you find yourself becoming dissatisfied with God's decree? How does knowing that disbelief is the servant's act—not God's decree—free you to be both content with Allah's decree and intolerant of the sin itself? What is one area where you need to adjust your heart accordingly?
?
Examine your own life: have you ever subtly blamed God for a bad outcome that actually resulted from your own choices? The hadith warns that displeasure with decree is like seeking another lord. Where do you need to repent of that attitude today?
?
If being pleased with God's decree requires you to know that disbelief is evil, how deeply does that knowledge affect your own relationship with sin? Do you treat your own small sins with the same severity the passage describes? What step will you take today to cultivate a genuine detestation of disbelief in your heart?

The Verse 'Everything by a Measure' and the Nuance of Qadar
Maturidi examines the verse and defends a nuanced view of qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's nuanced interpretation of the verse 'We created everything by a measure' and how he distinguishes between what is fitting (qadar) and what is not fitting (disbelief) while affirming that disbelief is also encompassed by divine measure in a specific sense.

Core Idea
Not everything decreed is morally fitting, but everything has a measure from Allah.

This lesson explores a challenging passage from Imam Maturidi where he addresses a common question: does the verse 'We created everything by a measure' mean that even disbelief is decreed by Allah? Maturidi carefully navigates this by distinguishing between qadar as 'measure' and the moral nature of acts. He emphasizes that before answering such questions, one must clarify what the questioner means by 'decree' and 'measure'.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and initial statement
Begin by reading the verse 'Indeed, We have created everything by a measure' (54:49). Maturidi states that qadar (measure) pertains to what is fitting, while disbelief is not fitting. Ponder the apparent tension.
The verse is often used to support predestination.
2
Understand the two aspects of qadar
Notice that Maturidi says the statement about qadar is from one aspect (the aspect of measure) and from that aspect it is fitting. This indicates that disbelief, while not morally fitting, still has a measured existence and timing.
This is a key subtlety: moral disapproval is separate from existential decree.
3
Consider the rhetorical question
The passage then suggests it would be fitting for God's measure to be 'ugly and foul'? This seems ironic or rhetorical, highlighting that even if disbelief has a measure, it does not become morally good.
The phrase 'ugly and foul' intensifies the contrast.
4
Learn the correct method of answering
When asked 'Did God decree disbelief?', Maturidi insists that one must first ask the questioner what he means by 'decree' and 'measure'. Only then can a precise answer be given. Jumping to answer without clarification is negligence.
This underscores the importance of semantic clarity in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وفى قوله: إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ والقدر مما ينبغى، والكفر مما لا ينبغى، وإنما القول بقدر منه فمن الوجه الذي ذكرنا، ومن ذلك الوجه مما ينبغى. و بعد، فإنه ينبغى أن يكون قدره قبيحا سمجا، ثم قال: سألك هل قضى الله الكفر وقدر، يجب أن يستخبره عن المراد. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: فيقال: إذ وجب ذا تجميع ما أجبت قبل الاستخبار عنه إغفال ولا قوة إلا بالله
He said: And in His statement: 'Indeed, We have created everything by a measure'—and al-qadar is of what is fitting, and disbelief is of what is not fitting. The statement about qadar is from the aspect we mentioned, and from that aspect it is of what is fitting. And after that, it is fitting that His measure be ugly and foul? Then he said: He asked you: Did God decree and measure disbelief? It is necessary to ask him about the intended meaning. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: So it is said: Since this is required, the gathering of what you answered before the inquiry is negligence. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage continues the argument about the verse 'We have created everything by a measure' (54:49). The author (likely a disciple or the same) states that qadar pertains to what is appropriate (yambaghi), whereas disbelief is not appropriate (la yambaghi). However, the statement about qadar is from one aspect (the first aspect of measure) and from that aspect it is appropriate—meaning that even disbelief has a certain measure in terms of its existence and timing, though not in terms of its moral goodness. Then the author suggests that it would be fitting for God's measure to be ugly and foul? (perhaps a rhetorical statement). Then the question is posed: Did God decree and measure disbelief? The answer is that one must first ask the questioner what he means by 'qada' and 'qadar' (i.e., which sense). Abu Mansur (Maturidi) responds that if one must gather all that you answered before inquiring about the intended meaning, it would be careless. This likely means that one should clarify the meaning first before answering.

Key Terms
إنا كل شيء خلقناه بقدر
القدر مما ينبغى
الكفر مما لا ينبغى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'We created everything by a measure' implies that everything Allah decrees is morally good or pleasing to Him.
Correction: The verse speaks of existential measure, not moral approval. Disbelief is decreed in terms of its occurrence and timing, but it is not 'fitting' in a moral sense.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how should one respond to the question 'Did God decree disbelief?'
A) Yes, He decrees everything including disbelief in a morally approving sense.
B) No, He does not decree evil at all.
C) One must first ask what the questioner means by 'qada' and 'qadar'.
D) The question is invalid and should not be answered.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'We created everything by a measure' affirms that all things, including disbelief, have a precise divine measure, but this does not imply moral approval; theological questions require clarifying the intended meaning of terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a painful event in your life that you know was decreed by Allah. Do you secretly harbor resentment because you feel the event was 'unfitting' or 'wrong'? How does this lesson's distinction between existential measure and moral approval challenge you to realign your heart with true submission (tawakkul)?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a sin, do you ever think, 'Since Allah decreed it, it must be acceptable'? This passage warns against conflating decree with approval. How can you cultivate compassion without compromising your moral stance, and what specific step will you take this week to correct this tendency in yourself?
?
The passage criticizes answering a question without first clarifying the intended meaning. Recall a recent disagreement you had about religion—did you assume what the other person meant by their words? Are you willing to practice the discipline of asking 'What do you mean by that?' before responding, even when you feel strongly? Write down one concrete situation where you will apply this.

Being Pleased with Allah's Decree
Maturidi explains the hadith on rida bi qada' and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means to be pleased with Allah's decree according to Imam Maturidi, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Pleasure with Allah's decree means recognizing the evil of sin, not passive acceptance of all events.

This lesson presents a key hadith about being pleased with Allah's decree and its interpretation by Imam Maturidi. The text explains that true pleasure involves acknowledging that disbelief is evil and leads to punishment, and it distinguishes between Allah's decree and human actions. It also addresses the Mu'tazilite view, showing that the hadith challenges their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its explanation
Start by reading the sacred hadith where Allah says whoever is not pleased with His decree or patient with His trial should seek another lord. Then read Maturidi's explanation that this is similar to a previous point.
Notice that the hadith sets the stage for defining what it means to be pleased.
2
Understand the definition of 'being pleased'
Maturidi defines being pleased with Allah's decree as knowing that disbelief is evil and perishing, and that it incurs Allah's wrath and punishment unless one repents. Understand that this is an intellectual and moral acknowledgment, not emotional acceptance of all events.
This definition links pleasure with correct belief about the nature of disbelief.
3
Distinguish between divine decree and human action
The text argues that disbelief and evil are acts of the servant, not Allah's decree. Allah's decree pertains to the reality of the act, not its moral quality. Grasp this theological distinction.
This is a key Maturidi principle to avoid attributing evil to Allah.
4
Examine the refutation of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila hold that Allah does not decree spiritual afflictions like disbelief, only material ones with compensation. The hadith challenges them: if they are not pleased with such decrees, they would be seeking another lord. Reflect on how this refutes their position.
The Mu'tazila are forced to accept either Allah's decree or seek another lord.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بما روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال، قال تعالى: خبره من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليتخذ ربا سواي. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: هذا مثل الأول، وإن الرضا بقضائه أن تعلم بأن الكفر مضمحل قبيح وأنه شر وفساد وأنه يوجب مقت صاحبه وتعذيبه إلا أن يتوب، فمن لم يرض بهذا فهو كافر، فيكون على ما جاء به الخبر. على أن الكفر والقبح هو فعل العبد، ومحال أن يكون هو قضاؤه، فثبت أن قضاء الله هو ما ذكرت مما عليه حقيقة الفعل، ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن حقيقة الخير في الأمراض والمصائب ألا ترى أن التخليد في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة وكذلك الخذلان والإضلال ونحو ذلك، فليرض الكعبي لنفسه ذلك، وإلا طلب ربا سواه. والمعتزلة يقولون: ليس لله القضاء بالأمراض والمصائب في الدين، لا ذنب لهم إلا بالعوض، فإذا لا يرضون بها حتى يعطوا عليها العوض، وذلك معنى ما روى فليتخذ ربا سواي. وقال: علينا الرضا بقضاء الله. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقد بينا كيف يرضى به، وما عليه في ذلك أيضا.
He argued with what was narrated from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, that he said: Allah, exalted, said: 'Let him who is not pleased with My decree and does not bear My trial patiently seek a lord other than Me.' The faqih, may Allah have mercy on him, said: This is like the first one, and being pleased with His decree is that you know that disbelief is perishing and evil, and that it is corruption and mischief, and that it necessitates the wrath of its possessor and his punishment unless he repents. So whoever is not pleased with this is a disbeliever, and thus it is according to what the report came with. Moreover, disbelief and evil are the act of the servant, and it is impossible that they be His decree. So it is established that Allah's decree is what I mentioned regarding the reality of the act. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, the reality of good in illnesses and afflictions is that you do not see that eternal punishment in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, as well as abandonment and misguidance and the like. So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with that for himself, otherwise he would seek a lord other than Him. And the Mu'tazila say: Allah does not decree illnesses and afflictions in religion; they have no sin except with compensation. So they are not pleased with them until they are given compensation for them, and that is the meaning of what was narrated: 'Let him seek a lord other than Me.' And he said: It is incumbent upon us to be pleased with Allah's decree. The shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: We have already explained how to be pleased with it, and what is upon him in that also.
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins by citing a hadith about being pleased with Allah's decree. Then it explains that being pleased with His decree means acknowledging that disbelief is evil and leads to punishment, so whoever is not pleased with that is a disbeliever. It then argues that disbelief is the servant's action, not Allah's decree, so the decree is about the reality of the act. Then it discusses the Mu'tazila position that Allah does not decree spiritual afflictions, and they only accept material afflictions with compensation, so the hadith applies to them. The shaykh notes that the explanation of how to be pleased has been given.

الرضا بقضاء الله
القضاء
الكفر
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think being pleased with Allah's decree means accepting all events, including sin and injustice, as good without any moral discernment.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that being pleased means recognizing that disbelief is evil and deserving of punishment, not endorsing the act itself. It is about acknowledging the moral reality, not passive acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does being pleased with Allah's decree primarily involve?
A) Accepting all events without complaint
B) Knowing that disbelief is evil and leads to punishment
C) Never experiencing hardship
D) Denying that evil exists

Key Takeaway
True pleasure with Allah's decree is to acknowledge that disbelief is evil and leads to punishment, while recognizing that evil acts are from the servant, not from Allah's decree.
