The Two Aspects of Qadar Regarding Disbelief
Maturidi argues that disbelief has two aspects of qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes between the creative and moral aspects of qadar to explain the relationship between God's decree and disbelief.

Core Idea
Allah creates everything, but moral evil is not from His approval.

This passage tackles a challenging theological question: if Allah creates all things, including disbelief, does that make disbelief something He approves? Maturidi answers by drawing a crucial distinction between qadar in the sense of creation and qadar in the sense of moral appropriateness. Read on to see how he navigates this delicate issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of argument.
The Arabic has an unclear word marked [?] – do not worry about it yet.
2
Identify the two aspects of qadar
Notice that the passage mentions qadar from 'the aspect we mentioned' (creative) and distinguishes it from moral appropriateness. Find where this distinction is made.
The verse 'Indeed, all things We created with qadar' is the starting point.
3
Analyze the question and response
The passage presents a question: 'Did Allah decree disbelief?' and then gives Maturidi's response. Understand why the response involves asking about the intended meaning.
The key is that without clarifying which aspect of qadar is meant, the answer can be misleading.
4
Reflect on the methodological point
Maturidi criticizes those who answer without first inquiring about the question's intent. Consider why this caution is important in theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وفي قوله: إنا كل شيء خلقناه بقدر. والقدر مما ينبغي، والكفر مما لا ينبغي، وإنما القول بقدر منه فمن الوجه الذي ذكرنا، ومن ذلك الوجه مما ينبغي. وبعد، فإنه ينبغي أن يكون قدره قبيحا سمجا. ثم قال: سألك هل قضى الله الكفر وقدر [؟]، يجب أن يستخبره عن المراد. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: فيقال: إذ وجب ذا تجميع ما أجبت قبل الاستخبار عنه إغفال. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: And in His saying: 'Indeed, all things We created with qadar.' Qadar is what is appropriate, and disbelief is not appropriate. Rather, the statement about qadar from it is from the aspect we mentioned, and from that aspect it is appropriate. After that, it is appropriate that its qadar be ugly and displeasing. Then he said: He asked you: Did Allah decree disbelief and decree [؟]? He must ask about the intended meaning. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It is said: When this is necessary, [the one who] compiled what you answered before asking about it is negligent. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the meaning of qadar in relation to evil. It quotes a verse that all things are created with qadar, then notes that qadar is appropriate, but disbelief is not appropriate. So the qadar of disbelief is from one aspect (creation) appropriate, and from another aspect (moral) not appropriate. Then a question is posed: did God decree disbelief? The answer is that one must clarify the intended meaning of the question. The shaykh then says that if one does not distinguish the two aspects, they are negligent.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الكفر
التقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that if Allah creates disbelief, then He must approve of it or decree it in a moral sense.
Correction: Allah creates disbelief, but He does not approve of it; qadar has a creative aspect (everything created) and a moral aspect (what He commands and loves). Disbelief falls only under the creative aspect.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how is disbelief related to qadar?
A) Disbelief is from qadar in the sense of creation
B) Disbelief is not from qadar at all
C) Qadar only applies to good deeds
D) There is no relationship

Key Takeaway
Allah's creating disbelief does not mean He approves of it; qadar has both a creative and a moral dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says disbelief's qadar is 'ugly and displeasing' from the moral aspect. When you look at your own shortcomings, do you see them as Allah's decree but also as morally ugly? How will you respond to them—by resigning yourself, or by seeking His forgiveness and changing?

Divine Decree

6 min
The First Reason: Divine Decree Follows Human Choice
Maturidi enumerates first proof that qadar is not an excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine decree cannot be an excuse based on the fact that God's decree is contingent upon His foreknowledge of human choice and preference.

Core Idea
God's decree is based on His knowledge of your choice; you cannot use it as an excuse.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing that the very act of divine decree and creation is rooted in God's eternal knowledge of what humans will choose and prefer. This first reason demonstrates that since the decree is posterior to God's knowledge of human choice, it cannot serve as a justification for wrongdoing – after all, the one who acts is the same one who freely prefers the action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في القضاء والقدر والتخليق والإرادة أن لا عذر لأحد بذلك لأوجه ثلاثة:
أحدها أن الله تعالى قضى وخلق، وما ذكر لما علم أن ذلك يختار ويؤثر، وبما أراد وخلق وقضى يصلون إليه ويبلغون ما أثروه، فلم يكن لهم الاحتجاج بما هو آثر الأشياء عندهم وأخيرها، على ما لم يكن لهم ذلك بالعلم والكتاب والإخبار إذ كانت بالتي يكون منهم مختارين مؤثرين، وبالله نستعين.
والثاني إن جميع ما كان لم يحملهم على ما هم عليه، لو لم يكن شيء من ذلك، الأفعال، فعلوه، لم يدفعهم إليه ولا اضطرهم بل هم على ما ويتوهم كونهم بلا ما ذكرت، وقد مكنوا أيضا من مضادات ما عملوا، فما ذلك إذ لم يضطرهم ولم يحول عنهم حقيقة بما علم كل منهم إنه مختار مؤثر فاعل ممكن من البرك [؟]، لا كخلق سائر الجواهر والأعراض والأوقات والأمكنة التي فيها تقع وإن لم يحتمل كون شيء من ذلك عذرا لهم أو حجة لم يكن ما نحن فيه حجة أو عذرا، والله الموفق.
والثالث إنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم ولا كانوا عند أنفسهم وقت الفعل إنهم يفعلون لشيء من ذلك، فالاحتجاج لما ليس لذلك الفعل عند المحتج باطل وكذلك العذر بما لم يكن عند نفسه بالذي يفعل لكان ذلك باطل مضمحل، ولو كان لهم بذلك احتجاج لكان لهم بالإخبار وبالعلم والتقوية ونحو ذلك احتجاج على أن لهم لو كان هذا اعتذار لكان لهم بما جهلوا الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد وبما جهلوا موقع مآتمهم بالمحل الذي وقعت، ولكان لهم بما لا يضر الله ولا ينقص ملكه عذر، ولو كان لهم بذلك عذر لكان بما خلقهم على العلم بما يكون منهم عذر ولو كان لهم في ذلك احتجاج لكان بما هو واضح لهم كله، وهو الذي يكون مثله وقت الفعل متصوراً في الوهم من نحو الكرم والجود والغنى عن تعذيبهم وبما هو عفو غفور وبما ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في ضرر، فإذا لم يكن الاحتجاج بشيء من ذلك لم يكن في الأول. فإن من ذلك
The principle regarding qada', qadar, creation, and will is that no one has an excuse on account of them, for three reasons:
First: Allah, exalted, decreed and created, and what was mentioned is because He knew that that [action] would be chosen and preferred, and by what He willed, created, and decreed, they attain and reach what they preferred. So they cannot argue by that which is the most preferred and best thing according to them, just as they cannot argue by knowledge, the Book, and informing, since those [actions] come from them as choosing and preferring. And with Allah we seek help.
Second: All that was did not compel them to what they are upon. Even if none of that had been, they would still have performed the actions. It did not push them nor compel them; rather they are upon what they are, and they imagine themselves without what was mentioned. And they have also been enabled to do opposites of what they did. So since it did not compel them nor remove from them the reality [of choice] by what each of them knows that he is a chooser, preferrer, doer, capable of righteousness [؟], unlike the creation of other substances, accidents, times, and places in which [actions] occur; and if none of that could be an excuse or a proof for them, then what we are in cannot be a proof or an excuse. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Third: None of that occurred to their minds, nor were they at the time of the act thinking that they were acting because of any of that. So arguing on the basis of something that is not relevant to the act is false, and likewise excusing oneself by what was not in one's own mind at the time of action is false and vain. And if they could argue by that, they could argue by informing, knowledge, empowerment and the like, that they would have an excuse even if they were ignorant of the command, prohibition, promise, and threat, and ignorant of the location of their misfortunes in the place where they occurred. And they would have an excuse by that which does not harm Allah nor diminish His kingdom. And if they had an excuse by that, they would have an excuse by His creating them with knowledge of what would be from them. And if they had an argument in that, they would argue by what is clear to them all, which is something like at the time of the act it is imagined in the mind, such as generosity, bounty, independence from punishing them, and His being Pardoning and Forgiving, and that there is no benefit for Him in their obedience nor harm in their disobedience. So if they cannot argue by any of that, then they cannot argue by the first [i.e., qadar]. For from that...
Source pages p. 908

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three proofs that no one can excuse themselves by divine decree. The first proof: God knows that humans choose and prefer their actions, so they cannot use the decree as an excuse since they themselves prefer those actions. The second proof: The decree did not compel them; they had the ability to do otherwise and were not forced; they are aware of their own choice. The third proof: At the moment of action, they were not thinking of the decree as a cause, so it is invalid to use it as an excuse. Then an additional reductio argument: if they could use the decree as an excuse, they could use many other things (like God's forgiveness, His independence, etc.) as excuses, but since those are not valid, neither is this.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الاختيار
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine decree removes human responsibility because God decreed the action.
Correction: This passage clarifies that the decree is based on God's knowledge of the human's own choice. Therefore, the responsibility lies with the human who chose and preferred the action, not with the decree.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, why can't a person use qadar as an excuse?
A) Because qadar is hidden from humans
B) Because humans choose and prefer their actions, and the decree follows that choice
C) Because qadar only applies to good deeds
D) Because Allah's decree is irresistible

Key Takeaway
The first reason: divine decree is not an excuse because it is grounded in God's foreknowledge of the human's own free choice and preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you find yourself internally excusing it because 'it was written'? How does Maturidi's first reason directly confront that excuse? What would it cost you to stop using that excuse and take full responsibility for your choice?
?
Consider a sin you committed recently. Before you acted, did you prefer it? According to this passage, your preference is exactly what God knew and decreed. How does that change the way you view your own responsibility?
?
The passage says you cannot argue by the decree just as you cannot argue by God's knowledge. Have you ever used 'God knows my heart' as a shield? How does this analogy remove that shield?

The Second Reason: No Compulsion from Decree
Maturidi presents second proof: decree does not compel, humans have alternatives and self-awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine decree does not compel human actions; humans have the ability to do otherwise and are aware of their own choice, so the decree cannot be an excuse.

Core Idea
Decree does not force you; you could have done otherwise, so you cannot blame qadar.

Imam Maturidi's second argument tackles the idea of compulsion directly. He states that all that Allah decreed did not compel people to act. Even if there were no decree, they would still perform actions by their own choice. Moreover, they have the ability to do the opposite of what they did. This demonstrates that the decree is not a coercive cause, and thus it cannot serve as a valid excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في القضاء والقدر والتخليق والإرادة أن لا عذر لأحد بذلك لأوجه ثلاثة:
أحدها أن الله تعالى قضى وخلق، وما ذكر لما علم أن ذلك يختار ويؤثر، وبما أراد وخلق وقضى يصلون إليه ويبلغون ما أثروه، فلم يكن لهم الاحتجاج بما هو آثر الأشياء عندهم وأخيرها، على ما لم يكن لهم ذلك بالعلم والكتاب والإخبار إذ كانت بالتي يكون منهم مختارين مؤثرين، وبالله نستعين.
والثاني إن جميع ما كان لم يحملهم على ما هم عليه، لو لم يكن شيء من ذلك، الأفعال، فعلوه، لم يدفعهم إليه ولا اضطرهم بل هم على ما ويتوهم كونهم بلا ما ذكرت، وقد مكنوا أيضا من مضادات ما عملوا، فما ذلك إذ لم يضطرهم ولم يحول عنهم حقيقة بما علم كل منهم إنه مختار مؤثر فاعل ممكن من البرك [؟]، لا كخلق سائر الجواهر والأعراض والأوقات والأمكنة التي فيها تقع وإن لم يحتمل كون شيء من ذلك عذرا لهم أو حجة لم يكن ما نحن فيه حجة أو عذرا، والله الموفق.
والثالث إنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم ولا كانوا عند أنفسهم وقت الفعل إنهم يفعلون لشيء من ذلك، فالاحتجاج لما ليس لذلك الفعل عند المحتج باطل وكذلك العذر بما لم يكن عند نفسه بالذي يفعل لكان ذلك باطل مضمحل، ولو كان لهم بذلك احتجاج لكان لهم بالإخبار وبالعلم والتقوية ونحو ذلك احتجاج على أن لهم لو كان هذا اعتذار لكان لهم بما جهلوا الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد وبما جهلوا موقع مآتمهم بالمحل الذي وقعت، ولكان لهم بما لا يضر الله ولا ينقص ملكه عذر، ولو كان لهم بذلك عذر لكان بما خلقهم على العلم بما يكون منهم عذر ولو كان لهم في ذلك احتجاج لكان بما هو واضح لهم كله، وهو الذي يكون مثله وقت الفعل متصوراً في الوهم من نحو الكرم والجود والغنى عن تعذيبهم وبما هو عفو غفور وبما ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في ضرر، فإذا لم يكن الاحتجاج بشيء من ذلك لم يكن في الأول. فإن من ذلك
The principle regarding qada', qadar, creation, and will is that no one has an excuse on account of them, for three reasons:
First: Allah, exalted, decreed and created, and what was mentioned is because He knew that that [action] would be chosen and preferred, and by what He willed, created, and decreed, they attain and reach what they preferred. So they cannot argue by that which is the most preferred and best thing according to them, just as they cannot argue by knowledge, the Book, and informing, since those [actions] come from them as choosing and preferring. And with Allah we seek help.
Second: All that was did not compel them to what they are upon. Even if none of that had been, they would still have performed the actions. It did not push them nor compel them; rather they are upon what they are, and they imagine themselves without what was mentioned. And they have also been enabled to do opposites of what they did. So since it did not compel them nor remove from them the reality [of choice] by what each of them knows that he is a chooser, preferrer, doer, capable of righteousness [؟], unlike the creation of other substances, accidents, times, and places in which [actions] occur; and if none of that could be an excuse or a proof for them, then what we are in cannot be a proof or an excuse. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Third: None of that occurred to their minds, nor were they at the time of the act thinking that they were acting because of any of that. So arguing on the basis of something that is not relevant to the act is false, and likewise excusing oneself by what was not in one's own mind at the time of action is false and vain. And if they could argue by that, they could argue by informing, knowledge, empowerment and the like, that they would have an excuse even if they were ignorant of the command, prohibition, promise, and threat, and ignorant of the location of their misfortunes in the place where they occurred. And they would have an excuse by that which does not harm Allah nor diminish His kingdom. And if they had an excuse by that, they would have an excuse by His creating them with knowledge of what would be from them. And if they had an argument in that, they would argue by what is clear to them all, which is something like at the time of the act it is imagined in the mind, such as generosity, bounty, independence from punishing them, and His being Pardoning and Forgiving, and that there is no benefit for Him in their obedience nor harm in their disobedience. So if they cannot argue by any of that, then they cannot argue by the first [i.e., qadar]. For from that...
Source pages p. 908

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three proofs that no one can excuse themselves by divine decree. The first proof: God knows that humans choose and prefer their actions, so they cannot use the decree as an excuse since they themselves prefer those actions. The second proof: The decree did not compel them; they had the ability to do otherwise and were not forced; they are aware of their own choice. The third proof: At the moment of action, they were not thinking of the decree as a cause, so it is invalid to use it as an excuse. Then an additional reductio argument: if they could use the decree as an excuse, they could use many other things (like God's forgiveness, His independence, etc.) as excuses, but since those are not valid, neither is this.

القضاء
القدر
الاختيار
الإكراه
التمكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that divine decree means humans are forced to sin, leaving them no alternative.
Correction: This passage affirms that humans have the ability to do opposites and are conscious of their own choice. The decree does not remove this ability, so it is not compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the second reason, what proves that the decree did not compel humans?
A) They are forced to act
B) They have the ability to do the opposite of what they did
C) The decree is inescapable
D) They do not think about the decree

Key Takeaway
The second reason: divine decree does not compel human actions, and humans retain the ability to choose otherwise, so the decree is not an excuse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says you know yourself to be a chooser. In which area of your life are you pretending you have no choice when you actually do? Name one specific action you can change starting tomorrow.
?
If inanimate things cannot use their creation as an excuse, how much less can you, who were given choice and ability? What will you stop excusing starting now?

Divine Decree

The Third Reason and Reductio: Irrelevance of Decree in the Agent's Mind
Maturidi presents third proof and a reductio ad absurdum against using decree as excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key arguments: (a) people do not think of qadar when they act, so it cannot be their motive; (b) even if they did, a reductio shows that accepting qadar as an excuse would lead to many absurd excuses, so it is invalid.

Core Idea
You don't think of qadar when you sin, and if you could use it, you could use anything as an excuse.

Imam Maturidi's third reason combines a psychological observation with a logical argument. First, he notes that at the moment of committing a sin, the agent does not have the decree in mind as a cause – they simply act. Second, he imagines hypothetically that someone does use the decree as an excuse: then they could also use many other things (knowledge, book, empowerment, ignorance, God's generosity, etc.) as excuses, all of which are obviously invalid. Therefore, the original claim must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في القضاء والقدر والتخليق والإرادة أن لا عذر لأحد بذلك لأوجه ثلاثة:
أحدها أن الله تعالى قضى وخلق، وما ذكر لما علم أن ذلك يختار ويؤثر، وبما أراد وخلق وقضى يصلون إليه ويبلغون ما أثروه، فلم يكن لهم الاحتجاج بما هو آثر الأشياء عندهم وأخيرها، على ما لم يكن لهم ذلك بالعلم والكتاب والإخبار إذ كانت بالتي يكون منهم مختارين مؤثرين، وبالله نستعين.

والثالث إنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم ولا كانوا عند أنفسهم وقت الفعل إنهم يفعلون لشيء من ذلك، فالاحتجاج لما ليس لذلك الفعل عند المحتج باطل وكذلك العذر بما لم يكن عند نفسه بالذي يفعل لكان ذلك باطل مضمحل، ولو كان لهم بذلك احتجاج لكان لهم بالإخبار وبالعلم والتقوية ونحو ذلك احتجاج على أن لهم لو كان هذا اعتذار لكان لهم بما جهلوا الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد وبما جهلوا موقع مآتمهم بالمحل الذي وقعت، ولكان لهم بما لا يضر الله ولا ينقص ملكه عذر، ولو كان لهم بذلك عذر لكان بما خلقهم على العلم بما يكون منهم عذر ولو كان لهم في ذلك احتجاج لكان بما هو واضح لهم كله، وهو الذي يكون مثله وقت الفعل متصوراً في الوهم من نحو الكرم والجود والغنى عن تعذيبهم وبما هو عفو غفور وبما ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في ضرر، فإذا لم يكن الاحتجاج بشيء من ذلك لم يكن في الأول. فإن من ذلك

Refutation of Misunderstanding 'Good and Evil are from God'
Maturidi refutes a claim about the Muslim statement 'good and evil are from God'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of the Muslim statement 'good and evil are from God' and how it distinguishes between the decree of evil and actual evil acts.

Core Idea
The decree of evil is from God, but evil itself is not from God.

Imam Maturidi refutes a claim that the Muslim saying 'good and evil are from God' was only meant to oppose heretics and did not concern the acts of servants. He clarifies that Muslims affirm predestination of good and evil, but the decree of evil is not evil itself. This distinction upholds God's wisdom and justice while affirming human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the strong refutation language ('lie').
The passage contains a claim and a counter-argument.
2
Identify the claim and the refutation
The claim is that Muslims only meant to oppose heretics, not to include acts of servants. Maturidi refutes it, stating the real meaning: the decree of good and evil is from God, but the decree of evil is not evil itself.
The faqih calls the claim a lie.
3
Understand the key distinction
Note the crucial distinction: God's decree of evil is not evil itself. Only those who perform evil are evil. This protects God's attribute of being All-Wise and All-Knowing.
This is a central point in discussions of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: قول المسلمين: الخير والشر من الله إنما أرادوا مخالفة قول الزنادقة. وأما فعل العباد لم يخطر ببالهم، بل قال الله: من عمل الشيطان. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: مما ذكر من قول المسلمين فهو كذب، بل يقولون: قدر الخير والشر من الله، وقدر الشر ليس هو الشر، ولا كان القول في شأن الزنادقة لكان إذا قبيح إضافة الشر إلى الحكيم العليم، بل [من] فعله الشر فهو شرير، وسَن فعله الإفساد فهو مفسد. وقوله: "لم يخطر ببالهم"، كذب، بل لا يخطر خصوص الذى ذكر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: The Muslims' saying: 'Good and evil are from God' — they only intended to oppose the saying of the heretics (zanadiqa). As for the acts of servants, it did not occur to their minds. Rather, God said: 'It is from the work of Satan.' The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: What is mentioned from the Muslims' saying is a lie. Rather, they say: The decree of good and evil is from God, and the decree of evil is not evil itself. And if the statement concerning the heretics were [as claimed], then how ugly is the attribution of evil to the All-Wise, All-Knowing! Rather, whoever does evil is evil, and whoever does corruption is corrupt. And his statement: 'It did not occur to their minds' is a lie. Rather, it does not occur specifically what was mentioned. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim made about the Muslim statement 'good and evil are from God'. The claim is that Muslims meant only to oppose heretics (zanadiqa) who denied predestination, and that they did not mean that the actual acts of servants are from God; rather, God says evil is from Satan. The faqih (the author or a quoted authority) strongly refutes this, calling it a lie. He asserts that Muslims actually say: the decree (qadar) of good and evil is from God, and the decree of evil is not evil itself. This is a crucial distinction: predestination of evil is not the same as attributing evil acts to God. Then the faqih argues that even if the claim were about heretics, attributing evil to God (even as decree) would be ugly because God is All-Wise and All-Knowing; only those who do evil are evil, and those who do corruption are corrupt. He then denies that the claim 'it did not occur to their minds' is true; rather, it does not occur specifically what was mentioned (i.e., the actual evil acts). The passage emphasizes that evil is not attributed to God even as a decree in the sense of actual evil, but only as preordination which is not evil itself.

Key Terms
الخير والشر (al-khayr wa al-sharr)
قدر (qadar)
الزنادقة (al-zanadiqa)
الحكيم العليم (al-hakim al-alim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Muslims who say 'good and evil are from God' mean that evil acts are directly from God.
Correction: The passage clarifies they mean predestination of evil, not the evil itself. The decree of evil is from God, but evil itself is not attributed to Him.

Checkpoint
What do Muslims really mean when they say 'good and evil are from God' according to the faqih?
A) They mean evil acts are directly from God
B) They mean the decree of good and evil is from God, and the decree of evil is not evil itself
C) They mean God creates evil acts
D) They mean only good is from God

Key Takeaway
Muslims affirm that the decree of good and evil is from God, but the decree of evil is not evil itself; only those who do evil are evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something bad happens in your life, do you instinctively blame God or feel that He has wronged you? How can you train your heart to see His decree as distinct from the evil that may result from your own choices?

The passage says 'whoever does evil is evil.' Think of a sin you committed recently. Did you fully accept responsibility, or did you rationalize it by saying 'Allah wrote it'? What would it take for you to sincerely repent and own that sin?
?
If you truly believed that the decree of evil is not evil, and that evil only comes from your own actions, would you live differently? Identify one specific habit or attitude you would change starting today.

Kufr is Not from God in Any Way
Maturidi denies any attribution of evil to God, even by creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi denies that kufr or any evil can be attributed to God, even by way of creation, and what principle governs proper attribution.

Core Idea
Evil and foul things cannot be attributed to God, even though He creates them.

In response to an objection that kufr is from God by creation (al-khalq) rather than command (al-amr), Imam Maturidi delivers a categorical denial. He establishes a fundamental principle: only command and blessings can be properly attributed to God; foul things like Iblis, Satan, and dung are never attributed to Him, despite being created. This lesson examines that principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objector concedes command but insists on creation, and how Maturidi rejects any attribution.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا نقول الكفر من الله من طريق'.
2
Analyze the principle of attribution
Find the sentence that begins with 'والأصل في ذلك'. This states the principle: attribution to God must be in the manner of claiming command or attributing blessings.
3
Consider the examples of foul things
Maturidi lists examples like Iblis, Satan, dung, and impurities. Ask yourself why these are not attributed to God despite being created.
The principle is that such things are only mentioned with contempt, so they cannot be attributed to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: فإن قيل: لا أقول الكفر من الله من جهة الأمر، ولكن نقول من جهة الخلق. قال: الأمر دون الفعل. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فنقول: لا نقول الكفر من الله من طريق، ولا شر بإطلاق القول من الله، وكذلك من الله. وكذلك لا أحد يقول: إبليس من الله، أو الشيطان من الله، أو كل قذر ونتن من الله، أو كل فساد ثبت أن هذا اللفظ فاسد فيما كانت الخلق أيضا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل في ذلك أن القول منه يخرج مخرج دعوى الأمر أو إضافة الأنعام، وليس في ذلك واحد منها البتة، فلا يجوز الإضافة إليه، وهو كما قلنا: إن الله في التحقيق وإن كان رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء وخالق كل شيء، وكل شيء له، لا يقال ذلك في الأرواث والخبائث والشيطان ونحو ذلك من الأشياء التي لا تذكر أنفسها إلا على الاستحقاق بها، فإضافتها الواحد يخرج على ذلك، وإن كانت في أنها مخلوقة كفرها مما يضاف إلى الله، فمثله الذي نحن فيه، ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى هذا يكره القول في الكفر والمعاصي إنها بقضاء الله وقدره وإرادته لوجهين: أحدهما ما ذكر من القبح، أو هي لا تذكر إلا على الاستقباح والاستهانة، والذي ذلك وصفه لا يضاف إلى الله تعالى على ما أخبرت، وإن كان في التحقيق من قول ووجه آخر إنه يتكلم به على الاعتذار والاحتجاج، ذلك المفهوم منه، وقد بينا أن لا عذر لهم في ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he said: If it is said: I do not say that kufr is from God from the aspect of command, but I say from the aspect of creation. He said: Command is less than action. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: So we say: We do not say that kufr is from God in any way, nor evil by absolute speech from God, nor [anything] like that from God. Likewise, no one says: Iblis is from God, or Satan is from God, or every filth and impurity is from God, or every corruption—it is established that this wording is corrupt even in what was created as well. And there is no power except by God. The principle in that is that speech [attributing something] from Him comes out in the manner of claiming command or attribution of blessings, and none of that is present at all, so attribution to Him is not permissible. And it is as we said: God in reality, even though He is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, the Creator of everything, and everything belongs to Him, one does not say that concerning dung, impurities, Satan, and the like of those things that are not mentioned except with deserved contempt. So attributing them to the One [God] comes out according to that, even if they are created—their [؟] which is attributed to God, so the like of it is what we are in. And there is no power except by God. And upon this, it is disliked to say about kufr and sins that they are by God's decree, predestination, and will for two reasons: one is what was mentioned of ugliness, or they are not mentioned except with disdain and contempt, and whatever has that description is not attributed to God Almighty as I have informed, even if in reality it is [such] from speech and another aspect—that one speaks about it in the way of excuse and argumentation, that is understood from it, and we have explained that there is no excuse for them in that. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding whether kufr (disbelief) is from God by way of creation (al-khalq), not command. The objector concedes that kufr is not from God by command but suggests it is by creation. The reply (from 'the Shaykh') categorically denies that kufr or any evil is from God in any way. The author argues that no one would attribute Iblis, Satan, filth, or corruption to God, and such language is corrupt even if those things are created. The principle is that attribution to God must be in the manner of claiming command (da'wa al-amr) or attribution of blessings (idafat al-an'am); since neither applies to evil and foul things, they cannot be attributed to God. Even though God is truly the Lord, God, and Creator of everything, we do not attribute dung, impurities, or Satan to Him because such things are only mentioned with deserved contempt (istiḥqāq). The passage then explains why it is disliked to say that kufr and sins are by God's decree (qaḍā'), predestination (qadar), and will (irāda) for two reasons: (1) the ugliness (qubḥ) of such attribution, and (2) because such things are only mentioned with disdain and contempt, so they should not be attributed to God. The author notes that sometimes such speech is used in the context of excuse or argumentation, but that understanding has been refuted—there is no excuse for such attribution.

Key Terms
الكفر (al-kufr)
الخلق (al-khalq)
الأمر (al-amr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That since God creates everything, disbelief and evil are attributable to Him as created acts.
Correction: Creation does not imply attribution; only command and blessings allow attribution to God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what principle governs whether something can be attributed to God?
A) If it is created, it can be attributed.
B) Only if it is a commanded act or a blessing.
C) Only if it is good.
D) If it exists.

Key Takeaway
Evil and foul things, though created by God, are never attributed to Him because proper attribution requires the thing to be a command or blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When I face hardship or see evil in the world, do I ever, even in my heart, blame Allah or feel that He is the source of that evil? How does this lesson expose that hidden thought, and what will I do to replace it with trust in His perfect wisdom?
?
If I am told not to attribute evil to Allah, then whom do I naturally blame for my own sins or misfortunes? Could I be attributing evil to His decree in a way that lessens my personal responsibility? What concrete step will I take today to align my speech and belief with this teaching?
?
The passage says that foul things are only mentioned with contempt. When I complain about my difficulties, do my words reflect contempt for what Allah has decreed? How can I train myself to only speak of Allah's decree as a blessing or command, never as evil?

Divine Decree
intermediate
4 min
Why Attributing Sins to God's Decree is Disliked
Maturidi gives two reasons for disliking the attribution of sins to God's decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons Imam Maturidi gives for disliking the attribution of kufr and sins to God's decree, predestination, and will.

Core Idea
Sins are mentioned with contempt, so they cannot be attributed to God, even if He decrees them.

Having established that evil and foul things cannot be attributed to God, Imam Maturidi now addresses a common practice: saying that sins are by God's decree (qaḍā'), predestination (qadar), and will (irāda). He provides two specific reasons why this wording is disliked, rooted in the ugliness of such attribution and the fact that sins are only mentioned with disdain. This lesson unpacks those reasons.

Guided Path
1
Read the two reasons
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the phrase 'لوجهين'. Identify both reasons.
The first reason is 'القبح' (ugliness); the second is that sins are mentioned with 'الاستقباح والاستهانة' (disdain and contempt).
2
Analyze each reason
Consider why each reason makes it improper to attribute sins to God. The first prevents associating ugliness with God; the second respects that sins are only spoken of with contempt.
3
Reflect on the exception
Maturidi notes that such speech can be used in argumentation (اعتذار واحتجاج) but clarifies that there is no excuse for it. Think about why.
The exception does not justify the attribution; it only acknowledges its occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What are the two reasons Maturidi gives for disliking the attribution of sins to God's decree?
A) Ugliness and that sins are temporary.
B) Ugliness and that sins are mentioned with contempt.
C) That sins are not created and God doesn't will them.
D) That God does not decree sins.

Key Takeaway
Attributing sins to God's decree is disliked because it associates ugliness with God and because sins are only mentioned with contempt.

?

?
If you truly believed that attributing your sins to Allah's decree is disliked (makruh), what would change in how you speak about your own failures?
?
The author says sins are 'only mentioned with disdain and contempt.' When you recount a past sin to yourself, do you feel that same contempt, or do you downplay it? Will you commit to never using divine decree as a shield for your wrong actions?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Qadariyya on Divine Misguidance
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's claim that misguidance is attributed to Allah only metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical arguments refuting the Qadariyya's claim that misguidance is attributed to Allah only metaphorically through trial or naming.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya's attribution of misguidance by trial is logically inconsistent and contradicts the Quran.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi directly addresses a key sectarian dispute with the Qadariyya, who argued that misguidance is not truly caused by Allah but only attributed to Him by way of trial or naming. Maturidi exposes the internal contradictions in their reasoning and reaffirms the principle that all actions are created by Allah in a way consistent with His wisdom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Note the dense theological terminology.
Pay special attention to the terms 'mihna' (trial) and 'takhliya' (abandonment).
2
Identify the Qadariyya claim
Find where Maturidi reports the Qadariyya's position on how misguidance and good are attributed to Allah. Understand their distinction.
They attribute misguidance by trial/abandonment and good by command/strengthening.
3
Analyze Maturidi's critiques
Look for two main counter-arguments: the inconsistency in attribution logic (why not apply same to good?) and the contradiction over prohibition (why attribute misguidance if prohibited?).
Also note the refutation of the 'naming' alternative.
4
Understand the foundational principle
Examine the concluding principle: Allah's action is to create, whether graciously or justly. This underpins the entire refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : قالت القدرية : فيما أضيف إلى الله الإضلال والإزاغة و صرف القلوب فما قال : صَرَفَ اللهُ قلوبهم ، ونحو ذلك ، إن ذلك كان بالمحنة والتخلية ونحو ذلك ، وفى الخيرات بالأمر والتقوية ونحو ذلك ، ولو كان بالذى قالوا يضاف إليه الإخراج من النور إلى الظلمات كما أضيف إليه الإخراج الظلمات إلى النور عندهم بالأمر والتقوية ، إذ صارت علة الإضافة في الخير إليه الأمر والتقوية ، وذكر الهداية ، وكل ذكر يقابل ما ذكر ؛ إذ الأمر والتقوية هما المحنة وفيها التخلية ، فإذا استقام ذا ، ولم يستقم الآخر بان إن في ذا معنى ليس في الآخر. مع ما زعمت القدرية إن الشرور لا تضاف إليه ؛ لأنه نهي عنها ، فقد نهى عن الضلال والغواية والزيغ ، فلم أضيف إليه ؟ والله الموفق وقالوا فى الإضلال بالتسمية ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لما وجد من غيره ولم يضف إليه ، ولما ليس فى التسمية فضل حكمة يذكر فى موضع الوصف بالغنى والسلطان كقوله تعالى : " مَنْ يَشَا اللهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلُهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيم "، وذلك في موضع القوة والسلطان ، وبالله نستعين والأصل في هذا كله عندنا أن الله إذ هو موصوف بفعله ، ومعنى فعله خلقه أولى ، متفضلا فى فعله أو عادلا ، لا يخلو وصف فعله كل شيء على ما هو
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The Qadariyya said concerning what is attributed to Allah of misguidance, deviation, and turning of hearts — when it is said 'Allah turned their hearts' and the like — that that was by trial and abandonment and the like, and concerning good things by command and strengthening and the like. If it were as they said, then bringing out from light to darkness would be attributed to Him just as bringing out from darkness to light is attributed to Him by command and strengthening, since the cause of attribution in good to Him is command and strengthening, and the mention of guidance and every mention corresponds to what was mentioned. Since command and strengthening are the trial and in it is abandonment, so if this is consistent and the other is not, it is clear that in this there is a meaning not in the other. Besides, the Qadariyya claim that evils are not attributed to Him because He prohibited them. He has prohibited misguidance, error, and deviation, so why is it attributed to Him? And Allah is the Giver of success. And they said concerning misguidance that it is by naming, and that is corrupt: because it is found from other than Him and is not attributed to Him, and because there is no merit of wisdom in naming that is mentioned in the context of describing by wealth and authority, like His saying: 'Whom Allah wills He leads astray, and whom He wills He places on a straight path.' And that is in the context of power and authority. And from Allah we seek help. The principle in all this is that since Allah is described by His action, and the meaning of His action is to create it, whether He is gracious or just in His action, His description of action is not free from everything being as it is.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the Qadariyya's position on the attribution of misguidance to Allah. The author reports that the Qadariyya claim that when Allah is described as leading astray or turning hearts, this is only by way of trial (mihna) and abandonment (takhliya), whereas good deeds are attributed to Allah by way of command (amr) and strengthening (taqwiya). The author points out an inconsistency: if the same logic applied, then taking someone from light to darkness should be attributed to Allah in the same way as taking from darkness to light. But the Qadariyya treat them differently, showing that their explanation fails. Moreover, the author notes that the Qadariyya claim evils are not attributed to Allah because He prohibited them; yet misguidance, error, and deviation are also prohibited, so why are they attributed to Allah? This highlights a contradiction. The author then refutes their alternative explanation that misguidance is attributed only as a name (tasmiya), meaning without real attribution. He argues that this is invalid because such things are found from other than Allah but not attributed to them, and because the Quranic verses about Allah leading astray occur in contexts of power and sovereignty, not merely naming. Finally, the author restates the foundational principle: Allah is described by His actions, which are either graciously or justly creating things, and all actions are consistent with His wisdom.

Key Terms
قدرية
إضلال
إزاغة
محنة
تخلية
تسمية
قضاء
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Qadariyya position of attributing misguidance only by test or name is consistent with divine justice and the Quranic verses.
Correction: Maturidi shows internal inconsistencies and scriptural contradictions, proving that misguidance is truly created by Allah in wisdom.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the Qadariyya's claim that misguidance is attributed to Allah only by way of test and trial?
A) He proves that test and trial apply equally to good and evil.
B) He shows that the same logic would require attributing both good and evil in the same way, but the Qadariyya treat them differently.
C) He argues that test and trial are not real causes of action.
D) He cites consensus that misguidance is directly created by Allah.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that attributing misguidance to Allah as a mere name or test fails logically and scripturally; true divine action encompasses all creation in wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Maturidi's refutation, do you sense in yourself a tendency to limit Allah's sovereignty to protect your own comfort about divine justice? How might this passage shake that tendency and call you to submit to a will that creates both good and evil in wisdom?
?
Think of a time you attributed a good outcome to your own effort and a bad outcome to external circumstances. How does the Qadariyya's logic mirror that impulse, and what would it mean for you to acknowledge that Allah creates both without you needing to 'protect' Him?
?
This passage forces you to confront that any attempt to 'improve' Allah's justice by denying His action in evil is actually a rebellion against His authority. Are you willing to accept a Lord whose ways you cannot fully understand, and to thank Him even for trials? What will you do this week to practice that surrender?

Refutation of the Qadariyya's Proof from 'Take It with Strength'
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's proof from the verse 'خذها بقوة' and similar verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's proof from the verse 'take it with strength' by showing that strength refers to the time of action, not a pre-existing power.

Core Idea
Quranic 'strength' is at the time of action, not before.

The Qadariyya claimed that Quranic commands like 'take it with strength' prove human power precedes the action. Maturidi counters this by analyzing the verse's context and offering a rational interpretation that strength coincides with the act itself. This lesson unpacks his refutation and its implications for the belief that all power comes from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qadariyya's proof
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi presents the Qadariyya's argument from the verse 'take it with strength' and other verses. Notice their interpretation of 'strength' as causes or pre-existing power.
The Qadariyya used this to argue that human power is effective independently.
2
Examine Maturidi's initial refutation
Maturidi says the apparent meaning is 'take it with strength at the time of taking.' Consider why this is a direct rebuttal: if power is only at the moment of action, it cannot precede it.
He uses an analogy: telling someone to take with hands and see with eyes implies simultaneity.
3
Analyze the additional examples
Maturidi addresses the Qadariyya's use of the verse to Moses and the statements of the jinni and the woman. See how he argues that these refer to past or specific instances, not a general pre-existing power.
The strength of Moses was known only at the time of drawing water, not beyond.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi ends by saying there is no strength except with Allah, reaffirming the Ash'ari/Maturidi doctrine that all power is from God. Reflect on how this refutes human autonomy.
This also ties to the broader debate on qadar (predestination).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتجت القدرية فى تقديم القدرة الفعل بأى من كتاب الله تعالى، منها قوله : 'فَخُذْهَا بِقُوَّةٍ'، وقال أهل التأويل : فاعمل بها يجـــد راجتهاد، فكأنهم رأوا القوة هاهنا الأسباب، لكن الظاهر من ذلك قولنا : خذها بقرة، أى وقت الأخذ؛ لأنها إذا لم تكن فى وقت الأخذ يكون الأخذ بلا قوة، ثبت به الذي يذهب، كمن يقول لآخر : خذه بيديك وانظر إليه ببصرك، فهو على الالتقاء، وعلى ذلك قوله لموسى : فَخُذْهَا بِقُوّة وَأْمُرْ قَوْمَكَ يَأْخُذُوا بأَحْسَنِها، واحتجوا أيضا بقول الجنبي : وَإِنِّي عَلَيْهِ لَقَوِيٌّ أَمِين، وقول المرأة : إِنَّ خَيْرَ مَنِ اسْتَأْجَرَتَ القَوِيُّ الأَمِينُ: قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والحرفان مما ليس لهم التعلق به لما كانت قوة موسى التي علمت بها إنما علمت وقت النزح، وهى لا تبقى إلى ذلك الوقت، وكذلك نوة الجنى على ما امتحن نفسه فيما سبق، والله الموفق . والثاني على إرادة وقت الاستعمال بالعادة الجارية بالحدوث في كل وقت لما شاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقد احتجوا بما فى القرآن من ذكر الاستطاعة، وقد بينا ذلك الوجه، ولا لوة إلا بالله .
Then the Qadariyya argued for the precedence of power over action from the Book of God, among them His saying: 'So take it with strength.' The people of interpretation said: Act with it, you will find [؟] exertion. They seemed to think that strength here means causes, but the apparent meaning of that is our saying: Take it with strength, i.e., at the time of taking; because if it is not at the time of taking, the taking would be without strength, establishing what [؟] goes, like one who says to another: Take it with your hands and look at it with your eyes, it is about meeting. And likewise His saying to Moses: 'So take it with strength and command your people to take the best of it.' They also argued with the saying of the jinni: 'Indeed, I am strong and trustworthy over it,' and the saying of the woman: 'Indeed, the best one you hire is the strong and trustworthy.' The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: The two letters [؟] are not something they can cling to, because the strength of Moses which was known was only known at the time of drawing water, and it did not remain until that time, and likewise the strength of the jinni based on what he tested himself in the past. God is the granter of success. The second [؟] intends the time of use according to the customary habit of renewal at every time as He wills. And there is no strength except with God. They also argued with what is in the Quran mentioning capacity, and we have already explained that point. And there is no [؟] except with God.
Source pages p. 918

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya's proof from certain Quranic verses that they use to argue that human power (qudra) precedes the action and is effective independently. Maturidi first presents their argument: they cite the verse 'So take it with strength' (Quran 7:145, 19:12 etc.) and interpret 'strength' as the causes or power that enables the action. They also cite the verse to Moses 'take it with strength' and the statements of the jinni and the woman in the Quran about being strong and trustworthy. Maturidi then gives the refutation attributed to the Shaykh (likely Abu Hanifa or a earlier authority): these instances do not support their position because the strength of Moses was known only at the time of drawing water (a specific past act) and was not a permanent power. Similarly, the jinni's strength was based on past experience. The point is that power is not a pre-existing entity that the human possesses independently; rather, it is created at the time of the action. The phrase 'the second' likely refers to another interpretation that the strength pertains to the time of use. Maturidi concludes by noting that they also argue from the Quranic concept of capacity (istita'a), but he says this has already been explained (i.e., in previous discussion). His final statement 'There is no strength except with God' reaffirms the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that all power is from God.

Key Terms
القدرة
الفعل
القوة
الاستطاعة
الوقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic command 'take it with strength' proves that humans have power that precedes and enables the action independently of God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'strength' refers to the time of the action, not a pre-existing entity; it is simultaneous, not preceding.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does 'strength' (quwwa) in the verse 'take it with strength' refer to?
A) A pre-existing power that enables the action
B) The time of the action itself
C) Physical strength only
D) God's power, not human power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya by showing that Quranic references to strength refer to the moment of action, not an independent human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the last time you set out to accomplish something good (like praying, fasting, or helping someone). Did you feel confident in your own strength and ability to do it? Or did you recognize that the strength to act truly comes from Allah at that very moment? How would your approach change if you began each task by acknowledging 'لا قوة إلا بالله'?
?
The Qur'anic command 'خذها بقوة' is a command to act with strength at the moment of action. Is your faith and trust in Allah something you rely on only before an action, or is it alive and present in you at the moment you do the action? Where in your daily life do you find yourself acting without that living connection, as if your own strength is sufficient?
?
If your ability to obey Allah is not your own possession but a gift given at each moment, how does that affect your sense of humility and gratitude? Are there moments when you feel proud of your obedience? How can the lesson that 'there is no strength except with Allah' cure you of that pride?

Maturidi's Critique of Mu'tazila on Divine Will
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila position on divine will logically leads to jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila position on divine will logically entails compulsion (jabr), and how this refutes their claim to uphold divine justice and human responsibility.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's separation of will from action turns God's act into compulsion, not choice.

Maturidi continues his polemic against the Mu'tazila by exposing a fatal flaw in their doctrine of divine will. According to the Mu'tazila, God's will precedes the action and is not present at the moment of action. Maturidi shows that this view actually makes God's action compelled, not chosen—contradicting their own rejection of jabr. The passage below presents this argument in Maturidi's own words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow. Notice the two main parts: the initial argument and the additional point starting with 'ثم من قولهم'.
The Arabic has some unclear spots due to OCR. Trust the translation for now.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise
Find where Maturidi states the Mu'tazila view: that will is choice, it precedes action, and is not present at the time of action. This is the starting point.
3
Follow Maturidi's first deduction
See how he argues that if will/choice is absent at the moment of action, the action occurs without choice, and the original choice is nullified because compulsion can occur later. Therefore, the action is by compulsion.
Note the terms: ikhtiyār, iḍṭirār, jabr.
4
Analyze the implications
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila assign eternal reward or punishment for these compelled actions, which is exactly the position of the Jabriyya. Consider why he calls them 'false Jabriyya'.
5
Examine the second argument
The second part: if God wills an action at the nearest time, it occurs even against His wish, implying compulsion. Reflect on how this reinforces the main conclusion.
The word 'يتع' is likely 'يقع' (occurs).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر أن قول المعتزلة : إن الإرادة هى اختيار الفعل ، وإنما تكون متقدمة على الفعل، وليست بموجودة، وأنه وجد فى وقت الوجود بلا إرادة منه ولا اختيار ، وحق اختيار الأول عنه زائل ، إذ يجوز ورود الاضطرار في الوقت الثانى ، ومحال وروده في الوقت الذى فيه الاختيار، والاختيار قائم ، ثبت أن فعله في التحصيل ليس باختيار . وأنه اضطرار ، وعلامة الجبر هذا . وبهذا الفعل يوجبون العداوة والولاية والخلود في الجنة والنار ، الواقع وقت وقوعه بلا اختيار ولا قدرة ولا أمر أيضا ولا نهى ، فمن تأمل ذلك وجده عند التحقيق قول الجبرية في التصريح ، لكن هو لا جبرية كاذبة ، وأولئك جبرية صادقة . ثم من قولهم : أراد الفعل لأقرب الأوقات إليه يتع ذلك الفعل وإن كرهه وأراد صرفه ويقع إن من له به العداوة والولاية ، وإن صار بحيث لا يمكنه الصرف قبل وقوعه أو ثم يكون ذلك الوقت ليس بوقت محال نفوت ذلك الفعل ؛ إذ قد يجوز عندهم فوته بالمنع والقهر ، ثبت بما ذكرت وقوعه بالجبر في التحقيق.
Another aspect: that the Mu'tazila say that will (al-irāda) is the choice (ikhtiyār) of the action, and that it precedes the action, and is not present [at the time of action], and that it [the action] occurs at the time of existence without will from Him nor choice, and the right of the first choice from Him is gone, since it is possible that compulsion (al-iḍṭirār) occurs in the second time, and impossible its occurrence at the time in which there is choice, and choice is established. It is confirmed that His act in acquisition (taḥṣīl) is not by choice, and that it is compulsion, and this is the sign of compulsion (jabr). And by this action they impose enmity and friendship and eternity in Paradise and Fire, occurring at the time of its occurrence without choice, without power, and also without command and without prohibition. Whoever reflects on this finds it, upon investigation, the statement of the Jabriyya explicitly, but they are false Jabriyya, and those [true Jabriyya] are truthful Jabriyya. Then from their statement: He willed the action at the nearest of times to it, that action occurs even if He dislikes it and wills its repulsion, and it occurs to whomever has enmity and friendship, and if it becomes such that He cannot repel it before its occurrence, then that time is not a time in which it is impossible to miss that action; since it is possible according to them its missing by prevention and subjugation. It is confirmed by what I mentioned that it occurs by compulsion in reality.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Mu'tazila doctrine on the divine will logically entails compulsion (jabr). Maturidi begins by stating the Mu'tazila claim: that God's will is identical to His choice (ikhtiyār) and that it precedes the action; at the time of the action's occurrence, no will or choice is present. From this, Maturidi argues that the original choice (al-ikhtiyār al-awwal) is effectively nullified because it is possible for compulsion to occur at the second moment (the time of action) while it is impossible for compulsion and choice to coexist. Therefore, the action that comes into existence is not freely chosen but compelled — a sign of jabr. Furthermore, on this basis, the Mu'tazila assign eternal reward or punishment for actions that occur without choice, power, command, or prohibition, which is explicitly the position of the true Jabriyya, though the Mu'tazila are false Jabriyya (they deny it but their logic leads there). Maturidi adds another point: if God wills an action at the nearest time, that action occurs even if He dislikes it and wishes to repel it, and if He cannot prevent it, then it occurs by compulsion. This confirms the conclusion.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
اضطرار (iḍṭirār)
جبر (jabr)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila doctrine of divine will avoids compulsion because God chooses freely.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if will is absent at the moment of action, the action is not chosen but compelled, thus the doctrine implies jabr.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim the Mu'tazila position on divine will leads to?
A) Free will for humans
B) True divine justice
C) Compulsion (jabr) in reality
D) Abolition of punishment

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila definition of divine will, which separates will from the moment of action, logically entails that God's action is compelled, contradicting their own rejection of jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you feel they are truly chosen or sometimes compelled? How does Maturidi's argument challenge your assumption about divine will? What would it mean for your relationship with God if His actions were compelled, as the Mu'tazila inadvertently suggest? Will you today consciously perform one action with the awareness that it is not merely permitted but directly willed by God?
?
If the Mu'tazila are wrong and God's will is not separate from His action, then every moment of your existence is directly willed by Him. How does this realization change how you see your daily choices? Will you live more consciously aware that every breath is not merely permitted but willed by God? Commit to one specific change in your mindset today.
?
This lesson reveals that even well-intentioned theological positions can lead to conclusions that undermine God's perfection. Are there any beliefs you hold that, upon reflection, might actually diminish God's sovereignty or justice? What concrete step will you take today to align your understanding more closely with the truth as presented in this argument?

Who Are the True Determinists? Maturidi on Mu'tazila and Husayniyya
Maturidi argues Mu'tazila are truer Jabriyya than Husayniyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila, despite accusing others of jabr, are themselves more deserving of the label based on their own conception of human power and will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila, not the Husayniyya, are the true determinists in the debate over human power.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a striking reversal: the Mu'tazila, famous for defending human free will, end up being accused of jabr (determinism). Maturidi carefully dissects the positions of the Mu'tazila and the Husayniyya on the nature of human power (qudra) at the moment of action, showing that the Husayniyya actually grant more agency to the servant. The Mu'tazila's accusation, therefore, is unfounded and even hypocritical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get the overall flow. Note that Maturidi uses a comparative and accusatory tone.
2
Understand the positions
Identify the key differences: Husayniyya affirm power over the action but not its opposite; Mu'tazila claim no power at time of action. Notice that both agree on limited power but differ on explanation.
3
Analyze the reversal
Notice how Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila's own view (servant acts whether he wills or not) actually implies jabr, and that the Jabriyya would accuse the Mu'tazila of making God subject to human will.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument fits into Maturidi's broader theology of human agency and divine power, and why he defends the Husayniyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سمت المعتزلة الحسينية مجبرة بما قالت الحسينية للعبد قدرة ما هو فيه من الفعل ، وليست له قدرة ضده وقت الفعل وقبل ذلك الوقت : الاختلاف بين الحسينية وبين المعتزلة إنما هو فى الاسم ما هو فيه خاصة ؛ لأن الحسينية تقول هو على فعند الله لطف لم يعطه ، والمعتزلة يقولون : لم يبق عند الله شيء فيه صلاحه إلا وقد أعطى ، فقد اتفقا على قدر ما أعطاه ، ولا قوة له وقت الفعل عند المعتزلة ، وعند الحسينية له قدرة ما هو فيه وله اختيار ما هو فيه ، فكان الذي معه من القدرة والاختيار أكثر من الذى عند المعتزلة ، فكيف سمتهم المعتزلة مجبرة لولا قلة الحيا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل عند الحسين إنه عند الفعل مضيع أحد القدرتين ولا عذر له في التضييع ، وعند المعتزلة لا قدرة لا بالتضييع ولا غيره ، فأى الوصفين أشبه بالجبر لو كان ثمة إنصاف ؟ ثم الذى يحقق أن المعتزلة هي المجبرة قولهم : للعبد الفعل شاء العبد أو أبى ، ومن زالت عنه المشيئة فى فعل فهو ساه أو جاهل أو عاجز لا يخلو عن ذلك ، ما قد جعلوا للعبد أن يريد فى سلطان الله ما لا يريده ، مع ويشاء في ملكه ما لا يشاؤه ، وهو يشاء خلافه ، ويريد غيره ، وذلك علامة القسر والجبر ، فعابت الجبرية فى جبر العبد بما رأوا لله الملك والجلال ، قالت يجبر رب العالمين سفها بغير علم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the Mu'tazila called the Husayniyya 'compelled' (mujbara) because the Husayniyya said that the servant has power (qudra) over the action he is in, and he does not have power over its opposite at the time of action and before that time. The difference between the Husayniyya and the Mu'tazila is only in the name, concerning what he is in specifically; because the Husayniyya say he is such that there is from God a grace (luṭf) He did not give him, and the Mu'tazila say that nothing remains with God of what is beneficial for him except that He has given it. So they agreed on the extent of what He gave, and he has no power (quwwa) at the time of action according to the Mu'tazila, while according to the Husayniyya he has power over what he is in and he has choice over what he is in. Thus what is with him of power and choice is more than what is with the Mu'tazila. So how did the Mu'tazila call them 'mujbara' except due to lack of shame? There is no power except with God. And the principle according to Husayn is that at the time of action he wastes one of the two powers, and he has no excuse for the waste, while according to the Mu'tazila there is no power, neither by waste nor otherwise. So which of the two descriptions is more similar to jabr if there were fairness? Then what confirms that the Mu'tazila are the true Jabriyya is their statement: 'The servant has the action whether he wills or refuses, and whoever has will removed from him in an action is either forgetful, ignorant, or unable, no exception.' They have made it such that the servant wills in the dominion of God what God does not will, and wishes in His kingdom what He does not wish, while God wills the opposite and wishes otherwise. That is a sign of coercion and jabr. So the Jabriyya criticized [them] for compelling the servant while they consider that God has sovereignty and majesty; they said: 'He compels the Lord of the worlds, foolishly without knowledge.' And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of the Mu'tazila and Husayniyya (followers of Husayn al-Najjar) on the issue of power (qudra) and compulsion (jabr). Maturidi first notes that the Mu'tazila label the Husayniyya as 'mujbira' because the Husayniyya hold that the servant has power only over the action he is engaged in, not its opposite. Maturidi then points out that the only real difference is verbal: both agree that the servant's power is limited; the Husayniyya attribute the lack to a withheld divine grace (luṭf), while the Mu'tazila claim God has given everything beneficial. Importantly, according to the Mu'tazila, the servant has no power at the time of action, while the Husayniyya affirm both power and choice over the action. Thus the Husayniyya actually grant more power, so the Mu'tazila's accusation of jabr is baseless (lack of shame). Furthermore, Maturidi develops his own argument: the Mu'tazila declare that the servant's action occurs whether he wills or not, and if he lacks will he is forgetful, etc., effectively saying the servant can will against God's will in God's dominion. This is precisely a sign of coercion (qasr) and jabr. Maturidi sarcastically concludes that the Jabriyya (the true determinists) would accuse the Mu'tazila of attributing compulsion to God—'He compels the Lord of the worlds foolishly'—because their position forces God to be subject to the servant's will.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
جبر (jabr)
لطف (luṭf)
مشيئة (mashī'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Husayniyya, who believe the servant only has power over the action he is performing, are more deterministic than the Mu'tazila.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila deny the servant any power at the time of action, while the Husayniyya affirm power and choice over the action. Thus the Mu'tazila are actually closer to jabr.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, who has more power at the time of action: the servant according to the Husayniyya or according to the Mu'tazila?
A) The Mu'tazila grant more power
B) The Husayniyya grant more power
C) Both grant the same amount
D) Neither grants any power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi turns the tables on the Mu'tazila by arguing that their denial of power at the time of action makes them more deserving of the jabr label than the Husayniyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accused someone of a failing that you yourself possess? How does Maturidi's exposure of the Mu'tazila's hypocrisy challenge you to examine your own criticisms of others?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed the Husayniyya were determinists while themselves holding a position that is even more deterministic. When you argue about theological matters, do you first check whether your own position could be turned against you? What would it take for you to be as rigorous with your own beliefs as you are with others'?
?
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila's lack of shame (qillat al-ḥayā') led them to call others determinists. Where in your life might you be avoiding the shame of admitting that your own stance is flawed? Are you willing to be wrong for the sake of truth?

The Real Issue in the Debate on Divine Will
Maturidi identifies the real issue in the debate on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the real disagreement between the Husayniyya and Mu'tazila regarding divine will is not about the meaning of will itself, but about whether human actions are created by God.

Core Idea
The debate on divine will is really about divine creation of human actions.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid clarifies a common confusion. Many assume the controversy between early theological schools centered on the attribute of will. Maturidi shows that the Husayniyya and Mu'tazila actually agreed on the definition of divine will—that it means God is not overcome. The real disagreement lies elsewhere: in the creation of human actions. Understanding this shifts the focus of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Maturidi presents a report of two positions: Husayn's definition of will and the Mu'tazila's agreement.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فتبطل المسألة فى الإرادة' which signals a conclusion.
2
Identify the agreement on definition
Both Husayn and the Mu'tazila define divine will as 'He is not overcome nor subdued'. This is a point of consensus.
3
Locate the true point of contention
After stating the agreement, Maturidi points out that the real issue is whether human actions are created by God. Husayn says they are created; the Mu'tazila deny this. So the debate is about 'khala al-af'al' (creation of actions), not 'al-irada' (will).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال حسين : معنى الإرادة فى الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يقهر ، وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى فى جميع أفعال الخلق ، إنه لم يغلب ولم يقهر ، فتبطل المسألة فى الإرادة ، إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير الحسين : إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة ، فأراد خالقها كونها على ما خلقها ، ومذهب المعتزلة إنها ليست بمخلوقة لله ، فتكون المسألة فى خلق الأفعال / لا فى الإرادة .
And Husayn said: The meaning of will (al-irāda) regarding God—exalted is He—is that He is not overcome nor subdued. And the Mu'tazila have granted this meaning in all actions of creation: He is not overcome nor subdued. Thus the issue concerning will becomes invalid; only the interpretation of will remains. Husayn [said]: The actions of servants are created (makhluqa), so their Creator willed their being as He created them. And the school of the Mu'tazila is that they are not created by God. So the issue becomes about the creation of actions, not about the will.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage reframes the debate on divine will between the Husayniyya and the Mu'tazila. Maturidi reports that Husayn defines divine will as meaning that God is not overcome or subdued (lam yaghlub wa lam yuqhar). The Mu'tazila accept this definition with respect to all created actions—they too agree that in the context of actions, God is not overcome. Therefore, Maturidi argues, the real disagreement is not over the meaning of divine will (since both sides agree on its definition) but over the interpretation of that will—specifically, whether human actions are created by God. Husayn holds actions are created by God, so God wills them as they are created; the Mu'tazila deny that actions are created by God. So the substantive issue is the creation of actions (khalq al-af'āl), not the concept of will itself. This clarifies that the doctrinal debate should focus on the creation of human acts rather than on the attribute of will.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
خلق الأفعال (khalq al-af'āl)
مخلوقة (makhluqa)
غلب (ghalaba)
قهر (qahara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute between Husayniyya and Mu'tazila is about the nature of divine will itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows both sides agree on the definition of divine will; the real disagreement is about whether human actions are created by God.

Checkpoint
What does the passage identify as the real point of disagreement between Husayn and the Mu'tazila?
A) The meaning of divine will
B) Whether actions are created by God
C) Whether God is overcome
D) The interpretation of power

Key Takeaway
The essential point of this passage is that the debate over divine will actually centers on whether human actions are created by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—both your obedience and your sin—is created by Allah? How does that belief affect the way you view your own responsibility and your reliance on Him? Will you reflect on this tonight before sleeping?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to protect God's justice by denying He creates evil actions. But if God does not create your actions, are you then independent of His will for even a single moment? Where do you find yourself on this spectrum between divine sovereignty and human autonomy? Will you commit to a specific change in your daily trust in Allah based on His complete control?
?
If the real debate is not about the nature of will but about whether He creates your actions, consider this: Your next intention, your next move—will you attribute it to yourself alone or to the Creator who wills it? What would it look like to live with the constant awareness that your actions are created by Him? Will you promise to make a dua tonight asking Allah to make you conscious of His creation of your actions?
