Maturidi's Counter-Argument: The Mu'tazila as the True Jabriyya
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila are actually the ones who hold a jabr position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite position on human power actually implies a form of compulsion (jabr) that they accuse others of.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own view of human will leads to a harsher jabr than the one they condemn.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi turns the tables on the Mu'tazila. They commonly accused other groups of jabr (compulsion), but Maturidi shows that their own account of human action—where the slave's will can oppose Allah's will within Allah's dominion—is itself a form of jabr. By analyzing their statements, he reveals an internal contradiction in their position. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a rhetorical question to make his point.
2
Identify the two positions compared
The passage begins by contrasting the view of al-Husayn (al-Najjar) with that of the Mu'tazila on the matter of power (qudra) at the time of action. Find the key difference.
Al-Husayn says one power is wasted; the Mu'tazila say there is no power at all.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite statement quoted by Maturidi
Maturidi then cites the Mu'tazila's own words to show that they effectively attribute to the slave a will that can override Allah's will. This, he argues, is the sign of compulsion.
Reflect on why Maturidi considers this a form of jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل عند الحسين إنه عند الفعل مضيع أحد القدرتين ولا عذر له في التضييع، وعند المعتزلة لا قدرة لا بالتضييع ولا غيره، فأى الوصفين أشبه بالجبر لو كان ثمة إنصاف ؟ ثم الذى يحقق أن المعتزلة هي المجبرة قولهم : للعبد الفعل شاء العبد أو أبى، ومن زالت عنه المشيئة فى فعل فهو ساه أو جاهل أو عاجز لا يخلو عن ذلك، ما قد جعلوا للعبد أن يريد فى سلطان الله ما لا يريده، مع ويشاء في ملكه ما لا يشاؤه، وهو يشاء خلافه، ويريد غيره، وذلك علامة القسر والجبر، فعابت الجبرية فى جبر العبد بما رأوا لله الملك والجلال، قالت يجبر رب العالمين سفها بغير علم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The principle according to al-Husayn is that at the time of the act one of the two powers is wasted and he has no excuse for the waste. According to the Mu'tazila there is no power either by waste or otherwise. Which of the two descriptions is more similar to jabr (compulsion), if there were fairness? Then that which confirms that the Mu'tazila are the jabriyya (compulsionists) is their saying: 'The slave performs the act whether he wills or refuses, and whoever has lost the will in an act is either heedless or ignorant or incapable, not beyond that.' They have made the slave will in the dominion of Allah what He does not will, and wish in His kingdom what He does not wish, while he wishes contrary to it and wills other than it. That is the sign of coercion and jabr. Thus they reviled the jabriyya for compelling the slave, because they saw that Allah has sovereignty and majesty. They said: 'The Lord of the worlds compels foolishly without knowledge.' And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument in which Maturidi turns the Mu'tazilite charge of jabr against them. He begins by stating the position of al-Husayn (al-Najjar) that when an act occurs, one of the two powers (the power to do or to refrain) is wasted, and the slave has no excuse for that waste. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, hold that there is no power at all—neither by waste nor otherwise. Maturidi asks which of these two views more resembles jabr if one were to be fair. He then provides the clinching evidence: the Mu'tazila themselves say that the slave performs the act whether he wills it or not, and that if the will is absent the slave is either heedless, ignorant, or incapable—thus they effectively attribute to the slave the ability to will and wish what is contrary to Allah's will within Allah's dominion. This, Maturidi argues, is the mark of coercion and jabr. Ironically, the Mu'tazila criticized the true jabriyya (such as Jahm ibn Safwan) for compelling the slave, but their own position imposes an even harsher compulsion by making the slave's will override Allah's will. Maturidi then quotes them as saying 'The Lord of the worlds compels foolishly without knowledge'—a statement that reflects their critique but, in Maturidi's view, actually applies to themselves. The passage ends with the formula 'la quwwata illa billah' (there is no power except with Allah), asserting divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
الجبر
القدرة
المشيئة
المعتزلة
الجبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi is defending a deterministic jabr position in this passage.
Correction: Maturidi is not defending jabr; he is refuting the Mu'tazila by showing that their own view entails a form of jabr.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what indicates that the Mu'tazila are actually the ones holding a jabr position?
A) Their denial that Allah creates human acts.
B) Their statement that the slave performs the act whether he wills or not, and that he can will in Allah's dominion what Allah does not will.
C) Their belief that humans have complete free will.
D) Their affirmation that Allah's will encompasses all things.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite view of human will and power inadvertently attributes a coercive jabr to the slave, contradicting their own critique of jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that does not go according to your wishes, do you secretly resent Allah's decree or do you surrender with 'la quwwata illa billah'? What would it take for you to truly mean that phrase in your heart?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed the slave can will against Allah's will. Do you ever find yourself acting as if your will is independent of Allah's, desiring something He has not decreed? How can you train yourself to align your will with His revealed commands?
?
Maturidi accuses the Mu'tazila of holding a view that makes the slave a mere puppet. Are there areas in your life where you live as if you have no power or responsibility, blaming 'fate' or 'divine will' for your failures to act? What specific step can you take today to embrace both Allah's sovereignty and your accountability?

Agreement on the Reality of Power: Husayn and the Mu'tazila
Maturidi exposes a verbal dispute between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila while showing their substantive agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agreed on the underlying meaning of the unbeliever's lack of capacity for faith, despite differing in terminology, and that the real issue is about the nature of divine assistance and abandonment.

Core Idea
Verbal disagreement often conceals deeper agreement on the reality of human capacity.

In this passage, Maturidi reveals an important example of how theological disputes can be about words rather than substance. Al-Husayn al-Najjar and the Mu'tazila appeared to disagree on whether an unbeliever has the power to believe, but Maturidi shows they actually share the same fundamental position. Let's examine how he uncovers this agreement and what it teaches us about the real issues at stake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claims made by al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila.
Pay attention to the terms tawfiq, isma, khidhlan, and tark.
2
Identify the points of agreement
Notice how the Mu'tazila agreed with al-Husayn that the unbeliever is not infallible and is left to his opinion.
Look for the phrase 'ليس بمعصوم ولا موفق'.
3
Focus on the terminology difference
Observe that they differ only in whether to call this state 'power of unbelief' or something else.
Maturidi says they agreed on the meaning while differing over its name.
4
Understand Maturidi's conclusion
He argues the real issue is about the connection between divine assistance and power, not just the word 'power'.
The phrase 'حق المسألة بينهم في جعل التوفيق والعصمة قوة الإيمان' is key.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this reframes the debate on qadar and the importance of precise terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر طرفا مما عابت المعتزلة حسينا فى النطق ووافقته في التحصيل. قال حسين : الكافر وقت كفره ليست له قدرة الإيمان، وقدرة الإيمان عنده التوفيق والعصمة، ووافقته المعتزلة على أنه ليس بمعصوم ولا موفق، بل هو مخذول متروك على رأيه، وذلك معنى قدرة الكفر عند الحسين. فاتفقا على المعنى الذي اختلفا في اسمه، فحق المسألة بينهم في جعل التوفيق والعصمة قوة الإيمان، والترك والخذلان قوة الكفر لا إفراد التكلم فى القدرة والاغضاء عن حقيقة ما يجب القول به، وبالله التوفيق.
Then he mentions an aspect of what the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn for in expression, while agreeing with him in meaning. Al-Husayn said: 'The unbeliever at the time of his unbelief does not have the power of faith; the power of faith, according to him, is tawfiq (divine assistance) and isma (infallibility).' The Mu'tazila agreed with him that he is not infallible nor assisted, but rather he is forsaken and left to his opinion. That is the meaning of the power of unbelief according to al-Husayn. So they agreed on the meaning while differing over its name. The true nature of the issue between them is regarding making tawfiq and isma the power of faith, and abandonment and forsaking the power of unbelief—not merely speaking about power in isolation and ignoring the reality of what must be said. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the apparent disagreement between al-Husayn (al-Najjar) and the Mu'tazila. Maturidi notes that although the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn's wording, they actually agreed with his substantive position. Al-Husayn asserted that an unbeliever during his unbelief lacks the power to believe, and that the power of faith consists of divine assistance (tawfiq) and infallibility (isma). The Mu'tazila conceded that the unbeliever is not given such assistance or infallibility, but is left to his own devices. Thus both parties accept that the unbeliever is abandoned (makhdhul) and that his unbelief is due to that abandonment. Maturidi concludes that their dispute is merely verbal: they differ over whether to call this absence of grace 'power of unbelief' or something else. The real issue is whether tawfiq and isma are the power of faith, and abandonment the power of unbelief. Maturidi warns against focusing on the term 'power' in isolation and ignoring the underlying reality. He ends with 'billah al-tawfiq' (with Allah is success), implicitly invoking divine guidance.

Key Terms
التوفيق
العصمة
الخذلان
الترك
قدرة الإيمان
قدرة الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila fundamentally disagree on human capacity, but Maturidi shows they agree on the core meaning.
Correction: They agree that the unbeliever is not infallible and is left to his opinion; their dispute is only about whether to label this state as 'power of unbelief' or not.

Checkpoint

B) The unbeliever is not infallible and is not given divine assistance, and is left to his own opinion.
C) The power of faith is entirely independent of Allah's assistance.
D) The dispute between them is about the reality, not the name.

Key Takeaway
Agreement on the meaning that 'the unbeliever is abandoned' shows that the real dispute between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila is about terminology, not the underlying reality of divine assistance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The unbeliever is 'forsaken and left to his own opinion.' Is there an area of your life where you are relying on your own opinion rather than seeking Allah's tawfiq? What one change can you make today to depend more on divine assistance in that area?
?
Al-Husayn says the power of faith is tawfiq and isma. Do you ever assume your good deeds come from your own power? How would acknowledging that every act of obedience is only possible by Allah's assistance change the way you pray, give charity, or control your tongue?
?
Maturidi warns against focusing on the term 'power' and ignoring the reality. Are there theological concepts you hold as terms but avoid living by their reality—like tawakkul without actual trust, or gratitude without humility? Can you ask Allah for true tawfiq to live the reality of what you claim to believe?

Disagreement on Major Sins and God's Forgiveness
Maturidi notes the disagreement about major sins, with Mu'tazila and Khawarij denying God's forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite and Khawarij position on major sins and how it conflicts with God's mercy and wisdom according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Denying forgiveness for major sins limits God's mercy and wisdom.

Imam Maturidi introduces a key point of contention between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila/Khawarij regarding the fate of those who commit major sins. The passage criticizes those who stubbornly deny God's power to forgive, highlighting their despair of divine mercy. Let's examine the text to understand their claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to capture the overall argument.
Notice the strong language: 'obstinacy' and 'rebellion'.
2
Identify the groups and their claim
The passage names the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. What do they claim about the perpetrator of a major sin? They say he remains in a state and cannot be forgiven without repentance.
3
Analyze the criticism
Maturidi describes them as being obstinate and rebellious, and as despairing of God's mercy. Why is this a theological problem? It limits God's absolute power and mercy.
The phrase 'they despair of the mercy of God' echoes Quranic criticism.
4
Connect to Sunni doctrine
Compare this with the Sunni view that God may forgive any sin if He wills. The passage sets up a refutation of the Mu'tazilite/Khawarij position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف في صاحب الكبيرة لا يزال الذى ذلك وصفه بتعنت المعتزلة ونمرد الخوارج بل يدعى لهم بما آثروا لأنفسهم وأيسوا من رحمة الله ، يروا لله [؟] ولم في حكمته جواز العفو عنهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then they disagreed regarding the perpetrator of a major sin (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) – he remains as described, due to the obstinacy of the Mu'tazila and the rebellion of the Khawarij. Rather, they claim for them what they have chosen for themselves and they despair of the mercy of God. They do not see for God [؟] nor in His wisdom the permissibility of pardoning them, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This passage highlights a key point of contention: the status of a person who commits a major sin (kabīra). The author says that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij stubbornly insist that such a person remains in a state of sin without possibility of forgiveness if he dies unrepentant. They deny that God's wisdom (ḥikma) permits Him to pardon such individuals. This is a distinct position from the Ahl al-Sunna, who affirm that God may forgive major sins if He wills, based on His mercy. The phrase 'they despair of the mercy of God' (a'isū min raḥmat Allāh) echoes the Qur'anic criticism of those who lose hope in God's mercy. The author is setting up the refutation of this position later in the text.

رحمة الله
حكمة

Misconception: Some think that denying forgiveness for unrepentant major sinners is a mark of strict adherence to justice.
Correction: Maturidi portrays it as obstinacy and despair of God's mercy, which contradicts the Quranic emphasis on God's vast mercy and wisdom.

Checkpoint

D) They believe only minor sins are forgiven.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij deny God's forgiveness for major unrepentant sinners, which Maturidi criticizes as limiting God's mercy and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Murji'a on Inference and the Early Ummah's Two Opinions on Major Sins
Maturidi critiques the Murji'a view on inference and presents the early Ummah's two opinions on major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Murji'a view on inference and faith, and the nuanced positions of the early Ummah regarding the committer of major sins.

Core Idea
The Murji'a error is about reducing faith to mere assent; the early Ummah allowed nuanced categories for the sinner.

Imam Maturidi begins by analyzing the Murji'a claim that inference is only needed for outward appearance, not for real assent. He refutes this by showing its inconsistency with language. He then turns to the pre-Mu'tazila Ummah, which held two nuanced opinions about the committer of a major sin: either a believing sinner or a disbelieving sinner, depending on the aspect considered. This lesson explores both arguments and their theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two distinct parts: a refutation and a presentation of views.
2
Identify the Murji'a claim
Understand what the Murji'a said about istidlal (inference) and tasdiq (assent). They claimed inference is only for outward appearance, not necessary for real assent.
Maturidi will refute this claim.
3
Observe Maturidi's refutation
See how Maturidi points out that the Murji'a statement contradicts the dictates of language and is false in narration.
The refutation is brief but decisive.
4
Learn the two opinions of the early Ummah
Before the Mu'tazila, the Ummah had two opinions: the sinner is either a mu'min fasiq (believing sinner) or a kafir fasiq (disbelieving sinner). Understand the analogy with haram makruh and halal makruh.
This shows the early Ummah's nuanced approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرجئة زعمت أن الاستدلال لظهور الصدق والكذب فى الغالب لا أن التصديق فى الحقيقة لا يكون دونه، والإيمان هو ذلك التصديق في الحقيقة، لو كان ليس فى الكبيرة نفيه ولا في إبقائها بحقيقة فى حق الوجود وإن كان في حق الدلالة على المستدل، والله أعلم. فحصل إذا القول منهم على ما توجبه اللغة، وظهر كذبه فى الحكاية. ثم الأمة كانت قبل حدوث الاعتزال وأهله على قولين في صاحب الكبيرة على أنه مؤمن فاسق أو كافر فاسق؛ ليعلم وجه كفره عند من يراه كافرا؛ ووجه فسقه عند من يراه فاسقا، وذلك ما يقال: حرام مكروه وحلال مكروه؛ ليعلم أن الحرمة هى بيان أخف الحرمتين وهي حرمة الكرامة لا حرمة الإطلاق، وإن الحل ليس هو حل الإيثار والرغبة بل فيه بعض ما يورث الشبه، فمثله أمر الأمة فيما ذكرت.
The Murji'a claimed that inference (istidlal) is for the appearance of truthfulness and falsehood in most cases, not that assent (tasdiq) in reality does not occur without it, and faith (iman) is that assent in reality. If it were not that in the major sin there is neither negation of it nor retention of it in reality in terms of existence, even if it is in terms of indication for the one seeking evidence. And Allah knows best. So their statement resulted in what the language necessitates, and its falsehood appeared in the narration. Then the Ummah before the occurrence of the Mu'tazila and its people were upon two opinions concerning the committer of a major sin: that he is a believing sinner (mu'min fasiq) or a disbelieving sinner (kafir fasiq); to know the aspect of his disbelief according to those who consider him a disbeliever, and the aspect of his sin according to those who consider him a sinner. And that is like what is said: forbidden and disliked (haram makruh) and permissible and disliked (halal makruh); to know that the prohibition (hurma) is an explanation of the lighter of the two prohibitions, which is the prohibition of honor (hurmat al-karama) not the absolute prohibition (hurmat al-itlaq), and that the permissibility (hill) is not the permissibility of preference and desire but it contains something that causes ambiguity. Likewise is the matter of the Ummah regarding what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage presents two matters. First, Maturidi discusses the Murji'a claim about inference (istidlal) and assent (tasdiq). The Murji'a held that inference is necessary only for the outward appearance of truthfulness and falsehood in most cases, not that real assent cannot occur without it; they equate faith (iman) with that real assent. Maturidi refutes them by stating that their own statement is contradicted by the dictates of language, and its falsehood is evident. Second, he notes that before the rise of the Mu'tazila, the Ummah had two opinions about the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira): either he is a believing sinner (mu'min fasiq) or a disbelieving sinner (kafir fasiq). This is to clarify the aspect of disbelief for those who consider him a disbeliever and the aspect of sin for those who consider him a sinner. Maturidi uses an analogy: just as there is 'haram makruh' (forbidden and disliked) and 'halal makruh' (permissible and disliked), where the prohibition is a lighter form (hurmat al-karama rather than absolute prohibition) and the permissibility is not of preference but contains ambiguity, so too are the names and rulings for the sinner nuanced. Thus the early Ummah had a sophisticated understanding, not a simplistic one.

Key Terms
مرجئة
استدلال
تصديق
إيمان
كبيرة
مؤمن فاسق
كافر فاسق
حرام مكروه
حلال مكروه
حرمة الكرامة
حرمة الإطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'a view is simply about delaying judgment on sinners.
Correction: Actually, the Murji'a claimed that inference is only for outward appearance, and faith is merely assent without need for inference. Maturidi refutes this by showing its inconsistency with language.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what did the early Ummah (before the Mu'tazila) hold regarding the committer of a major sin?
A) That he is either a believer or a disbeliever without qualification
B) That he is either a believing sinner (mu'min fasiq) or a disbelieving sinner (kafir fasiq)
C) That he is always a disbeliever
D) That his status is suspended

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Murji'a reduction of faith to mere assent and shows that the early Ummah had a nuanced, two-fold classification for the committer of major sins, avoiding simplistic judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Muslim who commits a major sin, do you tend to reduce their faith to mere inner belief, like the Murji'a? How does this lesson challenge you to see the complexity of their state rather than a simple label?
?
The early Ummah held two nuanced opinions, avoiding a one-size-fits-all verdict. Are you quick to judge someone's entire iman based on a single sin? What would it mean for you to embrace the nuance of 'mu'min fasiq' in your relationships with fellow Muslims?
?
Maturidi refutes the Murji'a by showing their position leads to linguistic inconsistency. Are there areas of your own belief where you hold contradictory views? How does this passage urge you to examine the coherence of your faith and repent from any internal contradictions?

The First Argument: Intercession Implies Injustice
Maturidi presents the first of three reasons why seeking intercession is ugly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of Maturidi's three arguments: that seeking intercession for someone whom it is not wise to punish implies asking Allah to be unjust or foolish.

Core Idea
Asking forgiveness for the guiltless implies Allah might be unjust.

Imam Maturidi opens by addressing the general case of supplication. He argues that intercession is context-sensitive. Here, he presents the first reason why seeking intercession for a person who has done good deeds and is promised reward is considered 'ugly' (قبيح). This reason focuses on the implicit suggestion that Allah could be unjust or foolish.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and the English translation to get a sense of Maturidi's argument structure.
Notice the phrase 'يقبح من وجوه' which introduces the list.
2
Identify the first reason
Look for 'أحدها' and note the logic: if punishment is not wise, then asking for forgiveness is like asking Allah not to be unjust.
3
Understand the implication
Why does Maturidi consider this 'takhrij al-tafsīq' (a way of declaring sinfulness)?
4
Reflect on the concept of divine wisdom
Consider why 'الحكمة' (wisdom) is central: Allah's actions are based on wisdom, not caprice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآية في الدعاء فكذلك نقول بدعاء لمن له ذلك الوصف ويشفع له كان في ذلك منه من المآثم والذنوب ، لا أنه إذا كان أفعالهم ذلك فيشفع لأنه لا يجوز في الحكمة تعذيبهم على ما ذكر من الأفعال بل لهم عليها أعظم الثواب وأرفع المأوى ، فطلب الشفاعة والمغفرة لمثله يقبح من وجوه : أحدها أن ذلك إذ لا يجوز في الحكمة تعذيبه فكأنهم طلبوا منه أن لا يجور ولا يسفه . لأفستى الخلق يخرج مخرج التفسيق ، فضلا من أن يتضرع إلى الله ، جل هذا الوصف. والثانى أن الحق في مثله معاقب عن مثان غير إذ هو يلقى ذلك منه بالشكر والحمد ، وفى الدعاء كتمان ذلك وكفرانه ومحال الإذن فى مثله والدعاء ، والله الموفق . والثالث أن ذلك فى الموعود له الجنة والمبشر بها فبطلان مثله يوجب الجهالة في ذلك ، إلا أن يكون الوقت لم يبين ، يكون ذلك في الاستعجال ، وهو قولنا ، في أصحاب الكبائر أنهم لو عذبوا بقدر الذنوب لكان ذلك في الحكمة عدلا فيشفع لسائلهم بالفضل والإحسان دون العدل والاستيفاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
As for the verse concerning supplication, we similarly say: [We] say supplication for one who has that description and who intercedes for him, there would be in that from him [the intercessor] sins and offenses; not that if their deeds are like that then he intercedes because it is not permissible in wisdom to punish them for the mentioned actions; rather they have for them the greatest reward and the highest abode. So seeking intercession and forgiveness for someone like him is ugly in several respects: First, since it is not permissible in wisdom to punish him, it is as if they are asking Him not to be unjust and not to act foolishly—this is a way of declaring His servants to be sinners, let alone that they should supplicate to Allah, exalted is this description. Second, the right in such a case is to requite [with punishment] for something other than [that], since he [the doer] meets that [good deed] with gratitude and praise; and in supplication there is concealment of that and ingratitude for it, and permission for such supplication is impossible. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Third, that [supplication] concerns one who is promised Paradise and given glad tidings of it, so the invalidation of that [promise] would entail ignorance regarding it, unless the time has not been made clear; then that would be in haste. And that is our saying concerning the people of major sins: if they were to be punished according to their sins, that would be justice in wisdom, then [the intercessor] intercedes for them by grace and beneficence, not by justice and full recompense. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 966

Commentary
In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses the verse about supplication (probably referring to the angels' supplication for believers). He argues that supplicating for forgiveness for someone who has done good deeds and is promised reward is inappropriate. He gives three reasons. First, if it is not wise to punish such a person, then asking for forgiveness implies that Allah might be unjust or foolish—a suggestion that is improper. Second, the appropriate response to good deeds is gratitude and praise; asking for forgiveness conceals and denies that gratitude, making such a supplication impossible. Third, the one being supplicated for is already promised Paradise; to ask for forgiveness for him would void that promise unless the time is not yet specified, but then it would be hastiness. He concludes by referencing the position of the Maturidis regarding major sinners: if they are punished according to their sins, that is just; but intercession is by grace and beneficence, not by demanding justice.

Key Terms
الآية في الدعاء
طلب الشفاعة
الحكمة
التفسيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession can be sought for any believer regardless of their state.
Correction: Maturidi argues that intercession is only appropriate when there is a possibility of punishment; for those already deserving reward, it is inappropriate.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first argument, seeking intercession for someone who is not due punishment implies what?
A) That Allah is merciful
B) That Allah might be unjust or foolish
C) That the intercessor is righteous
D) That the person deserves punishment

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that intercession for the undeserving of punishment is problematic because it implies a defect in Allah's justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make duʿāʾ for someone, do you ever pause to consider whether your request aligns with Allah's wisdom and justice, or do you assume that any good request is automatically appropriate? What would it mean for your prayer if you truly internalized that asking for the wrong thing can be a form of declaring Allah's servants sinful?
?
Have you ever asked Allah to forgive someone whom you believe is already righteous, without realizing that such a request might imply that Allah could punish them unjustly? How does this passage challenge you to examine the sincerity and understanding behind your supplications?
?
Think of a time you felt entitled to ask Allah for something because you thought it was 'good' or 'merciful.' How would this lesson correct that assumption? Are you willing to submit your requests to the standard of divine wisdom, even when you don't understand it?

The Second Argument: Supplication Conceals Gratitude
Maturidi presents the second reason: supplication for the good is a concealment of gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument: that supplication for a person who has done good deeds is a form of ingratitude and concealment of the gratitude they deserve.

Core Idea
Supplication for the good denies the gratitude they are due.

After establishing that intercession should not be sought for those undeserving of punishment, Maturidi presents a second, more subtle reason. This argument focuses on the proper response to good deeds: gratitude and praise. To pray for forgiveness for such a person is to verbally deny that goodness.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reason carefully
Examine the Arabic and translation, noting the contrast between 'معاقب' (punishment) and 'الشكر والحمد' (gratitude and praise).
2
Understand the logic
If a person has done good deeds, the appropriate response is gratitude. Supplication for forgiveness implies that those deeds are insufficient or that punishment is still possible, which contradicts gratitude.
Maturidi says 'فى الدعاء كتمان ذلك وكفرانه' (in supplication there is concealment of that and ingratitude).
3
Reflect on the impossibility
Maturidi concludes that such supplication is impossible ('محال الإذن') because it would require Allah to disregard the gratitude due.

The Third Argument and Intercession for Major Sinners
Maturidi concludes with the third argument and the ruling on intercession for major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third argument that intercession for those promised paradise is invalid, and the Maturidi position that intercession for major sinners is by grace, not justice.

Core Idea
Intercession for the promised paradise is invalid unless it's haste; for major sinners it's grace.

Maturidi completes his three arguments by addressing those who are already promised paradise. Seeking intercession for them would nullify that promise, unless the time is not yet specified, in which case it becomes an act of haste. He then clarifies the Maturidi position on intercession for major sinners: if punished according to sins, that is just, but intercession is an act of grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Note the reference to 'الموعود له الجنة' (the one promised paradise) and 'المبشر بها' (given glad tidings).
2
Understand the exception
Maturidi allows for the possibility that the time is not clear, making the request haste rather than ignorance.
The exception does not invalidate the general principle.
3
Connect to the Maturidi position on major sins
Where punishment is deserved, intercession is by grace, not by demanding justice.
This is the conclusion of the whole discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Kisa'i on Forgiveness and Major Sins
Al-Kisa'i interprets two verses on forgiveness and major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Kisa'i reconciles the general promise of forgiveness with the conditional expiation of minor sins through avoiding major sins.

Core Idea
The promise of forgiveness for minor sins does not nullify the threat of punishment for major sins.

Al-Kisa'i, a renowned Qur'anic reader and grammarian, offers a precise interpretation of two key verses that define the scope of divine forgiveness. He shows that the general promise of forgiveness for sins less than shirk is specifically applied to minor sins, while major sins remain under threat. This analysis helps us understand the nuanced relationship between God's mercy and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that al-Kisa'i quotes two verses and draws a conclusion.
Pay attention to the technical terms: wa'd (promise) and wa'id (threat).
2
Identify the two verses
Locate the references to Qur'an 4:48 (general forgiveness for sins less than shirk) and 4:31 (expiation of misdeeds by avoiding major sins). Understand how the second verse specifies the first.
The second verse is introduced as 'then He explained that concerning minor sins'.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Kisa'i concludes that the threat (wa'id) remains for major sins, while the promise (wa'd) stands for minor sins. Consider why this is significant for Islamic theology.
The phrase 'the promise remains, and its due has not ceased' indicates that forgiveness for minor sins is not undermined by the threat for major ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو بكر الكسائي : قوله تعالى : إِنَّ اللهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ ، أن الله وعد المغفرة فيمن شاء ، ثم بين ذلك في الصغائر بقوله : إِنْ تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنْكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُم الوعيد في الكبائر ، بقى الوعد ، فحقه لم يزل بالذى ذكر لاحتماله ما وصفت
Abu Bakr al-Kisa'i said: Concerning His saying, 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48): Allah promised forgiveness for whom He wills, then He explained that concerning minor sins by His saying, 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will remove from you your misdeeds' (4:31). The threat (wa'id) concerning major sins remains; the promise remains, and its due has not ceased by what was mentioned due to the probability of what I described.
Source pages p. 966

Commentary
This passage presents the interpretation of the Qari' and grammarian Abu Bakr al-Kisa'i regarding two key Qur'anic verses on forgiveness. He explains that in verse 4:48, Allah promises forgiveness for whoever He wills for sins less than shirk. Then this is clarified in verse 4:31, where avoidance of major sins results in expiation of minor sins. Al-Kisa'i concludes that the threat of punishment for major sins remains intact; the promise of forgiveness for minor sins is not negated, and it stands as described, meaning that major sins are not automatically forgiven without repentance or special mercy.

Key Terms
المغفرة
الكبائر
الصغائر
الوعيد
الوعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that verse 4:48 promises automatic forgiveness for all sins except shirk, without conditions.
Correction: Al-Kisa'i shows that the promise is conditional: forgiveness for minor sins is tied to avoiding major sins, and major sins themselves remain under threat of punishment.

Checkpoint
According to al-Kisa'i, what does verse 4:31 (avoid major sins) indicate about major sins?
A) Major sins are automatically forgiven
B) Major sins remain under threat (wa'id)
C) Major sins are expiated by good deeds
D) Major sins are the same as shirk

Key Takeaway
Al-Kisa'i shows that divine forgiveness for minor sins is conditional upon avoiding major sins, while major sins themselves remain subject to divine threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In light of Al-Kisa'i's interpretation, what major sins are you still clinging to that you need to abandon in order to hope for Allah's forgiveness of your minor sins? Are you taking this condition seriously in your daily life?
?
When you sin, do you tend to rely on the general promise of forgiveness without considering the condition of avoiding major sins? How does this lesson challenge you to balance fear and hope in your relationship with Allah?
?
The threat of punishment for major sins remains. If you were to die tonight with a major sin unrepented, what would your state be? Does this reality move you to immediate repentance and change?

Mu'tazila on Persistence in Minor Sins
Mu'tazila argue persistence in minor sins nullifies Quranic distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that persistence in minor sins makes them like major sins, and how this argument challenges the Quranic distinction between major and minor sins.

Core Idea
Persistence in sin is defined as refusing repentance, not repeating the act.

The Mu'tazila put forward a controversial argument about minor sins: if a person persists in a minor sin without repenting, it becomes as harmful as a major sin. Their definition of 'persistence' is key—they say it is not continuing to commit the sin, but rather abandoning repentance. This reasoning leads to a conclusion that seems to undermine the clear distinctions made in the Quran between different categories of sin. Let's examine their argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the Mu'tazila argument come through clearly.
Remember that this is the view of a group, not necessarily the author's own.
2
Identify the definition of persistence
Find where the Mu'tazila define 'israr' (persistence). Note that they distinguish between merely continuing an act and refusing to repent.
This is the crucial move in their argument.
3
Trace the implication
Work through the logical steps: if persistence is abandoning repentance, and all sins are forgiven by repentance, then any unrepented sin leads to eternal punishment. This nullifies the Quranic distinction between shirk and lesser sins, and between major and minor sins.
The author presents this as a problematic consequence of their view.
4
Consider the problem
Reflect on why this argument is presented as an objection. Why does it undermine the Quranic verses? The response (not in this passage) would need to address this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالت المعتزلة : صاحب الصغيرة إذا أصر عليها يضير صاحب كبيرة ، والاصرار على ذلك الفعل ليس هو لزومه ؛ لأنه لا فعل يمكن لزومه حتى لا يتحول منه إلى غيره ، فليس إذا الإصرار إلا ترك التوبة والندامة عليه ، وكل الذنوب من الشرك وغيره مغفور بالتوبة عنها والندامة عليها فيبطل على قولهم حق هذه الآية من التفضيل بين الشرك وما دونه ، وحق الآية الأخرى من التفضيل بين الكبائر وما دونها ، ويحصل على أن كل ذنب يوجب الخلود إلا أن يتاب عنه ، وذلك بين لمن تأمله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the Mu'tazila said: The owner of a minor sin, if he persists in it, harms the owner of a major sin. And persistence in that action is not its continuance; because there is no action that can be continued such that he does not turn from it to something else. So persistence is nothing other than abandoning repentance and regret over it. And all sins, from shirk and other than it, are forgiven by repentance from them and regret over them. So according to their statement, the right of this verse concerning the distinction between shirk and what is less than it is nullified, and the right of the other verse concerning the distinction between major sins and what is less than them is nullified. And it results that every sin necessitates eternal punishment unless one repents from it. And that is clear to whoever contemplates it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument regarding persistence in minor sins. They assert that if a person persists in a minor sin, it becomes equivalent to a major sin in harm. They define 'persistence' (israr) not as continuous commission of the act, because no action can be continuously performed without turning to something else. Rather, persistence is refusing to repent and feel regret. Thus, all sins, including shirk, are forgiven only through repentance and regret. This position, according to the author, undermines the distinction made in the Quranic verses: one verse distinguishes shirk from lesser sins (4:48), and another distinguishes major sins from minor ones (4:31). If all sins require repentance for forgiveness, these distinctions become meaningless. The conclusion is that every sin, if unrepented, leads to eternal punishment. The author presents this as a clear result of the Mu'tazila logic. The passage is an objection that likely expects a response, but the response is not included in the current window.

توبة
صغيرة
كبيرة
خلود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The student might think this passage represents the author's own view.
Correction: This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument, which the author later refutes. The author does not endorse this view; it is an objection.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila define 'إصرار' (persistence) in this passage?
A) Continuously committing the same act
B) Refusing to repent and regret
C) Intending to repeat the sin
D) Accumulating many minor sins

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument equates all unrepented sins, eliminating the Quranic hierarchy of sin.

First Argument: The Probabilistic Nature of Threat Verses
Maturidi's first argument on probabilistic interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that threat verses cannot decisively remove hope of forgiveness because they are open to interpretation.

Core Idea
A threat open to two interpretations cannot override a clear verse of hope.

Maturidi begins his response by addressing the assumption that threat verses decisively nullify the hope of forgiveness. He points out that such verses can be interpreted in different ways, and therefore cannot be used to make a definitive ruling. This lesson unpacks the first of his three arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting how Maturidi sets up the problem.
Focus on the phrase 'يحتمل الاستحلال والاستخفاف'.
2
Identify the key claim
What is the main point? That threatening verses are not final because they can be interpreted differently.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that making a decisive judgment on one interpretation while dismissing another is arbitrary (تحكم).
This shows his commitment to rational consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فنقول له بأوجه : أحدها أن الوعيد الذي ذكرته يحتمل الاستحلال والاستخفاف بالأمر والنهى فلا يترك ما أطمع بهذه الآية من المغفرة فيزال الطمع والرجاء بالوعيد المتوجه وجهين أو يوقف فيها ، فأما القطع في أحد الوجهين بالمحتمل ومنع القطع بالآخر للاحتمال فهو تحكم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثانى أن الآية فى التفضيل بين المحتمل للغفران والذى لا يحتمل ، فإذا صرفت إلى الصغائر بطل تخصيص اسم الشرك ، وتلبيس على السامع محله ، وليس أمر الوعيد فيما جاء بموضع التفضيل، بل الذى جاء بحق التفضيل ذكر الغفران بالتكفير ، والتكفير يكون بمقابلة الجزاء من حسنات أو عقوبات كقوله تعالى : إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهُوْنَ عَنْهُ ۚ والله الموفق . عن . والثالث أنه قال : لمن يشاء؛ وهذا كناية عن الأنفس المغفورات لا لاثام التى تغفر ، لم يجز صرف التخصيص إلى الآثام بالآية المكنى بها عن الأنفس ، وفى آيات الوعيد تحقيق في الذى جاءتهم وفيما جاء على ما قيل لا صرف فى ذلك فهو أولى ، والله الموفق . وبعد ، فإنه قال : و لمن شاء ، والصغائر عندكم مغفورة بالحكمة لا بالوعد ، والآية فى التعريف ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The jurist (al-faqih), may God have mercy on him, said: So we say to him with several aspects: First, that the threat which you mentioned is susceptible to [interpretation as] deeming permissible and making light of command and prohibition, so the hope [of forgiveness] aroused by this verse should not be left such that hope and expectation are removed by the threat that faces two directions, or one is paused regarding it. As for decisive judgment in one of the two directions by what is probable and preventing decisiveness in the other due to probability, that is arbitrary. There is no power except through God. Second, that the verse is about differentiation between what is probable of forgiveness and what is not probable. So if it is diverted to minor sins, the specification of the term 'shirk' becomes invalid, and there is confusion upon the listener in its place. The matter of threat in what has come is not a place of differentiation; rather, what has come with the right of differentiation is the mention of forgiveness by expiation (takfir). Expiation is by way of requiring reward, whether good deeds or punishments, as God Most High says: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden...' And God is the grantor of success. ... Third, that He said: 'For whom He wills' — this is a metaphor for the forgiven souls, not for the sins that are forgiven. It is not permissible to divert the specification to sins by the verse which is a metaphor for souls. And in the verses of threat there is confirmation regarding what has come to them and what has come, according to what was said, there is no diversion in that, so it is more appropriate. And God is the grantor of success. Then, He said: 'And for whom He wills' — and minor sins, according to you, are forgiven by wisdom (hikma) not by promise, and the verse is for informing. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (أوجه) against a position that misinterprets a verse about forgiveness (likely 4:48 or 4:116). The first argument addresses the probabilistic nature of threat verses: the threat could refer to deeming permissible or making light of obligations, so it cannot be used to remove hope of forgiveness; making a decisive ruling based on probability is arbitrary. The second argument shows that the verse differentiates between what is likely to be forgiven and what is not; diverting it to minor sins invalidates the specific mention of shirk and confuses the listener. The true differentiation is in forgiveness through expiation (تكفير), which balances rewards or punishments, as in Quran 4:31 about avoiding major sins. The third argument focuses on the phrase 'for whom He wills' as a metaphor (كناية) for the forgiven souls, not the sins themselves; thus, the specification cannot be diverted to sins. The verse is informative (تعريف), not a promise of automatic forgiveness for minor sins, which according to the opponent are forgiven by wisdom (حكمة) rather than by promise.

Key Terms
الوعيد
المحتمل
الاستحلال
الاستخفاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Threat verses always cancel out verses of hope.
Correction: Threat verses are open to interpretation and cannot definitively remove hope if another interpretation is possible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first argument, why should the threat verse not remove the hope of forgiveness?
A) The threat verse is weak in transmission.
B) The threat verse can be interpreted in multiple ways.
C) The opponent is not sincere.
D) The verse of forgiveness is stronger.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first argument shows that threat verses are not decisive when they are open to different interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a threatening verse in the Quran, do you immediately assume it applies to you unconditionally? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that assumption, and what would change if you began to see such verses as conditional?
?
Think of a time when fear of punishment made you despair of God's mercy. Based on this lesson, were you perhaps neglecting the clear verses of hope? Will you now consciously recall God's promise of forgiveness when fear overwhelms you?
?
Maturidi calls arbitrary interpretation 'tahakkum'. In your own understanding of the Quran, are there verses where you have insisted on a harsh interpretation while ignoring other possible meanings? What step will you take today to seek a balanced understanding?

Second Argument: Differentiation and Minor Sins
Maturidi's second argument on differentiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the verse of forgiveness differentiates between probable and non-probable sins, and cannot be restricted to minor sins.

Core Idea
The verse of forgiveness is about differentiation, not universal promise for minor sins.

The second argument focuses on the structure of the verse itself. Maturidi explains that the verse differentiates between what is likely to be forgiven and what is not. If one diverts it to minor sins only, the specific mention of 'shirk' becomes meaningless. This lesson breaks down that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how Maturidi establishes the purpose of the verse.
Look for the word 'التفضيل' which means differentiation.
2
Identify the problem with diverting to minor sins
Maturidi says it invalidates the specification of shirk and causes confusion.
3
Understand the correct differentiation
The true differentiation is through expiation (تكفير) which balances deeds, as in Quran 4:31.
This connects to avoiding major sins.

Third Argument: 'For Whom He Wills' as a Metaphor for Souls
Maturidi's third argument on metaphor and wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the phrase 'for whom He wills' refers to the souls being forgiven, not the sins themselves, and that minor sins are forgiven by wisdom, not by promise.

Core Idea
The phrase 'for whom He wills' is about souls, not sins, so it cannot be limited to minor sins.

The third argument tackles the exact wording of the verse. Maturidi points out that 'for whom He wills' is a metaphor (كناية) referring to the forgiven souls, not the specific sins. Therefore, one cannot use this verse to claim automatic forgiveness for minor sins. This lesson explores that reasoning and the final point about minor sins being forgiven by divine wisdom, not by a direct promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation, especially the part about 'لمن يشاء' being a metaphor.
Note the phrase 'كناية عن الأنفس المغفورات لا لاثام التى تغفر'.
2
Understand the metaphor
The verse speaks of 'for whom He wills' meaning the people, not the sins.
3
Grasp the final point about minor sins
Minor sins are forgiven by wisdom (حكمة), not by a promise - so the verse is informative, not a promise.
This concludes Maturidi's three arguments.

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Persistence in Minor Sins
Maturidi presents the Mu'tazila claim and refutes it via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that persistence in minor sins makes them equivalent to major sins by exposing the logical inconsistency in their definition of persistence.

Core Idea
Persistence is not continuous action but abandoning repentance—a distinction that unravels the Mu'tazila position.

The Mu'tazila argued that persisting in a minor sin makes it as severe as a major sin. They defined persistence not as repeating the act but as failing to repent. Imam Maturidi seizes this definition to show that if repentance forgives all sins, their distinction between sin categories collapses, and their view leads to the absurd conclusion that every sin merits eternal punishment. This lesson unpacks that logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the passage carefully, noting the Mu'tazila's assertion that persistence in a minor sin damages like a major sin. Also note their definition of persistence.
They say persistence is not continuous performance but abandoning repentance.
2
Trace the refutation
Maturidi points out two consequences: (1) the verse differentiating shirk from other sins becomes meaningless, (2) every sin would require repentance to avoid eternal punishment. See how these follow from their own definition.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the outcome
Consider why this refutation is effective. How does Maturidi's approach defend the traditional distinction between major and minor sins?
The logic shows internal inconsistency in Mu'tazila doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالت المعتزلة : صاحب الصغيرة إذا أصر عليها يضير صاحب كبيرة ، والاصرار على ذلك الفعل ليس هو لزومه ؛ لأنه لا فعل يمكن لزومه حتى لا يتحول منه إلى غيره ، فليس إذا الإصرار إلا ترك التوبة والندامة عليه ، وكل الذنوب من الشرك وغيره مغفور بالتوبة عنها والندامة عليها فيبطل على قولهم حق هذه الآية من التفضيل بين الشرك وما دونه ، وحق الآية الأخرى من التفضيل بين الكبائر وما دونها ، ويحصل على أن كل ذنب يوجب الخلود إلا أن يتاب عنه ، وذلك بين لمن تأمله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the Mu'tazila said: The one who commits a minor sin, if he persists in it, it damages like a major sin. Persistence in that action is not its continuous performance, because there is no action that can be continuously performed such that one does not turn from it to something else. So persistence is nothing but abandoning repentance and regret over it. And all sins, from shirk and otherwise, are forgiven by repentance and regret over them. Thus, according to their statement, the right of this verse regarding the differentiation between shirk and what is below it becomes invalid, and the right of the other verse regarding the differentiation between major sins and what is below them. And it results that every sin necessitates eternity [in Hell] unless one repents from it. That is clear for anyone who reflects. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage first presents the Mu'tazila objection: they claim that persistence (إصرار) on a minor sin makes it as harmful as a major sin. They define persistence as not the continuous performance of the action (since one can always stop), but rather the abandonment of repentance and regret. Then the author (Maturidi) refutes this by drawing the logical consequence: if all sins, including shirk and minor sins, are forgiven by repentance and regret, then the Mu'tazila's own interpretation of the verses that differentiate between shirk and other sins, and between major and minor sins, would be nullified. Furthermore, their position would lead to the conclusion that every sin, unless repented, results in eternal punishment in Hell—a view they do not hold. This reductio ad absurdum shows the inconsistency in their argument. The refutation relies on the principle that persistence is simply the lack of repentance, and since repentance forgives all sins, the distinction between sin categories collapses on their account.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence means repeatedly committing the same sin without stopping.
Correction: Persistence is actually abandoning repentance and regret over the sin, not continuous performance.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the actual meaning of persistence (إصرار) on a sin?
A) Continuously performing the same sin without stopping.
B) Abandoning repentance and regret over the sin.
C) Intending to commit the sin again.
D) Considering the sin permissible.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their definition of persistence as lack of repentance leads to the collapse of their own distinction between major and minor sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a minor sin you have committed repeatedly without sincere regret. If persistence is merely the absence of repentance, does your lack of regret mean you are persisting? What would genuine repentance look like for that sin today, and what might it cost you to perform it?
?
The Mu'tazila believed minor sins could become equivalent to major ones through persistence, but the text shows this reasoning collapses. In your own life, do you treat minor sins as insignificant, assuming they don't require urgent repentance? How would your daily habits change if you saw every unrepented sin as a barrier between you and Allah?
?
If every sin, even shirk, is forgiven by repentance, why do you find it easier to seek forgiveness for big sins while ignoring the 'small' ones? What does that selectivity reveal about your understanding of Allah's mercy and your own pride? Commit to repent for one specific minor sin today.
